THE RED PAGE.
For Her.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
There is no little heart so sweet
As this sweet-heart of mine;
Nor any bosom so replete
With innocence as thine ;
No other arms in whose caress
Luries such a subtle tenderness :
No other hand so kind as this,
Nor any lips so warm to kiss.
There are no eyes so deep and blue
As these I hold with mine ;
Nor any love so firm and true,
Sweet-heart, as this of thine.
There is no voice so blithely free,
Nor tuned with such divinity :
Nor any step so glad and light,
And oh, no soul so pure and white!
There is no prayer whose pleading tone
Moves Heaven so swift as thine :
I would thy faith were all mine own,
That God might answer mine.
And well thou knowst what tender prayer
My soul would ask that God to hear—
“ May He so bless this life of mine,
That I may make a heaven of thine !”
RENA WALLACE.
Woman—Man’s Parasite?
The prostitute slides past,
AH smiles and whispered sin,
Her body rotting fast,
Her soul aghast within.
That is a verse to arrest the hasty judgment
which would class as commonplace “ Human
Verses/’ by Eppie Frazer (Lond. Circle Co-opera-
tive Printers’ Society). One’B first impression of
this booklet is that it has little value : there is not
much art to it, and in spite of occasional good
lines the thought does not seem of extraordinary
force. But more considerate perusal collects here
and there suggestions which somehow cling to the
memory. There are most in a rudish dialogue
called “Prevention”—the persons a man and a
woman. Their topic is human misery, how to
end it. He says :by living a sane, simple life—
Bent on that least environment
Whieh grants full self-development—
on the theory that if content with mere neces-
saries you will need to “work” less, and conse-
quently there will be more “ work ” for those who
want it, the at-present workless. Tho doctrine is
silly enough, since it is the manufacture of un-
necessaries which keeps half the world of work-
men employed—“ the extravagance of the rich is
the Providence of the poor/' as Ingersoll puts it—
and to increase the supply of “work ” by lessen-
ing the demand for its products is only possible in
Utopia. But the verse glides to a more fruitful
theme the worth of woman « hen you’re climbing
the Alps of the soul. Says She—
I would 1 sii.irtd your dream !
H®- No dream !
But. real, Hot life !
Shk. So very real
That lo! you preach your flue,ideal
To whom ? To Woman, of all beings,
A dottrel dulled by prim uot-seeings,
An icicle through chill abstentious,
A puppet pulled by crass conventions,
A shuddering ielly, void of hone.
And impotent to stand alone.
Isn’t some of this truly and vigorously phrased
And She goes on
Yet such a weakling, so unfit
To earn her daily salt, you pit
Against the great rough world, and pray
It please her not to raid away
The great world’s bread ! Oh ! very real!
He. To Woman preach l this ideal
With calm intent. Who, nowadays,
Seduces Man from simple ways?
Who craves for comfort, show and pleasure,
Yea, will have all in ampler measure
Than have her neighbors ? Who will gloom
And wail, and taunt, and e’en assume
Decaying healtli should Man but try
To pass in action brotherly
Beyond the circle bounded by
Her petticoat ? An l when Man’s soul
Awakes, and, yearning to the whole,
Would soar and spun the brutish flesh,
Who weaves a subtle sensual mesh
To hold it down ? Who? Who but Woman
Could perpetrate these wrongs inhuman ?
"Vet, great as is her power for ill,
Her power for good is greater still.
Would she hut grasp her mighty mission,
Fight duwii the beast in stern contrition,
Trust her high thoughts, her aspirations,
Held (lien: aloft before all nations,
Then Man would da,re to put in action
His faint ideals; then fraud and faction
Would shrink away ; then competition
W ould cease with adequate provision
For simple wholesome needs; and then
Goodwill to all would mould all men
Till self-love would be counted crime,
And helpfulness the true sublime,
And lie rank first who nestles Highest.
And brings men closest to the Highest.
Why preach to Woman, quotha? Know,
Where Woman points, there Man must go.
f f h ’ but 111 homely verse it lays a
formidable indictment. And is it not largely
true so far as the individual is concerned? Is
not the average woman a sexual parasite, draining
hei fl partner s energies to feed her life of gaudv
trifles ? Just run over the married couples of your
acquaintance and answer, Does Woman drag
down, or build up?
* * * •
Ihe balance of literary opinion is against her.
1° be sure, literature is mostly made by males :
the JNevv Woman tells another story—a story of
tier high ideals, Tier noble aspirations, dwarfed and
trampled in the mire of marriage by a brutal Man.
JBut the balance of medical opinion is also against
Men some to business, some to pleasure take:
But every woman is at heart a rake.
Pope s dogma has a sound physiological basis.
Woman is essentially— naturally—necessarily—a
vessel of passion; she “suffers incessantly the
eternal woundof love,” as a Frenchman poetically
puts it.. Her sex is of much more moment to her
than his to a man (except negatively) : a man
may be three parts brain; a woman, to be
womanly, must always be three parts womb —
womb-man,” as the dictionaries show her.
• * • *
There is .no question of height or depth in this
status :it is natural. Nature looks to the race,
and is concerned merely to keep it alive. “ The
brutish flesh ” of Eppie Frazer and others is not a
thing to “spurn,’’ for spirit can no more than
name exist without a material basis. Genius has
lofty imaginations, ineffable inspirations, but
genius had a father and mother, was begotten of
alust of the flesh. Matter is to spirit as cause to
effect; and to blame cause that it is not effect as
well, to blame Woman that she is not fruit as
well as tree,. music as well as instrument, is,
racially speaking, foolish and ludicrous. Facially
speaking, Woman must build up, she cannot drag
down.
* * * *
But an individual woman’s position in regard
to tk.indifidual man is quite another matter.
There it is that Eppie Frazer’s exposition of the
characteristic mental littleness of woman applies.
The same idea is more poetically put in W. B.
Yeats’s “Cap and Bells,” recently quoted. It is
more savagely put—most savagely, perchance, in
literature—in Eunciman’s years-ago-quoted
TO “MY DEAR.”
Your face is mean ; your vulgar mouth,
Primmed with a long-lipped feline sneer,
Seems aching with perpetual drouth—
A drouth of whine, and snarl, and jeer.
The muddy reaches of your soul
Are strewn with rotten wrecks of lies;
The slimy currents foully roll;
Their surface glitters through your eyes.
You sanctimonious cockatrice 1
Were all the pulpit’s babble true,
I’d steep my soul in cankered vice
For fear of sharing Heaven with you.
You stab my heart with dastard speech;
You slay the new-born waifs o Igood ;
All hell alone had power to teach
Your callous, flaunting hardihood.
A poet once lived in my heart,
And made me glad for many a year!
You did not kill him with a dart-,
You smothered him in filth, My Dear,
Ah I dear, dead poet, rise again,
And make the joyous choirs resound,
Your song should cheer the sons of men . . .
A! as 1 he festers underground.
* » * •
There is a converse argument: you get it in
“ Locksley Hall ”
As the husband is, the wife is: thou art mated with a
clown,
And the grossness of his nature will have weight to drag
thee down.
You get it in “Maud Muller”—
She wedded a man unlearned and poor . . .
. . . who sat by the chimney lug,
Dozing and grumbling o’er pipe and mug.
You get it put eloquently by Dr. Holmes—
You talk of the fire of genius. Many a blessed woman,
who dies unsung and unremembered, has given out more
of the real vital heat that keeps the life in human souls,
without a spark flitting through her humble chimney
to tell the world about it, than would set a dozen theories
smoking, or a hundred odes simmering, in the brains of
so many men of genius. It is in latent caloric, if I may
borrow a philosophical expression, that many of the
noblest hearts give out the life that warms them.
Cornelia’s lips grow white, and her pulse hardly warms
her thin fingers,—but she has melted all the ice out of
the hearts of those young Gracchi, and her lost heat is in
the blood of her youthful heroes.
Hm ! Cornelia was an all-right mother doubt-
less ; but what sort of a wife did she make ?
How’d you like to live with Cornelia, bed and
breakfast and dinner, dinner and tea and bed ?
What killed Craechus pkre? The writer believes
that as a rule a woman of his own is worth to a
coarse-fibred man more than she costs, and costs
a fine-fibred man more than she is worth. And a
man of her own is worth to a coarse-fibred woman,
etc. — vice versa ? It is not so certain : at last
Australian census there were 50,000 widows to
only 30,000 widowers.—And the argument may
rest at that.
Reprints.
Some Villanelles.
[The germ of the modern villanelle seems to come from
the Provengal troubadours, the time of whose greatest
activity was about the twelfth and thirteenth centuries.
The form now chiefly current was fixed by Jean Passerat
(1534-1602). In this the villanelle is a poem of 19 lines,
rhymed on two sounds, and divided in stanzas as shown
below. (Joseph Boulmier’s “ Rose ” is a translation of
Austin Dobson’s immediately preceding.) Generally, it is
to be feared, the Enylisb villanelle is little more than a
quaint literary exercise: occasionally the difficulty of it
is overcome in lines of melodious grace.]
VILLANELLE.
A dainty thing’s the Villanelle.
Sly, musical, a jewel in rhyme,
It serves its purpose passing well.
A double-clappersd silver bell
That must be made to clink in chime,
A dainty thing’s the Villanelle;
And if you wish to flute a spell,
Or ask a meeting ’neath the lime,
It serves its purpose passing well.
You must not ask of it the swell
Of organs grandiose and sublime—
A dainty thing’s the Villanelle;
And, filled with sweetness, as a shell
Is filled, with sound, and launched in time,
It serves its purpose passing well.
Still fair to see and good to smell
As in the quaintness of its prime,
A dainty thing’s the Villanelle,
It serves its purpose passing well.
W. E. HENLEY.
WHEN I SAW YOU LAST, ROSE.
When I saw you last, Rose,
You were only so high ;
How fast the time goes !
Like a bud ere it blows,
You just peeped at the sky
When I saw you last, Rose !
Now your petals unclose,
Now your May-time is nigh
How fast the time goes !
And a life, —how it grows
You were scarcely so shy
When I saw you last, Rose I
In your bosom it shows
There’s a guest on the sly;
How fast the time goes!
Is it Cupid ? Who knows 1
Yet you used not to sigh,
When I saw you last, Rose; —
How fast the time goes !
AUSTIN DOBSON.
ROSE.
Vous 4tiez encore petite,
Rose, la derniere fois . . .
Dieu ! que le temps passe vite
Fleur innoeente qu’abrite
Tendrement l’ombre des boil
Vous etiez encore petite.
Et deja. la marguerite
Va s’ effeuillant sous vos dm'gts . . ,
Dieu 1 que le temps passe vite !
Oh, comme se preci'pite
La vie. A peine j’y crois . .
Vous etiez encore petite.
Dans votre sein qui palpite
Se glisse un h6te sournois . . .
Dieu ! que le temps pass- vite.
Chez vous Cupidon s’ invite :
Adieu, la paix d’ autrefois !
Vous etiez encore petite :
Dieu ! que le temps passe vite !
JOSEPH BOULMIER.
ROSES.
There are roses white, there are roses red,
Shyly rosy, tenderly white ;
Which shall I choose to wreathe my head?
Which shall I cull from the garden bed
To greet my love on this very night?
There are roses-white, there are roses red.
The red should say what I would have said;
Ah 1 how they blush in the evening light 1
Which shall I choose to wreathe my head?
The white are pale as the snow new spread,
Pure as young eyes and half as bright:
There are roses white, there are roses red.
Roses white, from the heaven dew-fed,
Roses red for a passion’s plight;
Which shall I choose to wreathe my head ?
Summer twilight is almost fled,
, Say, dear love ! have I chosen right ?
There are roses white, there are roses red,
All twined together to wreathe my head.
L. S. BEVINGTON.
Some Comments.
B.E. : “The faculty of criticism is generally
understood to be the power of holding aloof from
a predisposition ordinarily common to the mind
of allowing itself to be swayed—“ carried away”
—by any influence emanating from the subject
under criticism which may tend to force it it any
one direction.”
Whew ! Take breath here ; read it over aloud; and realise
to what uses a base editor must come. Why will people
lumber like that? Why not, plump and plain, “ Absence
of bias is of the essence of capable criticism”? It is
not the same thought; but it is simpler, stronger,
and corrector. For “B.E.’s” sentence is nonsense
as it stands. Every influence from every subject under
criticism must tend to “ force the mind in one direction.”
The faculty of critioism cannot possibly depend upon the
critic’s incapacity 'to be swayed by his subject, since
criticism is nothing but an expression of how the subject
sways the critic. “8.E.” seems to think that a critic
should be an insensitive block. He shouldn’t The more
numerous and more delicate the stimuli to which his
mind reacts, and the more certainly and vigorously it
reacts, the better the critic. The nature of his criticism
depends upon the quality of his brain and the extent of
his knowledge.
“If this be at all a correct definition of one
phase of the critical faculty then the trail of the
serpent is ov or the Red Page of Oct. 3rd. It has
been said that in the critic who is totally unin-
fluenced by any personal predilection will be
found the perfect human.”
Now it is evident what “8.E.” meant to express. He
meant to exclude from the critical faculty subjective
bias, not objective; “any influence emanating from”
the critic’s mind, not from “ the subject under
criticism.” Yet even here the thought is imperfect, since
it is only the critic’s mind which gives his critioism
value. What should have been said is “any unjust pre-
dilection ’’—any predilection that cannot be sustained to
the satisfaction of persons of approved literary taste—
admitting that a comparative standard of taste is at-
tainable.
“In the ‘Nousometer’ therein given, Heine,
Poe, Shelley, are classified under the heading of
‘lntense Genius.’ Now Poe, Heine, Shelley,
were all critics, the first a very close-reasoning,
analytical critic too. Who stands in the van of
genius in The Bulletin’s mind-measure—the
novelist, the playwright, the poet? Net quite—
the critic ! ”
This is dogma which can only be herefought by another
dogma—with appeal to C«esar Reader. Roughly speak-
ing, it may be said that the most distinctive quality of
the poet is power of imagination ; of the critic, power of
analysis. Imagination is constructive, creative, largely
synthetic. A critic needs power of synthesis too, but his
power of analysis is employed precedently in any given
case : he starts from a passive synthesis, the resultant of
his previous impressions, analyses actively, and ma-es a
fresh synthesis. What predominated in Poe, power of
imagination or power of analysis, poet or critic ? On the
whole, poet. And in Heine? Poet again. And in Shel-
ley ? Poet decidedly and unmistakeably. Therefore, the
conclusion is absurd :
“ The critical mind of the writer leaps, perhaps
unconsciously but none the less actively, to meet
the dead and gone mentality which appealed to it
the most forcibly. Similia similibus amantur.”
C.: “ The theory that genius is allied to madness
and disease is a modern and a foolish one.”
“ Take some vigorous art and compare the
artists Chaucer, Ben Jonson, Goethe, Hugo; in
painting, Millais and hosts of others; in music,
Bach, Handel, Haydn. Bah ! They were as
wholesome as their art, which (art) is the outcome
of their surplus thought and emotions.”
‘Ban! is good, and the conjunction of Chaucer and
Millais, Handel and Hugo, is belter. But so far it is one
word against another ; there is nothing for comment but
“surplus ” —which should be struck out.
“Buffon says the style is the man,”
It isn’t, in any fair meaning of the words in se, though
the epigram passes with the aid of the meaning you may
read into them. Accurately, the style is an effect a re-
sult of the man.
“ and it would take a lot of proving to show that
strong, beautiful work resulted from a diseased
activity.”
It takes a lot of explanation to get an idea into some
heads. To repeat, then : genius results from abnormal
activity in the hrain. The physiological result of con-
tinuously abnormal activity in any part of the human
body is-sooner or later—abnormal passivity in some
cdier part. Health is the result of normal activity in
all parts. Therefore, genius implies disease.
“Again, your note on artificial stimulants.—
what is more recuperative for brainwork than a
gallop on a good goer or a pull on the river ? ”
If these are undertaken with the object of “clearing the
head ”—f.e., of expelling waste matter from the brain-cells
and revivifying them bv an increased flow of blood—these
also are artificial stimulants. And they act, primarily, in
precisely the same way as (say) alcohol,' by quickening the
heart’s action.

BEFORE YOU
T , E A. 3?!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-,street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks, ” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

The Man with Rubber Pedals.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It has all the latest fixings—barrel hubs and
narrow tread ;
It weighs twenty pounds or under, is as rigid as the
dead;
It’s the very newest pattern, and the very latest
grade,
And it cost you all the cash that in the last three
months you made.
You lead it from the agent’s, and your bosom
swells with pride
As you lift it from the kerbstone and you start its
maiden ride . . .
Like the lightning past the tram-cars, cabs, and
everything you’ve sped,
When you see a man with rubber pedals plugging
on ahead.
He is forty years of age, and on an antiquated
crock,
Sitting upright as a soldier and as bandy as a jock;
He is wobbly, he is shifty, and he scarce knows
how to ride;
His gear is less than fifty, and his handle-bars are
wide.
From crank to crank his tread is eighteen inches,
and his frame
Is a pattern that was popular when first the safety
came;
And as you gain upon him you are thinking, “ I
must show
How a good man on a jigger that is up to date can
go.”
You fold your arms and pass him in an attitude of
grace,
When the beatific smile upon his open whiskered
face
Makes your conscience somehow smite you as
across his track you whiz
Lest you show him p’raps too cruelly what an utter
mug he is;
And when you think that he’s about a hundred
yards behind
That man with rubber pedals goes completely from
your mind,
Till a darkness at your elbow and a rattling on your
ear
Shows the man with rubber pedals still is battling
in the rear.
Then you think with some resentment, ’‘ This is
not as it should be ;
This man with rubber pedals Caking all his pace
from me;
Such presumption is opposed to all the canons of
the game,
And if I show him up he’s only got himself to
blame.”
So you drop your arms arid lightly touch the
neatly-nickeled head
With some ankling calculated just to kill that
fellow dead,
But after half-a-mile you are astounded still to feel
That man with rubber pedals hanging calmly on
your wheel.
You argue out the question and you’re bustled to
confess
That the man is what is technically known as
N.T.S. . '
Still for such as he to push you is a thing you can’t
allow—
He has asked for pace, and, Holy Moses, won’t he
get it now?
You drop your head twelve inches, grip your
handles tight and lift,
And as your calves and biceps swell, by Jingo,
don’t you shift!
Then you reckon that you’ve left him and it’s
nearly time to slack
When you hear the cursed rattle of his mud-guards
at your back.
He can hold h'is own at sprinting—that is proved
beyond a doubt,
So the'only way to beat him is to simply wear him
out.
You set a nice two-forty bat, and to yourself you
hiss:
“ That man with rubber pedals can’t stand many
miles of this. ”
Then the townships travel past you and the mile-
stones rise ahead
Till your thighs are working stiffly and you’re
feeling pretty dead;
Still you force your ped’ling even and your han-
dle-tips you clinch,
But that man with rubber pedals hasn’t shifted—
not an inch.
At last, in view of “business” and the “fast-
approaching night,”
You decide ’tis best for you to take the turning to
the right;
And as you swing around he passes upright as the
just,
With that beatific smile of his still glowing
through the dust.
Are you riding to Sans Souci ? He’ll be there to
“do you bad.”
He is*on St. Kilda Road ; and on each Western
camel pad.
Be you cycling in the country, be you cycling in
the town,
That man with rubber pedals will be there to take
you down.
Melb.

Important Announcement by Dixson & Sons,
Tobacco Manufacturers,
The BONANZA CIGAR.
DIXSON & SONS, by the aid of newly-invented machinery of surprising capa-
city, have placed upon this market at a Price Hitherto Un-
known Here a Cigar of exceptional quality.
The Finest Cigar Tobacco in the World
comes from Havana, and arrangements have been made for supplies from
there of the choicest Tobacco (Yuelta Abajo).
BONANZA Cigars are made of this Tobacco, with a necessary portion of
equally-choice Seed Leaf, and together they form a Cigar second only to the
recognised brands of high-class Havanas.
4 Full-sized Cigars for 6d.!l— wm at first no doubt
be prejudicial; but machinery, overcoming the great cost of production, just
permits a Cigar of such quality and weight to be made at a profit. DIXSON
and SONS intend thq Bonanza Cigar to illustrate and prove what can be done.
The “ BONANZA ”
comes as a pleasant surprise to many who, though capable of appreciating a
good Cigar, have lately been prevented through retrenched circumstances from
indulging in the hitherto expensive luxury.
Note; Sample Boxes of 100 Bonanzas will be sent (carriage paid) to any
address on receipt of 10s. while this advertisement appears.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
zrticle, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
my other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short 'Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings, All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But ive
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.c., any picture which has to be re-drawn — will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 17, 1896.
"Splendid Isolation.”
The Emperor of Germany had just affronted
Great Britain before the eyes of the world.
He had threatened armed intervention in
the Transvaal quarrel. Startled, the British
Government had taken the sense of the
Powers. Who would stand by it in case of
a fight 1 Mr. Chamberlain conveyed to the
public the result of those feelers, in the two
words above. One word would have sufficed
to describe the fact. Isolation. But the
single word sounds desolate, chill, and
lamentable. By an inspiration worthy of
Beaconsfield, the British Minister neu-
tralised the appalling name with a swagger-
ing adjective. “Splendidlsolation.” Gerard
Krefft, curator of Sydney Museum, once
wrote that New South Wales is “rich in
snakes.” The gorgeous friendlessness of
Great Britain which Mr. Chamberlain an-
nounced with the accent of exultation un-
doubtedly exists. It is, however, no new
condition. Nearly fifty years ago Ledru
Rollin wrote : ‘ ‘ England is sisterless among
nations. She counts her vassals by millions,
but she has no friends. England is isolated,
like the lone vulture in its eyrie.” The
“ splendid ” aspect did not strike the French-
man. There is nothing splendid about a
vulture. There can be no doubt that Mr.
Chamberlain correctly diagnosed the politi-
cal condition of Great Britain. The diag-
nosis was all the easier for him, because, in
the first place, the complaint is of long-stand-
ing and is chronic; and, in the second, because
it had just entered upon an acute phase.
Since Mr. Chamberlain spoke there have
been serious eruptions, so characteristic in
their nature that the character of the malady
could not escape the most superficial observer.
The utter and most humiliating discomfiture
of Lord Salisbury’s bullying diplomacy with
respect to Turkey has advertised the Isola-
tion of Great Britain with unparalleled em-
phasis. If isolation be regarded not as a
splendor but as a disease, there is no room
for doubt that it has become aggravated of
late years. At the early part of this century,
after the fall of Napoleon, although England
had no friends, she had but one enemy.
Austria was indifferent, Turkey careless,
Russia occupied with other matters, Spain
too exhausted to think, Prussia suffering
a recovery. Italy did not exist as a nation.
There was no Germany, in the present sense.
* * » *
But to-day the case is altered seriously for
the worse. If isolation be related to splendor,
the brightness is dazzling. France is undis-
guisedly hostile. Germany is distinctly un-
friendly. Russia is obstinately antagonistic.
Austria pretends no regard. Holland is
angry. Spain doesn’t matter. Turkey has
just slapped England in the face. Only
Italy, shivering in its military gaiters lest in
its weak condition it get smashed amidst a
contest between Powers, affects a sympathy
with Great Britain. There have been numer-
ous indications that not inclination but diffi-
culty of arranging things among themselves,
has, within the past few years, averted or
postponed a European coalition, having for
its object the smashing of the British Empire.
That British statesmen of all parties, who are
or have lately been within the arena of
Cabinet office, and acquainted with the
deeper secrets of the inner circle of diplomacy,
are aware of the perilous centripetal tendency
of Continental hostility to Great Britain,
many recent circumstances contribute to
show. Nothing less can account for the
rapid substitution of cringe for bounce in
Lord Salisbury’s foreign policy. His pos-
tures with relation to Turkey have been
those of a man who shook his fist in the face
of another and suddenly discovered that a
sabre-armed bystander was preparing to
chop at his wrist. Again, when the young
Czar visits Scotland, the late leader of
the Opposition, Lord Rosebery, is brought
into the political game, in order to
impress the Russian ruler with the un-
animity of all parties. Before the Czar
himself the statesmen of England are
like anxious dependents, nervously con-
triving to prepossess or influence him favor-
ably to their exigencies. And he has treated
them as solicitants. They have been dryly
told that if Great Britain will ‘ ‘ by some
act ” testify her lack of suspicion as to his
motives, he will deign to grant her latest fad
—reforms in Turkey. This is partly the
style of the “confidence-man.” As a mere
preliminary to the realisation of Mr. Bull’s
noble and disinterested aspirations, it is
necessary that he show the elevation of his
mind by consenting that Mr. Bear should put
Constantinople in his pocket and walk just
round the corner with it.
* * *
The transition from apathetic dislike to
aggressive animosity to Great Britain on the
part of the European Powers must be due to
some potent cause, cotemporaneous with the
growth of the sentiment. Such a cause pro-
trudes so prominently that it is strange how
the inevitableness of its effect has not struck
people long ago. The acquisitiveness of
Great Britain has long been a matter of
comment and envy among other nations.
She “ wants the whole earth,” and there is
every appearance that, if not checked, she
will get it. She has been snatching pieces
for a century or two. She has colonised,
but about half her outer possessions are not
settlements, but conquests. Other nations
colonised, but Britain has the property.
Even Hindostan she gobbled up by beating
off the French, who had their teeth in the
big morsel. Canada she wrenched from the
French. The Cape of Good Hope from the
Dutch. The West Indian Islands are mostly
grabbed from first robbers in Europe.
Gibraltar is a conquest, Malta the same.
Great Britain has her hands— Briareus had
not so many- —on hundreds of spots on the
world’s surface. Whenever she has been at
war within the present century and a-lialf,
she has taken whatever oversea possession of
her enemy she chose to pounce on. And on
restoration of peace she has kept all she
dared. To wondering mankind this inevitable-
ness and regularity of appropriation has been
incomprehensible. It has, like most other
incomprehensible successes, been ascribed to
luck. The uniform good luck of a man or a
nation will generally excite envy, but not
animosity. It is felt to be useless to hate a
man or a nation because he or it is favored
by fortune. But if there be a trick or an
art by which one has exercised a constant
advantage, making him a sure winner
* * * #
Some half-a-dozen years ago a Yankee cap-
tain wrote a book about naval affairs. At
that time the United States had no navy.
Some aged wooden war-vessels pottered
about the ocean, exposing the flag of the
United States Republic to ridicule. Conse-
quently there was not much disposition to
pay attention when Capt. Mahan, of the
U.S. navy, published a volume about the
influence of the command of the sea in time
of war. But presently a conviction spread
that the American officer had said some-
thing important. He had. He had pre-
sented as a system of clearly-related cause
and effect events which previously had only
been viewed as detached and unrelated
casualties. The thing was so simple and
obvious that people only wondered they had
never seen it that way before. It was just
that by the command of the sea any power
at war could multiply the effectiveness of its
forces almost indefinitely. The proposition
was supported by familiar illustrative cases.
The reduction to absolute impotence of the
Great Napoleon, when in the fullest posses-
sion of his extraordinary military faculties,
was a case in point. Great Britain, dominat-
ing the sea, was able, when he ventured to
throw himself upon Egypt, to paralyse the
greatest soldier who has scourged the
world since Julius C.esar died. It has
long been understood that when armies are
in the field, that army conquers which
manages to have most men fighting at the
effective point. Thus, if 80,000 Austrians be
in the field against 60,000 Prussians, and, on
the collision coming about, only 40,000 of
the 80,000 are so posted as to be able to fire
a shot or strike a blow, while 50,000 out of
the 60,000 Prussians are so handled as to
deal out fire and steel to them, the
50,000 will beat the 40,000, and the
result will be that an army of 80,000
will have been vanquished by an army
of 60,000. Nelson fought and won all
his naval battles on the same plan. In
Australia it is popularly known as “ double-
banking.” Nelson manoeuvred so as to bring
two of his ships against one of the enemy’s,
leaving half the enemy’s fleet out of action
till he had dealt with the first lot. But it
had not occurred clearly to anyone, till Capt.
Mahan wrote it down, that the power which
could prevent an enemy from shifting troops
by sea, while itself freely moving forces
wherever it chose or whenever it chose, must
get the best of a war when the regions dis-
puted are situate far apart and are accessible
by sea.
* * * *
Capt. Mahan’s reasoning was absorbed by
the rulers of every nation. The Bulletin
has not seen Wdlliam of Germany reading
Mahan’s book, nor has it special informa-
tion as to which edition was bought for the
Russian Admiralty. But the book had not
long achieved fame when a tendency to give
enhanced attention to naval matters was ob-
servable on the part of England, the U.
States, France, Germany, and Russia. The
German Emperor received a sort of practical
lesson. Perhaps Mr. Chamberlain really
knew that there was an element of splendor
in the lone hand Great Britain had to play
when the German Emperor threatened to
make trouble in the Transvaal, and when he
talked about “ splendid isolation.” It was a
fact that, so far as operations in the Trans-
vaal—or any other part of Africa—are con-
cerned, Great Britain, small and weak as is
its army compared with the huge military
machine which Germany can put in motion,
did not need to care one finger-snap for all
Emperor William’s talk, nor for anything
he with his millions of splendid soldiers
could do. He couldn’t get an army to the
Transvaal, and he couldn’t supply one there
if he had it, and he couldn’t get it back
again. So long as Great Britain maintains
her naval supremacy, Africa is her oyster.
If Belgium quarrels with her, she annexes
with scarcely an effort the Congo State. If
France be troublesome, the army in Algeria
will get no reinforcements, and no fresh
material from blockaded Toulon. No Euro-
pean nation, fighting singly, could gain or
hold any place outside Europe without
British consent. Only in Asia could Russia,
and Russia alone, attempt to approach a
British territory, and even then Britain
could with ease and economy pour in troops
and material which would cost Russia agonies
of exertion and treasures of cost to make
head against.
* * * *
The present trouble is due, beyond doubt,
to the fact that all the Powers have been
awakened to the state of affairs. Outside
Europe and North America, Great Britain
has a lien on the worlds, and evidently means
to foreclose on a big lot of property. She is
sliming over Egypt preparatory to swallow-
ing it. One eye is on the Transvaal, which
will drop to her presently. A long finger
with a fish-hooky nail is thrust up the Nile,
and other "fingers are feeling about the Equa-
torial lake regions. Now, the nation which
grabs continents will have their trade. And
most of the nations want external trade and
plenty of it. The nation that gets most trade
makes most money, and becomes the richest
nation. The richest nation builds and
keeps most ships of war. Most ships of war
secure command of the sea. Command of
the sea secures continents for trade. The
argument is circular. The moral is per-
emptory. Foreign powers see that unless
they smash Great Britain’s power at sea
now, the nation will grow so tremendous
that it will want to buy or steal half of
' Europe for its private estate. The tendency
consequently is for a European coalition
against Britain. The united navies of the
other Powers would be about enough to do
the business. The difficulty is that France,
with her fixed idea of vendetta against
Germany, can’t be brought into the
league which comprises that nation. When
German William was threatening England
about the Boer trouble, Salisbury’s riposta
was a straight threat of seeking alliance with
France. Britain must continue her course
of territorial appropriations, or die. But if
that course be not arrested, other nations of
Europe will be overshadowed by her com-
mercial wealth and supremacy. It is not to be
conceived that she will seize half the world
merely to render it available for the “Made
in Germany ” trade. Chamberlain’s Im-
perial Zollverein scheme is the expression
of the inevitable. Clash of interests so
tremendously vital to different nations can
be settled only one way—by war. Three
European coalitions were organised against
France, by England. France triumphed—
with the help of a national frenzy—over the
first. She triumphed—with the help of the
greatest soldier of the age —over the second.
But even that help was insufficient to enable
her to triumph over the third. If European
coalitions against France were possible, so
also is a European coalition against England.
It would be a better speculation.
The Suffield Case: the Man Speaks for
Himself.
The Bulletin has recently devoted a good deal
of time to the Suffield case, and consideration
of much fresh information to hand strengthens
its conviction that the verdict was against the
evidence, and that the prisoner would probably
establish his legal innocence at any unbiassed
enquiry. In its original statement of the case,
though every effort was made to ensure cor-
rectness, this paper regrets that several minor
errors occurred through the depositions being
unattainable. (These depositions, by the way,
though promised to be laid on the table of
N.S.W. Assembly on Sept. 22 last, had not
been produced at time of writing, nearly three
weeks afterwards.) The prosecutrix was not
18, but 22 years old, at the time of the alleged
assault. This cannot be called a . “ hysterical
age.” The maximum sentence was not imposed :
the Judge could have awarded 14 years in lieu of
10. With these exceptions, which in no way in-
validate the main argument, every scrap of fresh
information this paper has received goes to
strengthen the case for Suffield. The Bul-
letin has also been handed a recent communi-
cation from Suffield himself. It is acute,
forcible, and leaves a strong impression of the
man’s innocence. Some extracts follow, en-
tirely in Suffield’s own words.
* * * *
Suffield was convicted on Bth April, 1891.
He gives “a brief r6sum6 of my own efforts [to
establish innocence] by approach to the au-
thorities.” Until October, 1891, he states that
he was not aware that, being under separate
treatment, he could do anything for himselt.
In October (or about), ’9l, I made a lengthy statement
to the Comptroller, asking to be allowed to lay my case
before the Minister. In three days I got a point-blank
refusal, the Comptroller telling me he saw no reason for
allowing me to do so : that the evidence I adduced was
not fresh evidence, since it was available at the time of
trial.
As already pointed out, the statement that
“ fresh evidence ” is necessary before a case can
be re-opened is an official fiction. All that the
relevant Act requires is a petition from the
prisoner or made on his behalf, based on “ any
doubt or question ” arising as to his guilt, or
any mitigating circumstance, or any portion of
the evidence. But the Botany Bay bureau-
cracy has invented a gloss to slay the merciful
hope given by legislative enactment.
From then to April, ’92, I made repeated applications to
the governor of gaol, and to Mr. , the visiting magis-
trate.
In April, ’92 (about 11th) I saw Lord Jersey, Mr.
actually bringing me before his Lordship in these expres-
tions : “ Your Excellency, this is an absolutely innocent
man, and every word he says is the truth : that is, to the
best of my belief. I do not wish to compromise -—, who
was good to me ; so I have never referred to these strong
expressions of his and do not wish to. But Earl Jersey
repeated them.
Nevertheless The Bulletin refers to them
(suppressing the names), since if Mr. -, as
visiting magistrate, really believed this, he said
not one whit more than he ought; and The
Bulletin now calls upon Mr. , as an
honorable man and a magistrate of N.S.W.,
sworn to do its justice, to step forward and tell
the public if he used these words, and, that
being so, the grounds on which he believes
that Suffield is absolutely innocent.
In June, ’92, by order of Minister O’Connor, I again
made a lengthy statement, corroborating every paragraph
of it by' references, &c. I think it, was January , ’93,
before this was answered. Or, rather, it was never
answered. For Mr. Slattery, during his interregnum
(Mr. O’Connor having resigned) replied: “In answer to
the petition for a remission of sentence, the Minister sees
no grounds,” &c. I never dreamt of a petition for remis-
sion, and in every statement I have addressed to authori-
ties I have always specially stated that I did not wish for
any remission, but for an act of justice, which was cried
for by the whole facts of my case. If I ever asked for
remission I challenge the Minister to show such a re-
quest on any paper of mine. I never stooped to it.
From June, ’92, to January, ’93; I saw the visiting jus-
tice, the gaol authorities, the clergyman, and the Bishop
of Bathurst, each of whom made statements as follows.
The, visiting justice, who told me each time I came
before him that he thought I would have been a free man
before this, told the governor to make enquiries at head
office as to my papers. The governor (Mr. Stace) did so ;
no reply.
The clergyman. Rev. H. C. Vindin, wrote three or four
times to the Minister, begging that something might be
done in the matter, and declaring his firm and absolute
conviction of my perfect innocence.
The Bishop of Bathurst himself wrote to the Minister,
bringing it under his knowledge.
Mr. Vindin got answers to say “ the matter was under
consideration”; “the matter was under consideration.”
(The gaol authorities, to repeated applications, got no
answer.)
The Bishop got an answer to say' “ the papers were
before Dr. Manning for his report on them.”
During all this time I saw the Comptroller once of my
own accord, and twice at his request. He told me the
delay was not occasioned by him.
In the meantime, between April, ’92, and Jan., ’93, what
were my' friends doing outside? Were they not, too,
making repeated applications ?
I have had to trust entirely' to memory in every state-
ment I have made, nor have I told of one-half the appli-
cations I myself made between April, ’92, and Jan., ’93
none of which received any answer, as far as I can re-
member.
After narrating requests (refused) to be per-
mitted the services of an agent outside, Suf-
field goes on to say :
I have made whilst here, acting on Mr. ’s advice, all
sorts of advances to the Minister.
I then heard the Minister was coming here. The regu-
lations allowing any' prisoner to see a Minister of the
Crown, I put down my name to see him. Here, I thought,
was a golden chance. Just before this, I had received a
communication from him to this effect. “The Minister
cannot reopen the case unless the prisoner can produce
evidence not previously given before a court, tending to
show a reasonable doubt as to his guilt. But the Minister
was very much impressed with the reiterated statements
of the prisoner as to his innocence.”
Note the relentless tenacity of the official
fiction as to “ evidence not previously given be-
fore a court.” But there was fresh evidence in
the Minister’s hands : four important affidavits
were sworn by James Warren, Lydia Warren,
and Annie Warren, with whom Suffield had
lived, and by Robert Murphy, his room-mate
in the Warrens’ house, containing a great deal
of fresh and most striking evidence never given
at the trial. Those affidavits were buried along
with Suffield in his pigeon-hole, and the De-
partment of Alleged J ustice, beyond an acknow-
ledgment of receipt, has made not a sign re-
garding them to this day. What powerful
influences must be at work to keep Suffield’s
case away from tbe light of enquiry !
You may imagine, after so important an admission—for
would he have been impressed by my statements if they
were not based on solid ground ? —how my heart beat at
the thought of seeing him.
Well, he came ; he saw warders who wanted to transfer
to other gaols; he never said whether he would see me
or not, but simply went away without ever seeing the
man about whose guilt he was by his own admission more
than doubtful, who was undergoing moral murder and


unspeakable misery, and whom it was his highest duty, as
the earthly Minister of Justice, which is Divine, to pro-
tect, help, and consider. For, for whom IS the Minister
of Justice elected, if not for the utterly helpless ? My
heart felt broken.
Poor devil!
* * * *
The Bulletin, as before said, does not Jcnoiv
whether Suffield is innocent or guilty. But
it knows that, as a juryman, it could not find
him guilty on the evidence now before it, and it
demands, in the name of Justice, a prompt and
searching enquiry into Suefield’s case, with
every opportunity given him to prove his inno-
cence if he can. Out with Suffield from his
pigeon-hole !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Economic Revolution.
Editor Bulletin, —The small interest taken
abroad in the conflict now waging in the United
States is astounding. It is widely believed, by
friends and foes alike, that if not in Nov. next,
then four years later, a free-silver President
will be elected —Congress and Senate have been
captive long ago. And free silver practically
means the discounting of all monetary obliga-
tions by, say, 30 to 50 per cent. As the States
are the most gigantic borrowers in the world,
with personal state and corporate liabilities
totalling billions of dollars, such an act of re-
pudiation is second only to the vast land-confis-
cation of “The Terror.” Many of the Aus-
tralian dailies have stated that the silver cry in
the States is the voice of the mine-owner, as
they assert with equal cogency that Protection
is only the voice of a few manufacturers. What
does the back-blocks cocky, when he votes solid
for a tariff, trouble about unknown manufac-
turers ? In like manner the farmer of the
Western States cares not a cent for Nevada
silver men. He believes dear gold is ruin-
ing him under an unjust contract and that
the necessities of existence should override
that contract. For good or ill he is going to
try the experiment of white money, and furnish
us with a thrilling object-lesson more useful
than a truck-load of tracts. The press of the
Eastern States and Great Britain have consis-
tently minimised, ignored, or ridiculed the
issue at stake ; as though it was incredible that
a nation of farmers bowed down to earth in a
hopeless struggle against crushing mortgages
should kick against the pricks. “ Was there not
a bond ? ” How stands the question ethically ?
If the individual kills his enemy it is murder ;
if a nation it is heroic. If a man repudiates his
bond he is a swindler ; if a people does so, by
parity of reasoning, it should be “ the just
made perfect.” Australians, themselves deep in
the bog of debt, only hear vague rumors of this
strife in which they are so interested ; for this
new way of paying obligations, if successful, is
too attractive not to meet with wide-spread
imitation.—H. F.

Why Not Gamble ?
If Waddell, M.P., succeeds in persuading
N.S. W. Assembly to legalise certain art-unions
and lotteries, his success should settle the
gambling question. Waddell’s logical fault is
that he does not go far enough. He should
legalise all gambling, or, at least, make it not
illegal. The arguments of those against him
start altogether on wrong premises. They
assert that gambling is sinful, or, at all events,
is undesirable. The first contention is pre-
sumptuous and begs, the question; the second
is arguable, and not worth much even if allowed
to be tenable. Where Waddell’s armor of
logic is easily pierced is in the open joints that
would make co-operative gambling legal and
individual gambling illegal. Nevertheless, if
the lawmakers, and thereby the people, sanction
such a law, the public sanction would make
sucli gambling “ right,” notwithstanding the
contention of such publicists as Sydney Daily
Telegraph that public sanction cannot make
“ wrong ”“ right.” It can. Itdoes. “Right”
and “wrong” are abstract terms, and have as
many variations as there are communities. If
public sanction cannot make “ wrong ” “ right,”
it cannot make “right” “wrong.” But it
does. For instance : It is not ethically
“ wrong ” for a man to have 300 wives and 900
concubines, or such a wise and good man as
Solomon wouldn’t have had them. But Aus-
tralian law says it is “ wrong ” for one man to
have more than one wife at a time, and—well,
it is “ wrong.” Similarly, if “ public sanction ”
declared that murder was “right ” it would be
“ right,” for all practical purposes. There can
be no hard-and-fast definition of “right”
any more than there can be ever any
answer to Pilate’s question—“ What is
Truth ? ” The fact is, as regards gam-
bling, that the modern pietistic mania of
anti-gambling is the chlorosis of civilised hypo-
crisy. Everybody gambles. “ Chance ”is the
most potent factor in every human destiny, in-
dividual or national. It is because man has no
control over, and no defence against, “Chance,”
that he is a natural gambler. The merchant
gambles daily ; he miscalls “ Chance ” fore-
sight. Where it isn’t “ Chance,” purely and
simply, it is “ inside information ” —the very
thing that the turf-gambler and the monte-
magsman punt on. Only, those gentlemen
honestly name it “swindling.” The clergy
gamble. They, too, are not content with
“ judicious investments ” in lands, stocks, &c.,
according to their earthly lights, but they do
the purse-trick most effectually with lottery-
tickets cashable in Heaven. All the poor
widows and distressed clergymen who confessed
themselves crushed in the last big boom, merely
lost on a gamble. “ Chance ” was unkind to
them; but they “ went for ” what they
“ thought.” No need to illustrate by means of
Stock Exchanges and insurance companies.
Those bodies, at all events, don’t drape them-
self with hypocrisy. And they are legal, and
therefore “ right ” —in the sense used in this
consideration. The law that seeks to stamp out
gambling must, first of all, abolish “Chance.”
And law, itself, is pretty nearly all “Chance.”
We are all bound to gamble according to the
“ Chances ” that offer. It is to be hoped that
Waddell will succeed in his Bill, and that the
chlorosis of hypocrisy may be cured after-
wards.
The Legal Trade-Union.
The Ekaminers in Law of Melb. University
lately refused a law-student, who had passed
his final examination, his pass certificate —the
possession of which was necessary to enable
him to apply to the Full Court to be admitted
to practise as a member of the Legal Trade
Union, giving as their reason for so doing that
he held a commission in the Defence Force,
thereby contravening the rules forbidding a
candidate for admission to the Union to engage
in any occupation but that of a lawyer’s clerk
whilst serving the three years’ article to as
solicitor which every embryo shark is required
to serve by said rules. The student appealed
to the Full Court, but while the point was
being discussed the Examiners met in the
Court library, drew up the required certificate,
delivered it to the student in court ere the ink
on it was dry, and so saved the point from
being decided. According to the opinion of
Attorney-General Isaacs, it is clear under the
Rules that during the period covered by his
articles a student is prohibited from engaging
in any occupation but that of clerk to the law-
yer to whom he is articled. As an articled clerk
who is paid a salary is the rarest of mortals,
this prohibition effectually keeps out of the
Union anyone who cannot afford to do nothing
for a living for three years. The nominal
reason for requiring service of articles is that
the student may be familiarised with the prac-
tical work of the Union, but this reason is
“ given away ” by the fact that even 10 or 20
years’ good service in an office does nothing
towards qualifying an ordinary clerk, who, if
he can find time to study after his day’s work
is done—the articled clerk does a good deal of
his studying in office hours—must still serve
his three years in articles even though during
the better part of the 10 or 20 years he has had
the control of matters much more important
than come the way of three-fourths of the
Union in a lifetime. The real reason of the
requirement of articles is, of course, to confine
the membership of the Union to its present
members, their and their relations’ sons and the
sons of influential clients; and, to keep these
latter from being too eager to rush a good thing,
a premium is charged which, even in these
cut - throat times, in some offices runs as
high as £5OO. Again, a person desirous
of entering the profession is required to
pass an examination in Latin, History, &c.,
before he can enter into articles —for, so the
legal ring alleges, no person can understand the
law unless he has passed such examination.
Admitting the truth of this allegation, it is not
necessary that such examination should be com-
pulsory, as the student who had not passed it
would be unable to pass in law'. The fact is,
that the only subject in which a law-student
should be compelled to pass is law. Articles
should be abolished ; they are out of place in a
democratic country, creating as they do two
classes of clerks the young-gentleman-in-
articles and the common prospectless attorney’s
clerk. The United States and Maoriland get
on very well without articles, and any one who
has been sharked in those progressive countries
knows that their sharks are quite as competent
to shark as any other sharks.

Through. Chinese Spectacles.
Translation of remarks in a recent issue of the
Kwong Yick Wah Bo (Chinese paper pub-
lished at Sydney) re the anti-Chinese agitation
at Darlington and Redfern, N.S. W. :
Some low-class Europeans, who have not been very well
educated in the practice of hiding their feelings, are try-
ing to stop other Europeans from trading withjour country-
men in some of the outside places near Sydney. We know
that this agitation only comes from the ignorant, and that
the better classes of Europeans will not hold with it
because they have learned, as the Chinese have also
learned, that competition in business is like two nations
at war. In war if one country has the best generals and
the best ships and the best trained soldiers, and it succeeds
in overcoming, the country that is defeated does not cry
out to all the peoples of the earth that it was unfairly
defeated because the conquerors were the best prepared
for the war. The defeated country goes to work hard
without sparing pains to find the causes of its want of
success, and so often learns by its previous defeats to
remedy its defects, so that many days after it can succeed
by equalling or excelling its adversary. If these Europeans
cry out against Chinese competition it is a sign that they
fear it, and it is pitiable to hear strong men weeping with
fear. Let them rather recognise that it is by excellence
of management, by industry, by the habit of daily accu-
mulation taught us by our parents that the Chinese
succeed in business in foreign lands, when they have the
disadvantages of unjust laws and prejudiced lower orders
to contend with. Let them so recognise these things,
and when their brains have got them let them copy the
Chinese and they will be able to get some competency.
It is a saying with us that when a pig shrieks he
knows he is in Hanger, and that when a merchant talks
about his business troubles to strangers it is good to stop
his credit. Everything gets on through competition.
Even the Labor-members who are helping this agitation,
and are, of course, intelligent men themselves, though
those who hearken to them are not—are in the very act of
stultifying themselves competing for the votes of the
ignorant. These members live by politics, and they get
their living by the votes of the ignorant, and the man
who pretends he can do the most impossibilities gets the
most votes, and the man who will not promise to
get the moon down for the ignorant class of Europeans
cannot hope to get to be a member by their votes. So
that the inflaming of the prejudices of the ignorant is a
kind of competition, and a kind of competition that those
who are readers of Kwong Yick Wah Bo would not use.
We would compete with the Europeans, and try to show
them that we would exceed them in justice. If Chinese
here gamble for a few pennies it is thought very bad, and
shows such a wicked example as makes everybody shiver.
And then the members of Parliament and the police and
the magistrates go to the races and gamble for hundreds
of pounds, and no one shivers at all—except the Kwong
Yick Wah 80, which remembers what was said of Kung-
Fu-TZE-that he avoided a hypocrite as he avoided a par-
ricide. But we do not stop the Europeans in China from
betting on races at Shanghai. This shows that the
Chinese are not hypocrites and that they love justice.
Concerning Some Policemen.
Dear Bulletin, —One of the first things to
excite a snort from the Anglomaniac new-chum
in this city is the policeman. He appears so
absurdly thin-skinned and deficient in honest
self-esteem. A larrikin utters an inane ex-
pression of contempt for the man in blue as he
struts along his beat, and the official, instead of
walking on, with calm, stony countenance,
turns upon the larrikin and probably ends an
angry discussion by arresting the fellow for
insulting behaviour. Unnecessary interference
with “ drunks ” is a special line of Melbourne
police conduct, which, to British new-chums,
fresh from-a country where “drunks” are
never molested, save in disorderly cases, seems
contemptible. One naturally thinks it is a
pity that guardians of the peace don’t find
work more important. Their desire to assert
themselves in small matters is unmanly, like
their dread of ridicule. Only a few years ago
it was the police custom here to convey charges,
male and female, to the lock-up in a cab, a cus-
tom founded on constabulary notions of dignity.
“ Bobbie” was afraid of getting cliafFfrom the
small boys if he walked his prisoner to the police-
station. It wasn’t the feelings of the arrested
party that he studied, but his own feelings.
This by way of introduction to a story which
may be left to speak for itself. Last week a
“captain” and a “ private ” in the Salvation
Harmy were charged at Footscray (Melb.)
Police Court with using insulting words. They
had been engaged in working up an “open-air
service ” (Footscray is a dull place where such
Harmy business doesn’t interfere with traffic)
when a constable ordered them to move on.
Ere moving on as desired the noisy evangelists
“ remarked that the police were of no use ex-
cept to ‘ run-in ’ inoffensive people. They did
nothing to interfere with Sunday-trading at
hotels owned by magistrates.” On the strength
of which petulant but very truthful remarks
the constable summoned the two “soldiers.”
He felt wounded in his honor. As master of
the situation he could very well have afforded
to disperse the meeting and let the “insulting
words ” go for what they were worth. He pre-
ferred to bring the offenders before the Court.
They were fined 10s. and costs apiece, in default
48 hours’ imprisonment, and in the name of the
Lord they went to gaol.— Melbournian.
The Libel Law.
In a recent English case a person was libelled
in similar fashion by each of several news-
papers, all of which obtained the matter
complained of from a common Press Agency.
Plaintiff, on bringing his action, joined all the
papers as defendants. The jury found for
£lOOO damages, which they apportioned
amongst the various journals. Which pro-
cedure affords a fine example of common-sense
applied to law of libel. In N.S. Wales, which,
in all matters of legal reform, is generally about
a quarter of a century behind England, such a
course would be impossible. Here, each news-
paper would have been dragged separately
before a jury, and cast in damages varying
from £5OO to £lOOO, and the fortunate plaintiff
would have been made independent for life.
All newspapers deriving their information from
a common source run the risk of printing
something libellous —for the purpose of the
argument it matters nothing whether the libel
be a gross one or not —and if by accident they
be caught napping, they lay themselves open
to actions for damages. The libelled one may
be a man of straw ; but each paper must sub-
mit to be separately blackmailed. After plaintiff
has brought a dozen or forty-nine libel-actions
and obtained thumping damages in each, the
defendant in the thirteenth or fiftieth cannot
hope to escape adding his quota to the plain-
tiff’s banking-account, by showing that the
alleged injury to his (the plaintiff’s) character
has been amply salved by the proceeds of 12 or
45 previous verdicts. Even though the jury
did take that into consideration, each paper
would still have been put to the expense of a
separate defence. There is no excuse for such
a state of affairs. In the event of identical or
similar libels against the same person appear-
ing in a number of newspapers, common jus-
tice demands that the law should compel him
to consolidate his actions against the journals,
or, in default of his so doing, should provide
that judgment against one should be a bar to
all other actions arising out of the same subject
matter. This would not be unfair to a plaintiff,
for the jury could assess the damages in a lump
sum, stipulating what portion each paper should
pay. The speculative lawyer, who wants
“something on account” before issuing a writ
against a newspaper, would find a law of this
character little to his taste; his satellite, the
impecunious barrister, who looks to “ the other
side ” for payment of his fees, would find it
still less so ; and both would probably consider
the country was going to the devil; otherwise,
however, this alteration in legal procedure
would be an unmixed blessing.
Another Soudan Picnic.
New South Wales military authorities are
metaphorically about to twine a garland round
the horns of the Soudan goat. Another
gorgeous picnic is being incubated, and there is
the usual appeal to the miserable taxpayer to
pay for the beer and sandwiches. As it was
only the other day that there was a most suc-
cessful military “ demonstration ” up the
country, with champagne-corks popping
in all directions, and a big bill there-
for to be footed by the Treasury, despite
the most solemn protestations that the uni-
formed beneficiaries would pay up the deficit,
it is not surprising that Premier Reid rather
hangs back. He is only asked for £lsoo—a
mere trifle of £l5O0 —but as a gentleman in
scarlet-and-swaggah the other day remarked :
“ We only want the detn’d—haw—Gov’ment
to back our bill—and we’ll—haw —take care the
bill is all right. Once they go in with us they
cawn’t draw back —don’t you know !—and our
troubles about the £1500.” The thing proposed
is to send 32 fine-looking military people and
their horses to a British tournament next year
—to strut in Queen Vic.’s 60-years-reign
pageant, permit the British populace to gape
at their exhibition of “ colonial loyalty,”
and have “ a heluva good time, bai jove !
in society ” —more or less at the expense
of the beforementioned miserable taxpayer.
Why he should stump up doesn’t appear.
He won’t get any of the prize-money, or
the wine, or the ladies’ favors, or the big
cigars, or the glorious crimson nights. There
are frequent military tournaments in India,
but the regimental authorities never dream of
asking for a Govt, subsidy. If they did they
would be laughed at. The honor of victory—
and the profit—are quite enough for them ; and
if this Australian expedition has luck it may
easily clear expenses, since the prizes for which
it competes run into thousands of pounds. But
Australia and India are different.. Here is the
eternal home and happy hunting-ground of the
sponger on State revenues. Just a little
“ influence ” judiciously applied to the right
man at the right moment; and you can bleed
the Treasury till further orders. The beautiful
military authorities know this right well ; but
Premier Reid seems the wrong kind of peanut.
So the bait is hung out that the picnic will give
a splendid stimulus to the Australian horse-
trade—which is cunning. With the swagger
picnic aspect carefully put out of sight, and the
“business and commercial” idea prominently
put forward, surely the tax-paying schnapper
will grab this little squid !
“ President Faure, in proposing the toast of the Im-
perial visitor, said that on his arrival in France he would
feel that the heart of France beat in the capital. The
Czar, in responding to the toast, said that he fully recip-
rocated the warm sentiments felt for Russia by a friendly
nation. The Czar and President Faure then clinked their

Victoria’s talking Chief Justice, in a lecture
to Brunswick branch of the A.N.A., on what
he called “Safe (i.e., Tory) Politics,” illus-
trated the baneful effect to the State of the re-
publican form of government by pointing out
that, while the Chinese empire had existed
through tens of centuries to the present day,
the Greek, Roman and Venetian republics
had endured but for a comparatively brief
period. This dreadful inaccuracy and this
lamentable tendency to talk through his
hat seems to explain why Mr. Madden’s
judgments are reversed so often. China
certainly has endured for thousands of
years, for it was too big to be taken up with a
shovel and a barrow and carried away, but its
empire hasn’t lasted anything like that time.
China has been a succession of empires, as one
conqueror after another mopped up the great,
weak, squashy state which never had enough
vitality to be really alive, but there is no cer-
tain evidence that any one of them lasted
longer than the great republic of Venice.
And the Venetian republic is more alive
to-day, seeing that it has become part
of a semi-republican monarchy, than China
which is bossed by a foreign emperor. And
China is an exception, anyhow. The average
monarchy lives probably a century or two or
three; the empires of Alexander, the two
Napoleons, Charlemagne, Iturbide of
Mexico, Dessalines of Hayti, the Latin king-
doms of Constantinople and Jerusalem, the king-
dom of Joseph of Spain (which endured about
12 months), and many others could be picked out
to make an apparent average of about 20 years.
Anyhow, Venice hung out for 12 centuries, and
Switzerland has been a tolerably healthy re-
public for about 600 years, so it seems that
even the prospect of being ultimately disap-
proved of by Chief Justice Madden didn’t blight
them altogether. And who wouldn’t rather be
a dead Switzer or a defunct Venetian than a
live Chinaman ?

That little incidental scandal re the trial of the
City of Melbourne Bankers arose out of a bold
attempt to charge it, in a hazy sort of way,
against the Crown prosecution. The facts ot
the case are clear enough. When the jury was
being empanelled the person “ challenging” on
behalf of the defendants offered no objection to
a juryman who happened to be the father of a
youth employed in the office of Loader, jun.
Loader, jun., was in court at the time, repre-
senting the legal firm engaged for the defence
of Loader, sen., yet it did not occur to him to
suggest that this particular juryman (after-
wards elected foreman) might possibly be sus-
pected of a bias towards the Loader side. The
relationship of the foreman to young Loader’s
clerk leaked out almost immediately, but the
Crown Prosecutor reasonably assumed that the
son’s little billet would not influence the father’s
verdict. Therefore, all the counsel concerned
in the case lay low and said nothing until the
Age published an exaggerated statement of the
“scandal.” Then Purves, Q.C., arose on be-
half of the defence and railed at the newspaper-
par. as being an inspired attempt to injure
the cause of the unfortunate gentlemen on
trial, whereas it was merely a “ giving
away ” of a fact. Purves, Q.C., having
raised a dust, the Crown-Prosecutor felt called
upon to address Judge Holroyd, who adjourned
the Court in order to consult with his brother
Judges. The Judges in conclave came to the
conclusion the Crown-Prosecutor had arrived
at days before — viz., that the juryman’s son
made no probable difference to the course
of justice, and the trial went on again as usual,
after Purves had apologised for insinuating
that the Age paragraph was maliciously in-
spired. For any “scandal” connected with
the jury foreman the defence is solely respon-
sible. In the first place, the challenger acting
for Loader, jun., was not instructed to object
to the father of Loader, jun.’s clerk, and in
the next place, when the identity of the parent
aforesaid became known to all the defending
lawyers and counsel, they were content to hug
in silence the remote possibility of an unfair
advantage. When the silence was publicly
broken, their leading man rushed in to impute
improper motives to the other side, without
showing an atom of excuse for his manoeuvre.

Perjurer-poisoner Dean’s Privy Council
appeal is progressing. As Dean’s funds were
exhausted, the appeal was made in forma pauj irt*.
The Council has agreed to dispense with the usual
official fees, and as barrister Colonna Close is per-
sonally providing the London attorney’s fees,
the convict’s appeal will be heard, after all.

Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
In point of interest and value, “A Gilded
Fool,’ at Sydney Lyceum, comes about midway
between “ Ihe Nominee ” and “In Mizzoura. ” It
is a good way in front of the first, and a good way
behind the second. Intrinsically, the play is poor
enough ; almost all its effect comes, or does not
come, in the acting. It cries for mercy where a
play of Pinero’s merely asks for justice. All the
more to the credit of Nat Goodwin and his com-
pany that it is made fairly plausible and suffi-
ciently interesting.
* * * *
. The “Gilded Fool’’ is a young American
in love with the daughter of a banker whose part-
ner has brought him to the verge of ruin by reck-
less speculation. The Pool is rich, and the
partner, representing the firm as solvent, per-
suades. him to throw in his wealth to
its aid and become a co - partner. The
banker, however, refuses to accept the sacri-
uce; the firm fails, and is resuscitated by
the Gilded Fool, who is naturally rewarded
with the daughter; while the wicked partner is
shipped off to England to answer for previous
misdeeds. Nat Goodwin, as the Gilded Fool, is
half the play and a little bit over. He deftly
exaggerates the author’s comedy and subtly tones
down the author’s exaggerated tragedv. In a
word, he acts with brains. Most actors' act with
a good memory, or a good voice, or a fine stage
presence : they take what the author gives them,
and render it for the public as capably as they
can, or as they can’t. But Goodwin, like every
decent actor with all but half-a-dozen authors,
brings to the partnership far more than the play-
writer. He transfigures the drama, invents effects,
creates situations, intensifies what is weak, softens
crudities, and turns ranting puppets into flesh and
blood. To see what an actor can do with a head
is to nourish perennial regret that most actors
have none.
* * * *
In the first act the play is a little too
tedious, or the theatre is rather too warm, but the
effect is bad. When ruin hovers round the banker
the interest gets tenser, and it would be strong if
Clarence Handyside, the traditional heavy father,
would be a little less stereotyped and a little more
natural. Even Goodwin, by-the-way, fails to rise
to the height of one of his spasmodic serious moods.
You feel that there is thunder in the air and
declamation in prospect, and then —swish !—on
turns the limelight, the stage is- full of red fire,
and Goodwin declaims to slow music as per
formula. Why won’t the stage be natural ? The
red light and the music are highly unnatural:
nobody in real life ever uses them, or wants them ;
and the amount of their un-natufe is-precisely the
amount by which the actor’s result—his impression
on his audience—is spoiled.
** t * *
Frazer Coulter does the erring partner
vigorously and dramatically : a shade less unction
might be desirable, but his stilts are dwarfs to
Handyside’s. Arthur Hoops, as a minor lover,
leaves a good impression ; and the ladies are
moderately effective, Miss Browning being per-
haps a trifle more convincing than the rest. There
is, indeed, no very deep hole anywhere in the act-
ing, and the play is presented for all it is worth.
Next Saturday sees the first performance of “ An
American Citizen,” to be produced “for the first
time on any stage.”
** * *
“ Foiled,” at Sydney Majesty’s, is a drama
of a circular-saw, which is supposed to more or
less devour the hero at the instigation of the
villain, and the audience heaves and breathes
throughout the evening in anticipation of seeing
the awful teeth br-r-ring through the buttons.
The saw is artfully put as the very last item on
the programme of sensations, and it is unsatisfac-
tory to find that it does not devour, and does not
br-r-r, and does not even revolve. Moreover, it is
a very small saw, and its teeth are obviously tin,
and the audience has breathed itself into expecta-
tion of a gigantic implement as large as a small
moon, whizzing round with frightful rapidity, and
filling the stage with whirlwinds and roar.
* * * *
With the exception of the saw, “ Foiled ”
is a more or less satisfactory performance of the
usual melodramatic stamp.' It includes a rather
sticky villain, who is “recognised” in the
first and second acts for fun, and in the
third for keeps. There is also Wilfred Shine,
who makes love to his brother’s girl in the
drawing-room of a villa near Maitland and
a brown suit and gaiters ; and tracks the
villain to his lair (in a brown suit and
gaiters) ; and engages in a phenomenal rescue
wife, who has been thrown
oyer a chff (in a brown suit and gaiters); and
is finally tied on to the saw-bench by the villain,
and informed that he is about to meet his Maker
(m a brown suit and gaiters). Thanks to the in-
of Edward Fanning, who is a dumb
aboriginal in a torn shirt (to show there is no decep-
tion and he is really aboriginal all over), this un-
pleasantness is averted. The comic man, in the
name of John Forde, is by no means as sad as the
usual comic man ; and Miss Lita and Miss Wise-
man supply the most capable acting that the
conglomeration admits of.
** * *
At the Criterion the Brough Co. is making
merry over the last nights of “ Mrs. Ponderbury’s
Past, ’ with “ A Pair of Spectacles ” (preceded by
Gentle Gertrude, a musical melodrama ”) in
Saturday s prospect. The Tivoli announces the
departure of the Cinematographe, and the reap-
pearance for a few nights of the great Tennyson
and the equally great O’Gorman. The Lumiere
Cinematographe continues to boom marvellously
as it deserves in Pitt-st.; and Bland Holt’s
For England,” at the Theatre Royal, still waves
a furious flag and defies a frightened universe to
come on.
