“A Milk White Flag,” (like “A Trip to
Chinatown,” a musical comedy by Hon. Charles
Hoyt), was performed last Saturday night at
Melb. Princess’ to a crowded audience by the same
American artists as before, with the assistance of
another ’Murkan comedian and our old friend
Howard Vernon. The audience on Saturday felt
grateful for friend Vernon as long as he was get-
ting occasional chances to shine by comparison,
but after a while he took a back seat and faded
from view. A good many people concerned in
1 A Milk White Flag ” have to sit back and be
forgotten most of the time, whereas those who
seldom disappear for long are the very ones who
really ought to vanish altogether. Hence the per-
formance is probably open to improvement.
* * * •
“A Milk White Flag” has spots of
humorous business, and many humorous sugges-
tions crudely carried out. It takes as a subject
for satire the. amateur playing-at-soldiers of the
modern American militia, but satire in this case
seldom rises above the level of “ chaff’’—horse-
chaff. An idea that might have been effectively
treated by Gilbert and Sullivan is not made too
much of by Hon. Ployt. Hoyt’s capacity for writing
a libretto and stuffing it with unoriginal music is
not superior to that of Rice, the “ Evangeline ”
man, who never called himself honorable in the
bill of fare. Hoyt, all the same, is a more pun-
gent joker than his awful prototype. The leading
item in “A Milk White Flag” is a cadaverous
husband guilty of fraudulent designs on a life-
assurance society, and when he conspires with an
undertaker to feign disease his wife, or the pro-
fessional corpse-tenders, put his live body on ice
to keep him fresh. This is wholesome fun, although
it smacks of the charnel-house, seeing that the local
Ransome Guards take the opportunity to enroll
the alleged stiff ’un as a brother officer, and
arrange to bury him with full military honors.
The corpse on ice catches cold, and gets out of his
coffin to sneeze, consequently the funeral is in-
definitely postponed. A harmless old general, a
relic of the real American Army, who has been
doing nothing in particular all the evening, sud-
denly offers a milk-white flag to the Ransome
Guards, and the unfurling of the banner is a signal
for the audience to disperse.
-** * *
George A. Beane, as the corpse-on-ice, is a
fair success ; also, stage-manager Mitchell, cast
for the part of the undertaker. The fetching
Miss Sadie M‘Donald, in a short frock, would be
more generally acceptable if she could sing, and the
only two performers who were backed heavily on
their individual merits were Miss Bessie Clayton
and the whistler, once again. Miss Clayton’s new
dance is perhaps not quite as popular as her other
dances, but Alf. Lawton fools cleverly in addition
to whistling. Harry Conor’s vaunted travesty of
Buonaparte proves to be rather a disappointment,
for better comedians have produced much more
striking imitations of the Napoleonic pictures.
Miss M‘Caim plays an abbreviated second edition
other “Chinatown” widow married, to the ice-
bound-remains. As regards the plump tenor and
bulky baritone, The Bulletin will forbear from
saying much. The tenor is disguised in whiskers,
otherwise they present the same twin appearance as
before in tight-fitting uniforms. The baritone
gentleman opens his mouth freely to sing ballads
as before, at which times death, on ice, has no
terrors for the present dejected scribe. Two
pretty scenes by George Gordon grace this musical
comedy, and the management has supplied an ex-
cellently-dressed chorus. The chorus comes on
occasionally and retires as soon as possible to the
invariable regret of everybody in front. If there
were a lot more chorus, a little new music, and no
solo singing in the show ; also, if the corpse were
iced in public instead of being refrigerated off the
stage, “ The Milk White Flag ” might even repeat
the furious “Chinatown” hoom. It will, of
course, improve much on its first night attempt.
* #. * *
“Robbery Under Arms” is for various
reasons raging furiously at Melbourne Alexandra.
The principal reason is Dampier. An Alexandra
audience can always get persuaded by the easy
heroism of that veteran actor under any circum-
stances, and the combination of Dampier with
Australian bush is irresistible. Troopers Maginnis
and O’Hara come next in favor, on account of
their shocking discredit to the force, whilst even
the head policeman is considered to fill a long felt
want when Captain Starlight lodges a bullet in
his arm. For the rest, jthey are mostly Australian
natives revelling in localcolor and colonial oaths,
and, at the present rate of treasury returns, Ben,
Dick, and Jim may be expected to steal the fatted
calf until further notice.
** * *
“ Tommy Atkins,” in its last days at the
Royal, has taken on a new leading man. Plimmer
was originally the curate, who crumples liis white
choker in the dust and goes fighting for the
Queen, and the general effect of that slim young
man was highly satisfactory. Last week, George
Rignold engaged himself, at a vast expense, to
take Plimmer’s place, a change which made no
real difference to the show. Rignold is distinctly
too large for a pale-faced curate, but, on the other
hand, he has a fine assortment of stage tricks in
stock and an immense fund of British valor to fall
back upon at supreme moments. “Tommy
Atkins ” will retire on Friday, or thereabouts, and
rumors of “ Henry the Fifth ” are in the air. Mel-
bourne could do with some Henry Yee after all
these years.

A benefit concert to George Howard at
Prahran (Melb.) Town Hall last Friday testified
that Melb. Amateur Orchestral Society is some-
thing more than a matter of sounding brass and
tinkling cymbals. Among 45 performers who
follow the tactful Howard’s lead and get no indi-
vidual glory for their pains are only half-a-dozen
professionals, whilst the balance of amateurs in-
cludes 16 girls. These are said to be enthusiastic
young things with a fine capacity for discipline,
and their general appearance, as they sit to the
fore of the platform, is beautifully businesslike.
The two principal violins are Miss Florence Lewis
and little Miss May Nash ; nine other damsels
play first or second fiddles ; and four devote them-
selves to the ’cello or the viola.

The just-returned tenor, Weir, says that the perform-
ances of the French baritone Delmas, whom he heard
lately in Paris, are the finest he has witnessed anywhere.
Weir visited Shakespeare, the well-known London teacher,
and was initiated into the most improved style of breath-
ing, which has superseded the abdominal method.

with Foley, has tired of managing an American factory

He intends to visit Germany, and later on the London
boards will be his destination.

Re theatre accidents. Clement Scott remembers
Richard 111. striking Richmond’s sword into the Drury
Lane stalls. King Dick had to await his quietus till the
sword was politely handed up. In Henry IV., at Melb.
Royal, the Prince of Wales thus struck Hotspur’s sword
into the stalls, but Hotspur, of course, died immediately.
Why doesn’t Brough stage Wyndham’s beautiful play of
Rosemary ?
The company at the Novelty Theatre (Lond.), where the
poor stage hero was accidentally killed by his friend the
villain, wanted a change of piece on the night following
the catastrophe. This was impossible under the circum-
stances, but the management omitted the stabbing busi-
ness, to the intense, yet silent, regret of a large audience
that came prepared to shudder at the possibility of
another fatal mistake.
Frank Bateman, now touring profitably with his “ Har-
bor Lights ” co. through the English provinces, was
formerly a Melb. stonemason. He abandoned his chisel
and mallet to join Dampier’s co., at the Alexandra, after
which he opened a pub. in Westralia, and, having made
money, went ’Ome and blossomed into theatrical manage-
ment.
The Musical Courier wants to know why stars like
Melba and the De Reszke brothers can extract two ot
three times as much money for singing in America as they
are willing to accept in London, where they get consider-
ably higher fees than in Paris or St. Petersburg? Manager
Maurice Grau says it is because “ a ring, a combination, a
trust has been organised to keep prices on a fictitious
basis ” in America, and the Courier asks “ Why ? ” again.
The answer to which is simply that the singers, being able
to do it, make the most of their opportunity. In the first
place, fashionable, wealthy artists can’t be' tempted from
Europe to America except by the charm of exceptionally
fat fees, so their combination is a matter of mutual taste
as well as business. If a sliding-scale of charges were a
doubtful policy they might not adopt it, but experience
proves that theatrical “ stars,” unlike painters or shop-
keepers, can safely vary their prices for the same value
of goods, even in the same market. London music-hall
performers have for years been doing exactly the same
“turns” at various halls on a sliding scale. The ’alls
that take most money at the doors and refreshment-bars
are compelled to offer the biggest terms to the boss
“talent” of the moment. Managers grumble in vain.
Theoretically, Brown is not compelled to give £5O for the
particular services which Jones obtains for half the money,
or less. Practically, he is.

Lohr has been doing great business around Maoriland
with the Godfrey Vaudeville Co. Godfrey comes as a
revelation in serio-comic descriptive singing', and the co.
supplies a neat little assortment of “ turns.” On the last
night of the Dunedin season the police stepped in to stop
the sale of tickets. By the time the show got to Christ-
church even the local aristocracy, which had been wont to
snort at variety performances, passed a social resolution
to the effect that it could patronise Godfrey without
sinking in its self-esteem.

“ Promenade ” Concerts at Melb. Exhibition Building
are so termed because the audience remains carefully
seated all the time. The noise of many promenading feet
on the bare boards would drown the concert. Besides, all
the space within reasonable distance of the platform is
taken up by chairs.

Riviere’s Orchestra, lately performing around England
and Wales.

The Wild-Cat Column.
Labor-conditions at Broken Hill are causing
some anxiety. Many of the workmen are in a distinctly
rebellious mood, and don’t need much encouragement to
manifest it. On Eight-Hours Day practically the whole
of the underground men on day-shift—l9-20ths of them at
least—absented themselves in defiance of orders. Two or
three days after this (the day following a speech delivered
by the redoubtable Sleath, M.L.A.) the majority of
the truckers struck against the piece-work which had been
introduced. There are troublous times ahead, unless past
experiences are wholly useless as a guide to the future.
The truckers, who are mostly young men and of whom 90
per cent, belong to no union, had of course to return to
work as gracefully as they could. But the incident has
given the work of union-organising a fillip.
Junction North gets itself in funds in prepara-
tion for another div. by a sale of 1500 tons of shipping
ore for instant delivery. The contract was only possible
through over 1000 tons of ore being stacked on the sur-
face. The faces in this mine at the deep level have lately
been showing rather more garnet sand —the characteristic
deposit in this property; commercially valueless up to
date, and awkward to handle through its tendency to
“run.” Developments are, however, thoroughly satis-
factory. A winze below the deepest level—the 570 ft.—is
passing through friable sulphides, worth 360 z. silver and
66 per cent. lead.
Firewood costs the B.H. Proprietary Co. 14s. 6d.
per ton.- Most of it is brought in upon the Tarrawingee
line, but there are very limited supplies in that direction.
Of course not a great "deal of this fuel is used. Private
consumers in Broken Hill are paying over 20s. per ton.
The nearest supplies are towards Menindie, from 35 to 55
miles from the mines ; but no railway at present runs in
that direction.
Now estimated that from 40 to 42 per cent, of
the ore raised at Broken Hill is exported to Europe for
treatment.
“Made in Germany.” The B.H. Proprietary
now gets its coke from Germany. For a brief period
under Dibbs’ more or less Protectionist tariff, Newcastle
and other N.S.W. fields supplied the coke ; but the Con-
tinental maker is now landing a much purer article at
Port Pirie at 31s. per ton.
Concerning Silverton tram charges, thus a wit-
ness before N.S.W. Public Works Committee: “The
freight on a ton and a-half of ale from Sydney came to
£5 2s. lid., of which £2 13s. was charged by the Silverton
Tramway Co.” Silverton line is 35 miles long ; the other
distance is 300 by rail and 1250 by water.
B.H. Proprietary is reducing the strain upon its
slight carbonate reserves by the purchase of all the
second-grade concentrates produced by the British mill.
The latter treats from 2000 to 2200 tons of ore weekly, of
which about two-thirds are carbonates. The develop-
ments in the sulphide bodies in this mine are still fair in
lead, but from poor to very poor in silver. This mine
now employs about 400 men, of whom 260 are under-
ground.
The Birthday Co., Parkes, N.S.W., certainly is
the most persistently boomed mine in Australasia. Just
suppose for an instant that the alleged doings of every
other mine got proportionate space in the newspapers!
Why, there would be no room for anything else.
* * * *
Professor Liversidge finds huge beds of the
Hawkesbury sandstone to contain as much as 3dwt. of
gold to the ton, evenly distributed through the rock, and
several experts are on the search for a process to make its
extraction payable. The late Government geologist-
parson W. B. Clarke also showed free gold to occur in cer-
tain granites distributed over wide areas in N.S.W. and
Vic. Also, the Vic. coal-measures contain traces of gold,
especially in the nodules of iron pyrites or mundic. The
late Cosmo Newbery found as much as a pennyweight to
the ton in the pyrites forming the so-called platinum ore
of Colao, Vic. Also, the Silurian country rock of the gold-
fields of South-east Australia is known to be thoroughly
permeated with extremely fine, almost impalpable gold.

Six saloon passengers, in no way connected with
one another in business, but each with several parcels of
gold-specimens, sailed for England in a late mailboat
from Sydney. A local shipping-agent says that that
batch makes a total of over a hundred financial mis-
sionaries who, to his own knowledge, have within the last
four months left Sydney for London on the same business
intent.

The four English-owned mines at Lucknow,
N.S.W., employ about 600 men, and have a wages-sheet
of nearly £6OOO a month. About half is represented by
the Wentworth mine, next the Aladdin (practically the
same owners), then two purely-prospecting mines, the
Wentworth Extended and the D’Arcy Wentworth. The
vigorous mining policy followed is a sad commentary on
the hand-to-mouth way this camp was worked for 30
years. When Bull gets hold c f a good mine—and he
generally has to make it good, as in this case, by his own
development—he works it for all it is worth.

A Cobar (N.S.W.) paper recently complained
about the inrush of strangers to that mining-field, and
said that “ the old business people in consequence find
themselves uncomfortably jostled.” The article con-
tinued : “ It is admittedly hard lines for men to stick by
a place through years of adversity and find those that
never sowed coming in to divide the harvest with them.”
The true t’other-sider spirit.

Jack, the boss Govt, geologist, has been over
the Palmer, Limestone, and Normanby fields. Found
people working the conglomerate on the Palmer, but “the
prospects were far from brilliant so far as immediate
results were concerned.” Mr. Jack says the reefs have
never had a fair trial, and thinks they have a future.

The prospectus of the Resolute Gold-mining Co.,
at Walhalla, Vic., is advertised in this issue. The capital
is £lO,OOO, in 40,000 ss. shares, and the vendors ask £2500
in cash and £2500 in shares. £2500 cash goes to the co.’s
credit, and £2500 worth of shares is held in reserve. The
lease is 50 acres, with a 2ft. reef, which gave 550 z. from
16 tons. Development consists of a 200 ft. tunnel, from
which a winze is being sunk.

tures of the Buchan alleged silver-mine (Vic.) are exposed,

identified the mine as one he saw 20 years ago and did not

got out, and £lOOO worth of work seems to represent
£lO,OOO expenditure.

Vic. Govt, has quietly dropped the intention to
send its Assistant-Geologist to Britain on a mine-booming
errand. Mr. Stirling, the professor in question, asked to
be allowed to withdraw from the proposed mission when
he heard how Collins - street had begun to associate
it with the name of a certain newspaper - man and
a coal-mining scheme. So that complimentary glass of
wine which £ £ Smith, Butters and Co. were to tender to
the young missionary at Melbourne Town Hall will have
to stay in bottle.

* * * *
Herr Haber, the distinguished expert and
geologist, lately reporting on Mt. Lyell properties, main-
tained a sphinx-like “no savee” when interrogated by
anxious boomsters as to his opinion of the “pups.” A
specimen. Boomster : “Well, Mr. Haber, what do you
think of the ‘Great Gone Coon Extended?’ Fine show,
eh ? ” Expert (adjusting his pince-nez and bowing) :
“ Aha ! you are then a geologist ? ” Boomster : “ Oh, no ;
merely asked out of curiosity. Don’t you think the show
a first-class one ? ” Expert: “ Aha ! you are then a mining
enquirer?” Boomster : “No, not at all, merely an out-
sider ; but I’m slightly interested in that property, and
should like to know your opinion of it.” Expert : “Aha !
you are then an Australian nateeve?” [Here the native
falls down dead.]
• * * *
The Curtin-Davis Proprietary (Tas.) seems to be
the show silver-and-copper mine of the west coast. Lode
small, but tunnels driven into the mountain prove its
permanency, and, in places, marvellous richness. No
stoping has yet been done, although considerable stacks
of ore have been raised, some of which is valued at £392
per ton. Principal shareholders, Knox and Schlapp, and
other pioneers of Mt. Lyell. It is only reasonable to sup-
pose that other good silver and copper deposits should
exist in the district, and more reasonable that the bosses
of the parent mine should have a call on the best.
One of the natural qualifications of a gold-pro-
ducing country is that it should produce gold. Westralia
is a great gold-producing country according to the boom-
sters of the indigenous press—therefore, it ought to pro-
duce much gold, and go on doing it. The statistics pub-
lished by Melb. Mint for the first nine months of ’96, com-
pared with ’95, are not such as will get scare-headings in
the journals of the Gilded West. For three-quarters of
’95 the product was 160,9490 z.; for the present year the
figures fall to 152,2600 z.
This is a big drop, but considered as a drop it
does not really mean much. Tire Bulletin is not anxious
to take any unfair deduction from the figures—so the
jaded journalist out West, who turns out copy (when,
according to his own specifications, he ought to be turn-
ing in millions) will recognise the true inwardness of the
figures when he reads this par. The drop in the product
is accounted for by the drop in alluvial. Reef gold has
(for W.A.) considerably increased. A year ago the banks
were bringing parcels of alluvial gold—these are now
scarce. In their place come cakes and ingots. This
aspect of the question is admittedly healthy. How
healthy? The Gilded West has the capitalisation of the
Rand—the Rand produces as much in a month as the
G.W. in a year. Even the Rand yield is a disappointment
to investors—and in Paris there has been a slump in
African mining stocks.
Westralia must produce ten times as much gold
as it is getting now before the promises of its boomsters
begin to be realised. How long, oh Western Press, how
long?
* * * *
Cabled that a protest has been raised on behalf
of £25,600,000 of Bull’s capital invested in Westralia
against the Menzies railway starting from Southern Cross.
“ It was maintained that the route would be injurious to
mining —and that the proposal of the Govt, indicated that
they were regardless of the interests of capital invested.”
'What a revelation ! Was not the railway to Southern
Cross itself built in the interests of a certain few persons
in Perth who found they had made a mad investment in
mines too poor to pay, and of course tried to pull out
with a railway for which Bull found the money ? But for
Coolgardie, Bull and the boom, the railway would have
gone to the devil or the receiver. Who, own the mines,
poor as they are at Southern Cross, also the town and
surrounding" country? Does not the devil protect his
own? The writer suspected that the W.A. Govt, would
take full advantage of the squabble between Coolgardie
and Kalgoorlie, and start the line from Southern Cross.
But if Bull kicks really hard, Forrest must kne; which
is better than crawling, anyhow. Next move, Bull will
gently force the W.A. Govt, to build, or allow others to
build, the line from Esperance to Coolgardie.

Wired from Perth : “The Govt., after careful

starting-point for the railway to Menzies.” Bull has
kicked.

“Cephalos” writes from Roeburn, W. A., con-
cerning a mine there recently described as having a 14ft.
reef of lOoz. stone. It is, he says, a 24in. buck reef, with
not a particle of gold visible. When discovered it was
simply a pocket of boulders showing a few specks, the
stone assaying 17dwts. At 20ft. the buck reef came in
and remains.

The optimistic J. S. Read, broker, Perth :
“ Business anything but good, buyers scarce, attributable
to a variety of causes : enormous number of companies on
the market, small amount available in many cases for
working capital, necessitating regular calls, and the weak-
ness of the London market at the present moment. No
less than 49 co’s. made calls during the months. The
hundreds of miners working and producing not a color
of course has nothing to do with it. “The Nil Desper-
andum shares4n a London co. of £90,000 are well-worth
buying at about 255. There is a huge lode formation some
40ft. across which shows by panning 9dwt. per ton, and by
amalgamation process a fraction over loz. per ton.” Who
wants to sell ? That is the question. Mr. Reid should
know that no stone that only runs 9dwts. by panning will
go over an oz. by amalgamation.

* * * *
A feeling gains .ground throughout the Westra-
lian fields that the coming Christmas exemptions will see
a united' effort made by the big bosses to reduce wages.
Already the miners are preparing for the fight. Their
game is to fight against the two-months exemption usually
allowed. It is seldom availed of by the gold-producing
mines—which, unfortunately, do not number one in 100
of the total. How would it be to agree to take £1 a week
less to work where there was gold and demand £1 more
for the pain of working in mines having no chance of ever
getting any? Exemption means sparring for wind.

Jumping-frog Premier Reid, after warning the
Hawkesbury (N.S. WJnatives that protectionism increased
the number of competitors in any protected industry, and
so lowered prices (he had just before then been swearing
that protectionism raised prices by 50 percent.), began to
quote from Vic. statistics to show that protectionism in
Victoria had decreased the number of protected competi-
tors there !

London cable: “The W.A. Land Co. has
agreed to sell the Great Southern Railway to W.A. Govt,
for £1,100,000.” At the start this was a “ wild-cat ” rail-
way, and the “ wild-cat ” and other mines of the country
saved it from insolvency. Before the goldfields broke out
it was losing over £5OO a week. No wonder Albany put
up a monument to its pioneer, Anthony Hordern. Are
the millions of, acres given for building the line included
in the bargain ?

r * * * *
Quite a considerable percentage of Melbourne’s
male population must have a notion of clearing out to
South Africa, judging from the financial success of little
“ Uitlander” Sampson’s lectures on the subject. Samp-
son holds forth, with the assistance of dissolving views, at
suburban halls, and young men put themselves consider-
ably out of their way in order to hear his remarks for a
shilling or a florin. Sampson’s advice to those about to
emigrate is “ Don’t.” He talks fluently from a good
knowledge of South African cities, and has undoubtedly
given pause to many of his hearers.
N.S. W. Premier Reid argues that Protectionism
creates and develops “sweating,” because Melbourne
tailoresses are suffering as awful horrors as their kind
in the “ sweating” dens of London. “ Then, of course,
London is under Protectionism, Mi*. Reid ?” “ Oh, dear,
no; London is Freetrade.” Which shows that “sweating,”
like measles, is not confined to any caste. But, as with
measles, the better-doctored rich patients suffer less
severely, so the better-doctored patients under Protec-
tionism stand the best chance of resistance and early re-
oovery. , * . *
Some of the big Sydney firms this year refused
to contribute anything towards the Eight-Hour demon-
stration. The chief reasons they allege (amongst them-
selves) are that the Eight-Hour committee has a balance
in hand of £5OOO (Tiie Bulletin does not know whether
this is true, or nearly true); that this sum is to he spent
on useless bricks and mortar in connection with the new
Trades Hall; and that the Eight-Hour agents are still
“on the cadge.” Notice also that Attorney-General
“ Jack ” Want has been breathing desultory threatening®
about putting a stop to Eight-Hour “ art unions.

The Co-operative Bakery, Richmond, started
some months ago, with Rev. Chas. Strong as president,

ployes, notwithstanding a tramway boss’s pointed advice
to them “ not to have anything to do with it.”

* * * *
Vic. railway-manager Mathieson proposes to
cease running trains on a number of non-paying branch
lines (32 neither pay expenses nor interest, and 14 don t
pay interest) and work them as horse-tramways. To tnig
end he will construct a cinder-track between the rails.
Should this method not pay, he will doubtless in winter
spread mud, and in summer dust, on the track to a depth
of 3ft. and work the lines by means of Cobb’s coaches and
bullock-waggons.

* * * *
A grave abuse in matters re letters of adminis-
tration has been revealed in connection with a recent
Vic. probate. The only security that parties interested
in intestacies have against the mopping-up of the. pro-
perties by the administrators is the bond into which a
surety has to enter on the granting of the letters of
administration. But it seems it is quite possible, and has
happened, that a surety worth, say, £lOOO, may tie surety
for ten or more estates each worth £lOOO. So that the
beneficiaries in nine or more estates, many of them infants
of tender years, will have, in the event of one breach of
trust in one estate, nothing but the caprice of a step-
mother administratrix between themselves and starvation.

Dr. Abrahams, the Melb. rabbi, in a much-dis-
cussed sermon, the other day, described the temperament
of the Jew as a “loving optimism”—“they make the
best of what goes on around them.” A day or two later
a well-advertised, flatfooted financier, a child of Israel,
having lent a man £3O claimed £lOO as a bonus and 10
per cent, on the debt, and secured a verdict. “ They
make the best of what goes on around them.”
* * * *
The Bananaland frozen-meat trade is in a bad
way, principally owing to the preference shown by the
British consumer for American chilled meat. The Eagle
Farm works are closed, the Townsville works are only
kept going by the tick trouble, and Lake’s Creek is losing
thousands by its frozen trade.
* * * *
The placards of a well-advertised Melb. money-
lender are being removed from the Vic. railway-stations,
where they have occupied a prominent position for some
years past. The State evidently doesn’t intend to be a
vehicle for private enterprise in that line any longer.

The irrepressible H. L. Goodman again on the
flute on the wonders of the Ore Automatic Reduction
process. “It so prepares the ore that any of the well-
known methods for the recovery of gold can be applied to
the disintegrated ore. If the cyanide or any other wet
process is used no trouble will be experienced in what ia
known as leaching, as no slimes are produced no matter
what character of ore is treated, and if ordinary amalga-
mation is used the gold is in the best possible condition
for the saving of the mercury.” Great Scot 1 It is now being
hinted that the new process is merely a preparatory thing !

Vic. Commissioner of Customs Best, in
making a presentation of a group of photos, to a
popular football captain the other day, concluded
thus : “And in presenting you with this frame of
photos., I am sure you will always look back to
the happy time you have spent with the originals
of the photos, in this frame.” But he had been
holding the wrong group, and when he presented
the captain with the photo, of a committeeman’s
wife, the audience yelled.
“Newspapers,” says Sydney Daily Tele-
graph, in advocating free newspaper postage,
“are the medium through which people keep
themselves in daily touch with the world.” The
Bible, for the matter of that, is the medium
through which people are supposed to keep them-
selves in daily touch with the next world, yet the
Bible isn’t sent free through the post. Botany
Bay taxes the Almighty, but it subsidises Mister
James Fairfax and the Crowd for whom John
Haynes cackles in the Assembly.
It was here remarked the other day that,
in the interest of the silken bonds, we should no
doubt see cinematographs of the British Royal
Family in Australia. The Bulletin now notes
that someone in London has been protesting
against the cinematoscoping of royalty and the
subsequent exhibition of the photographs, on the
ground that in one of the series the Prince of
Wales is represented scratching his royal head.
The cinematoscoper has hastened to explain that
“ the movement referred to is simply a momentary
placing of the hand to the ear, probably to brush
away an intrusive fly.” It would never do for the
Prince to publicly scratch his head. People might
think there was something in it.

Melb. Presbyterian Assembly, which finds
that in Chow and South Sea Mission work a know-
ledge of obstetrics is a necessity, howls because the
Women’s Hospital authorities will not allow their
divinity students to walk the maternity wards
with the medicals. The way of the transgressor
will be hard indeed if the Assembly gets its way.
At present the “ erring sister ” is at any rate free
from the tyranny of cant while in hospital—
doctors, medical students, and nurses are people of
the world, without snuffle or prudery—but with a
herd of pimply, white-faced youths over-running
the wards and talking about “ Sin,” while the in-
evitable W.C.T.U. harridan sniffs round in search
of the cause of the patient’s fall with corroborative
details, the outlook of the “girl who has been be-
trayed ” will be as sad as that of the fictitious
article of the drama.

The competition among clergymen at-
tached to Melb. general cemetery is one of the
most interesting developments of true religion.
Several denominations have representatives there
daily (“corpse-watching,” it is termed), and
directly the sound of wheels is heard these
gentlemen, or some of them, hurry towards
the cortege in best sprinting form, with the
hearse for winning - post and the undertaker
for judge. If the corpse is guaranteed to have
had a special brand of faith the representatives of
the other denominations retire, and the lucky man
composes his face to an expression of woe and gets
through the patter, returning to wait for another
dead-’un. If the referee declares that the de-
ceased had no particular religion, the gospel-
grinders compete as vigorously for the job as
hawkers canvass for customers at the markets on
Saturday evenings, until the planter of stiffs ends
the debate by his decision. The alleged favoritism
shown towards one particular clergyman has
given rise to suggestions that he has managed to
euchre his rivals by dividing the “ commish ” with
the corpse-carters.

More sweating ! A Melb. parson has
owned up that he receives 255. a week from a

* * * *
The Civic Club being organised in Sydney,
in imitation of the Melb. Stock Exchange Club,
already numbers nearly 700 members. It will be
a “ lunching club,” and will probably play up with
the restaurants. But why anybody should wish
to belong to a club which aims at a thousand
members or over is not very clear.
The childlike and bland Li Hung was
working for old Fatman, when they had a quarrel
over a contract for planting fruit-trees. Fatman
insisted on the agreement being carried out at a
sweating wage, and Hung reluctantly and swear-
ingly gave way and planted the orchard, but took
the precaution every night to sit up and boil the
roots first.

Southern divers brag about the Catterthuri
business as if it were something marvellous
(writes a correspondent), and speak of 26J
fathoms as record dicing. Twenty-six and a-
half fathoms is an every-day occurrence up in
the Straits, and Torresian divers occasionally
venture 30 fathoms after shell. Thirty-two
fithorns, however, spells paralysis and sudden
death.

Queensland has its weekly leper, Victoria
has its annual jury scandal. In the Mercantile
Bank business the Grand Jury had an alien con-
cealed about its person, and the result was wreck-
age. In the Abrahams case, an outsider got en-
tangled and gaoled by trying to “approach” a
juryman. In the Rapiport-Baxter fiasco the
jury supplied a subsidiary reserve scandal. Two
foremen were appointed, and if Chief-Justice
Madden had not talked straight, this pretty inci-
dent would have furnished a chance for quashing
proceedings. Now the City of Melbourne Bank
directors have a jury adventure of their own.

According to Bourke Telegraph, a mob
of 56 Chinese passed into Queensland, the other
day, via Avon Downs, being supplied with food
and rations by the cook and gardener on the
station. Inasmuch, however, as the immigrating
Chows did not report themselves to the police, the
Government and its officials took no cognisance of
this increase in the pig-tailed population. Such
invasions don’t count in the statistics.

J.D. : “ For four

a central Tasmanian
hamlet. Fourteen

under thirty years of

with babies in arms,

This to my personal

leagues away.”

Grilhooley, from the
backblocks : —“ Some-
one said you could tell
what a people were by
the songs they sing.
According to this, the
British and colonials
are all gravediggers
and undertakers.”
Pastor Fitchett’s blood-and-thunder is
affecting the cherished principles of his rev.
brethren. One of them, Parson Nisbet, of Haw-
thorn, has sermonised on the subject, confessing
that the “Heeds that Won the Empire” yarns
have roused all the “fighting” instincts of his
nature, and that notwithstanding “we say in
calmer moments that moral courage is of far
higher value than physical courage,” “we know
that nothing that we could read of moral courage
would impress us in the same way.” From this
to the canonisation of Tom Sayers, Jem Mace,
Peter Jackson, and J. L. Sullivan is but a short
step.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictiires ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *

The action of N.S.W. Public Service
Board in offering only 30s. for a female typist cer-
tainly savors of meanness. The Government
salary, as a rule, has much to do with the rate
paid by private individuals and firms, and if the
Government fix the pay at 30s. weekly, the private
employer will always cut under.

In Port Melbourne (and other places in
Victoria) when a careful parent finds that ,1ns
youngsters are not advancing in “ eddication ” as
rapidly as he would like, he calls around at the
State school and “plugs” the first teacher that
comes to hand. Another version of payment by
results.

Brisbane City Surveyor recently visited
Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide to gain infor-
mation about abattoir matters. Later, a N.S.W.
Select Committee picnicked to Brisbane, Mel-
bourne and Adelaide to enquire about—abattoirs.
By and bye, Adelaide commissioners will fly
around Melbourne, Sydney and Brisbane to find
out about—abattoirs. Then a Melbourne delega-
tion will “do” Adelaide, Sydney and Brisbane,
with a view to discovering fresh things about —
abattoirs.
* * * *
The Sydney newspaper controversy be-
tween ritualistic and evangelical C. of E. parsons,
which was first started because Bishop Smith
attended a nonconformist missionary meeting,
clearly reveals the fact that the S.M. Herald is
the episcopalian parson’s organ of ventilation.
Knee-breeches and shovel-hats don’t recognise the
D.T., apparently.
It is a queer thing, by the way, that to obtain
literary rank in the S.M. Herald office you must
have been educated in a dim religious light of
some sort—as a Wesleyan or Baptist or Congrega-
tionalist parson, or even as a Christian brother,
for they aren’t prejudiced. You need not be holy,
or even a believer—they aren’t particular about
that; it seems to be only the clerical stiffness they
want, not necessarily the piety.

The definition of domestic servant in the
proposed Vic. Servants Registry-offices Act in-
cludes “lady helps and governesses.” Under
woman-suffrage, the drafter of that provision
would probably be sentenced to be flayed alive.
A demand is arising in Maoriland that if
the Bill for Pawing the Girl under 1G
who is out after 10 p.m. becomes law,
policewomen shall he appointed to carry
out its provisions. The policewoman
seems to be inevitable in such a case.
No decent community can give the
male constable extensive powers to
paw nice-looking girls in the “ exe-
cootion of his dooty,” seeing that
a policeman is only human, and it
is pretty dark after 10 p.m., and
few people are about, and some
girls like policemen, and police-
men like some girls, and a man
who is legally authorised to take
pretty girls home in a fatherly
manner for a distance of per-
haps three miles along a road
which is probably rather lone-
some is exposed to tempta-
tions that aren’t adequately
paid for at 455. a week.
The policewoman, on the
other hand, would rush the
erring sister home by the neck
in a purely business fashion, and
instead of lingering on the way she
would be more likely to belt the suspect
with her truncheon to make her go faster, and
deliver her in an exhausted condition through
hard travelling. The shortest way to settle the
relative merits of the case would be to leave six
girls, who lived at exactly the same distance from
town, but after ten, and get policemen to capture
three and she-constables the other three, and see
which sex made the best average time at getting
its girl home.
* * * •
The suggestion emanating from prominent
Sydney architects that the Government should re-
sume. as a site for the new Parliamentary build-
ings, part of Upper Fort-street, Princes street,
and the contiguous locality, merits consideration.
Property there has receded and looks as if it will
continue to recede in value, and the trail of the
Mongolian and other aliens marks the corkscrew
thoroughfares. Grimy huts and festering rookeries
vie with each other for condemnation. A Sydney
resident, writing to an English magazine in 1811,
said: “ Every evening the beautyand fashion of
our young settlement may be seen promenading
that delightful part of the city called The ‘Rocks.’”
But the Rocks have lost their good character since
then. The building of anew Parliament House
there would necessitate a general cleaning up and
an extensive rebuilding of the slum region, and
any scheme which involves such a cleaning up has
much to recommend it.

Weird story from a Melb. correspondent
to The Bulletin
The W.C.TrU.’s City Mission people tried to persuade
three female vagrants, who were in the habit of dossing
nightly in the doorway of an Ease Melb. brewery, to put
up at one of the “ Homes.” The invitation was emphati-
cally declined, as they would have to forego their morning
draught of the warm nectar brought freely to them by the
drain leading from the brewery, and which they said they
found “ so refreshing.”
A Melb. blasphemer to The Bulletin
A local paper some time ago described the sumptuous
dresses of the ladies at a garden-party given by Bishop
Goe. How would this read :—“Miss Magdalen, pale-blue
soft silk, with deep basque fringe of gold, handsome gold
embroidery trimming, gold-and-blue bonnet; Mrs. Mary
Joseph (in Mrs. Goe’s dress), royal blue silk, with deep
basqued bodice edged with black lace. Miss Pharisee
would be bound to turn up at a Jerusalem garden-party
of the year one in a pale-grey demi-trained crbpe, with
sash of shot silk, black hat with pink roses. Nor would
Mrs. Sadducee have been lacking in terracotta and cream
fancy silk, trimmed with passementerie, foulard, guipure
and fichu. Mrs. Widow’s Mite would come up in a hand-
some cream faille veiled in black lace, black lace bonnet,
with gold ornaments. Miss Martha and Miss Mary would
respectively be in green silk gown, small fancy hat, and
heliotrope cloth, trimmed with gold. Yes, altogether
they keep up the customs. Fancy poor old Lazarus wan-
dering on Bishop Goe’s lawn, where he would find Dives,
of course, and all the Miss Diveses. Funny, you know,
but we must have pabulum. Keep up your funny reli-
gion.”

A Quakers' marriage—there was one in
Sydney last week—consists largely of eloquent in-
tervals of profound silence. The bride is then
silent for the first and last times of her life.

Spagnoletti : “Thumb-nailed a matter of
ponder in Melb. to-day. A respectable funeral,
of a well-to-do person, trotting briskly to the
necropolis—not because it was late, but because
deceased didn’t want the humbug of a funeral
pace. Next the undertaker with the gin-red nose,
who blacks himself all over like the conscientious
Othello, will get a cheerful wainscot-painted
brown hearse. Or, how would a yellow hearse
with red wheels look ? ”
One of the most loathsome things you ever see
in the daily papers— The Bulletin has seen it
about a dozen times this last fortnight—is: “ the
coffin was of polished oak, with heavy silver
mountings.” Think of people being proud of
their coffin! As well be proud of your cancer.
“ The deceased gentleman died slowly of a most
beautiful malignant tumor, now on view at the
family residence.” It would be just as seemly as
the ioathsome, but now conventional under-
taker’s-cam-family puff about the heavy mount-
ings of the oaken corruption-case. As well might
the night-owl boast in print of the beauty of the
enamel which covered the hideousness of her
eczema.

CONFESSION.
Confession's a weakness, confession’s a curse,
Confession’s an absence of sense ;
If Accused’s case is bad, ’twill become all the worse
If he’s touched with such soft penitence.
If you own to a shark of “the Devil’s Brigade ”
That you “ did it,’’ as Dean did to M ,
He may tell some superior shark in the trade,
And "the latter may—Well, there y<m are !
Heart-broken, if you are imprisoned in quod,
And you feel superstitious a bit,
And reveal your remorse to a Chieftain of God,
He may deem it his ‘ ‘ duty ” to split.
So, never put trust in a “ parson by trade,”
And lie like the Fiend to “ the Devil’s Brigade.
This gives teetotal folk the gripes ;
We’ve heard that snakes abound in “ swipes,”
By will of the Creator ;
But finding snakes in water-pipes
Is dead agen all natur !
Oh, Adelaideans, we entreat
You, learn to take your serpents neat.
Dipso.

At a recent Vic. agricultural show, while
the milking was going on for Gov. Brassey’s medal
for the best butter-making cow, one of the stewards
noticed cream dropping from the sleeve of an at-
tendant on one of the cows. A search revealed in
the man’s sleeve a baby’s bottle containing Hlb.
of cream with an indiarubber tube attach, d,
through which the cream was dropping into the
milking-bucket.

Under the S.A. Electoral Act it is neces-
sary for candidates at Parliamentary elections to
file, within a few weeks of the return of the writ,
a statement of their expenses, which are limited
by Statute. The intention was to make the
matter public, but such heavy fees are charged for
inspecting the documents that it would have cost
about .£l5 to obtain the figures in re the Assembly
election last April. To get over this difficulty a
motion for a Parliamentary return was recently
tabled in the Assembly, but the Govt, opposed it
and it was defeated. Then the democrats in the
Council took the matter up, and, as they were not
personally interested, a majority there ordered the
figures to be laid on the table of that Chamber,
but they have not been printed. The Adelaide
press refuses to publish the returns, as the pro-
prietors wish to compel their advertisement in the
ordinary way and immediately after the close of
the elections, so that the constituents of a candi-
date may act as “private detectives” concerning
the correctness of his sworn figure.-,. The penalty
for exceeding the limit laid down or for furnishing
incorrect returns is heavy, and includes, among
other things, exclusion from Parliament for a
term covering the next general election. The
Bulletin here makes known figures which, so far,
have not seen the light in S.A. The letter “M ”
stands for Ministerialist, and “O ” for Opposition,
and in the following table are given all the
amounts over A100:
W. L. Ware, Sturt (O.), defeated, £4Ol ; A. L. Harrold,
North Adelaide (O.), elected, £344 ; J. Darling, East
Torrens (O.), elected, £317 ; W. E. Stock, Sturt (O.), de-
feated, £313; J. Riddoch. Victoria (O.), defeated, £248;
T. Bruce, East Adelaide (O.), defeated, £216; Jenkin
Coles, Light (Speaker), elected, £202 ; J. T. Soheatk, East
Adelaide (M.), elected, £175 ; W. H. Duncan, Onkapa-
ringa (O.), elected, £165 ; J. G. Jenkins, Sturt (Works
Minister), elected, £155; B. Moulden, Mount Barker (O.),
defeated, £128; E. W. Hawker, Stanley (O.), defeated,
£ll5 ;W. T. Mortlock, Flinders (O.), elected, £111; John
Downer, Barossa (O.), elected, £lO5 ; J. Vardon, West
Torrens (O), defeated, £104; W. Griffiths, N. Territory
(M.), elected, £lO2 ; A. Catt, Gladstone (China Com.),
elected, £100; J. A. M‘Lachlan, Victoria (O.), defeated,
£lOO.

Hourigan, £l9; McGillwray, £l4 ; Poynton, £l7 ; Price,
£45 ; Roberts, £35; and Wood, £l7.
In every instance, too, they represent big districts,
being mostly returned by the city electorates.
The cost to Ministers was also comparatively
small considering the virulence with which they
were opposed. The figures are :
Kingston, £29 ; Holder, £49 ; Cockburn, £93; Jenkins,
£155 ; Gillen (dead), £18; O’Loghlen, £46.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “Babeskin” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. S. Harris, 78 King-street, Sydney.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ rv ~ Sydney, Oct. 13, 1896.
Mr Dear Myee—
Guvmentouse garden-party guests, on Tuesday
°L: • * week ; were °f different ranks. To the
official eye, vice-regal invitations are like the Vic.
Oovt. stamp on the exported butter-boxes : they
cover graded qualities of the product. The expert
can distinguish at once between “private dinner ”
ball or garden-party,” and “ garden-party only ”
folk. Indeed, some of our Very Nicest would feel
uneasy if they knew how they are all ticked-off
Visitors Book thus: “Mrs. Dash, rich
« at°w, daughter engaged to Mr. Blank, R.N.”
Mr. Asterisk, station heavily mortgaged, five
daughters all out, garden-parties only.” “Miss
Hyphen, young, pretty, large dinners.” In the
local Doomsday Book, one star differeth very
much from another in glory. Last week’s revel
swallowed pretty nearly everybody on the list—in-
cluding a large, black, thin clerical contingent,
who all looked as if a three months’ dietary of
stou t and oysters at somebody else’s expense would
do them a world of good.
■f ashions were as varied as their wearers. From
the lady in the gown from gay Paree with six
godets in her skirt (I counted ’em) to a survival
from the hoary past who wandered around in
ringlets and bottle-green gloves (and other things,
i «? urse > fuere were exemplars of every style, in-
cluding sheath-skirts, bishops, and other ana-
chronisms. Among the throng the really well-
dressed women stood out—on the gravel-path
mostly for a 13-yard skirt is lost in a crowd.
Oolors were brilliant and atrocious 9 and there was
as much tinting on the faces as on the gowns.
15ut, then, what is one to do when the shade of the
season is green a color which turns nine women
out of ten a ghastly yellow ?
One very snappy costume in the fashionable hue
was topped off effectively with a whipped-cream
ettect in white puffings and bonnet with rose-pink
ribbons ; another green frock had cerise-velvet
fixings and an expensive appearance. There were
acres of grass-lawn usefully bringing up to date
last year’s soiled silk frocks. A military band on
the lawn, with feather-dusters in their hats,
mingled Wagner and comic-opera attractively.
Refreshments were vigorously struggled for in a
tent and on the verandah, where enthusiasts glued
their noses to the dining-room windows and
criticised the vice-regal table-decorations. The
preliminary hand-shake was performed under the
auspices of an outraged butler, who cried indig-
nantly at intervals, “no cards, please,” to those
over-anxious strugglers who insisted on thrusting
their pasteboards on him. Lady Hampden,
plainly dressed in black, suffered the grasp of
thousands with well-simulated gladness for hours,
but his Ex. and daughters collapsed early and
glided unobtrusively from the greetings.
The Y.M.C.A. hall seems to be saturated with
San key, and the enskied and painted choir-chairs in
the organ loft, when empty, don’t look much less
wooden than when their usual occupiers sit up in
them. But sometimes classical music is let loose
there, as on last Wednesday night at the Rivers All-
press violin recital, and then the Cultured attend
and, reading their annotated programmes, feel thrills
and raptures accordingly. Mr. Allpress was very
much applauded, especially for his “ Trillo del
Diavolo,” the comb and curl-paper effects in the
trillos ” being particularly Satanic. The violin
as a solo instrument needs to be played with a
broad quill pen, so to speak : with a pin-pointed
mb it becomes suggestive of the consequences of
eating unripe fruit. Mr. Allpress and Miss
Lottie Hyam gave the audience a great treat,
however, m Creig’s picturesque Sonata in C
minor. Miss V. Young, in a pretty white satin
-frock sang with applause, but she hardly made
her ‘ Summer Night ” warm enough.
f-fjris show up well on such an occasion
as the Hospital sports at Moore Park last Satur-
day, but they don’t begin to understand how to
boom a charity f6te. They were content to help
along the good cause by drifting about with
flowers, or unobtrusively vending ices in tents to
such as penetrated under the canvas of the Nicest
m search of refreshments. Perhaps they con-
sidered that the common herd got their shillings-
worth by gazing at pretty faces and high-priced
frocks, or perhaps it is bad form here to coax and
wheedle the multitude until all its coin is ulti-
mately transferred to the charity’s coffers. The
women of the sister city down below recognise
that at a fair they must hustle like mosquitoes or
the ( cause ” will get badly left, so they energeti-
cally rush into their clients enough weak tea to
float an ironclad, pin vast floral knots into reluct-
ant buttonholes, and—well, they boom the show.
The frocks at Moore Park were decidedly fetch-
ing. Post race-meeting gatherings are always
well-groomed, because of the desire to get all pos-
sible wear out of the dresses now the great occasion
is over. Government House was soberly clad.
Lady ll.’s willowy figure looked its best in black ;
her daughters, muslin-clad, adorned an aristocratic
tent. The entire list of this season’s novelties
was piled upon one costume built of Chin 6 silk
grass lawn, honiton appliqu6, lace-edged chiffon
and spangle trimming. Another ideally-cool grey
French muslin seemed to reduce the temperature
for yards in its vicinity, and the sleeves were
poems. But blue and purple on an aggressive hat
were as badly-assorted a couple as the “ Marseil-
laise ” and the Russian national hymn played
alongside each other, and made the aesthetic ob-
server as ill as the above combination of tunes did
Czar Nicholas. Talking of national anthems, when
‘ God Save ” speeded the departing vice-royalties
from the ground on Saturday, not a man lifted his
hat and not a woman arose from her seat.
Australians are not irreverent in essentials, but
.young yet to have had time to grow

n anrma, l meeting of the local branch of the
G.h.fe>. was worked up most successfully and
energetically by the secretary, Mine, de Montemas,
who captured many bishops and other notables.
A sermon was preached by Bishop Green on the
beauty of lowliness and submission. An ob-
ject of the G.F.S. is to keep up the serf
idea and form a servant class on English lines
—domestics perfectly submissive and automatic
in manner, and indifferent if not hostile to
their employers at heart. The breezy inde-
pendence of the Australian “ general ” who works
k e ten. trained domestics does the family-
washing in the morning, hands visitors in, neatly-
capped-and-aproned in the afternoon, and gives
her mistress friendly advice on all matters from
babies to bicycling, is utterly unknown in a stiffly
starched ’Ome family. And perhaps we shall
only begin to know her value here when we lose
her through growing stylishness and unsym-
pathetic treatment, and begin to give her a card
of merit instead of a new dress.
One night recently at the Criterion there was a
large d.c. party organised by an up-to-date Darling
Foint lady, who numbers many clerics among her
relatives. Included in the party were two
bishops, an archdeacon, and three curates, all
thoroughly enjoying the perplexities of “A
Mother of Three ’ !
The horrors of the coming Sydney Summer are,
I am told, to be mitigated by a real ice rink a la
.Niagara m London, and the Foie Nord, in gay
Paree. These two resorts are still the haunt of
fashion. A new praze of that sort usually lasts ,
with the Very Nicest for two seasons, and after
that Arry and his pals crowd in, and the upper
ten withdraw. The temperature of the room is to
be lowered and raised at will. It will look some-
what abnormal to see sassiety shivering in furs in
January and February, and there will no doubt
be some terrible falls at first, for real-ice skating
differs as much from roller skating as cycling does
“ om snow-shoeing. Falling will be so cool,
though, that the prostrate ones will be reluctant
to get up again. They will imitate the poor polar-
bear in Melb. Zoo in the scorching days of last
bummer : when he was handed a large block of ice
to lick, he sat down on it until it melted all away.
It pays to be a Westralian barmaid. I hear of
the waitresses in Kalgoorlie making vast sums
weekly, and ending by marrying some English-
man with a pile and a handle to his name. Not
long ago, an enterprising Western widow, with a
grown-up family and peroxided tresses, led to the
altar a youth of 22 summers, who now considers
himself a tremendously lucky man in acquiring
a wife and family in so short a time. In this
case, the bride still consents to swell the
joint income by serving behind the bar; she
wears her hair down and sports girlish muslin
frocks, and the diggers flock around and adore.
Barmaids in the West, however, suffer the penalty
of fame. Whenever a dramatic co. strikes the
Fields, the Hebes and their escorts, who form the
d.c. audience, have to stand a running fire of chaff
from the “boys” in the back seats. “Hullo,
Maud ; what price the opera-cloak ? ” &c.
lam told of a recent Kalgoorlie show : “ Uncle
Tom ’ was the play, Hosea Easton the pious hero,
little Eva a boy, aged probably 20. The train
with the_ co. on board broke down, so the show
was late in beginning ; the gods, all smart Sydney
and Melbourne youths, hurried things up by shout-
ing the camel-drivers’cry, ‘ Hooshtah,’ and made
every lydy blush furiously by loudly greeting her
by name as she entered the hall.”
In the “ highly commended ” class is the career
of a recently-deceased Mudgee lady, aged 80. “As
a young woman she began work on the Goulburn
as the first free-selector ; single-handed she worked
her selection, doing the ring-barking and fencing
herself, and it is said by those who knew her that
she was quite equal to doing the work of two men.
By her energy and frugality she made a small
fortune out of this selection.” There is real solid
luck in this for a woman. If she has twice the
energy and saving power of a man and has the
fortune to catch a good selection before she is 50,
and slaves on it for 30 or 40 years, she can hope
to lay up her modest pile by the time she is as old
as Gladstone. And her reward is an obituary par.
praising her sterling qualities. But not her
smartness, observe, my sisters. No really clever
woman ever worked hard. The sharpest feminine
brains lurk inside the frizzled heads of the dress-
maker’s dummy-women, who have found out the
trick of making other people work for them. Just
ponder on these facts, my ardent young friends
who are scorning delights and living laborious
days in the hope of getting B.A. degrees and
other trifles. It is Amaryllis in the shade who
has the best time in this world—and don’t vou
forget it.
Musical composers sometimes find it hard to get
appropriate somnolent verse to wed to their in-
spired strains. Goring Thomas has had to alter a
word or two in Le Gay Brereton’s lines on ‘ ‘ Sleep ”to
set them successfully to a tuneful, croonful lullaby.
Compositions to hand (some of them are not more
musical than a tea-tray falling down-stairs) in-
clude L. A. Alexander’s “Pretoria” waltz, already
popular and selling well, and a “Sailor’s Serenade,”
by a brother and sister, Yardley.
Gov. Hampden, who, as a rule, drives about
everywhere in a rather old-maidish little phseton,
turned out in style for the opening of the Artists'
Exhibition on Monday afternoon in an open
landau. Carriage etiquette is suffering changes
in all directions. The bike has killed it probably,
along with the chaperon.
After the Gov. had pronounced his blessing over
the Art Society’s show on Monday last and reck-
lessly squandered some part of his hard-earned
screw in Tombola tickets, the horde of smart folk
present eagerly rushed the refreshment buffet for
tea and ices, and the nicely-spread table proved by
far one of the most popular exhibits during the
afternoon. Woman as a worker clambered con-
spicuously to the top in this very fascinating
show, but as a subject of beauty she seems to be
taking a day off. Indeed, hardly one pretty face
glances out from the vast acreage of canvas.
One nude study shows a Potteresque slightness
about the shoulders and arms, and radiantly-pretty
Miss Eva Bryan loitered inconsiderately round the
displayed attempts to fix her unusual charms with
charcoal on cardboard, and showed how futile
said attempts had turned out. Mrs. Mary Stod-
dard’s portrait of Lady Hampden shows Her Ex.
with her most sugary expression turned on full,
and the demure black silk and white fichu of the
“pretty” school. The spreading girth of the
Premier has received unnecessarily faithful treat-
ment from Gordon Coutts, who, in a fine portrait,
religiously works in every inch of vest and frock-
coat. It would have been much kinder to the
“ subject,” one would think, to have tapered the
figure gracefully off somewhere about the second
button.
The friends of Madame Henry, who for some
years has run the Cercle Litteraire Frangais, are
arranging to give her a little benefit concert and
dance in the Oddfellows’ Hall, Pitt-street, next
Tuesday evening. The dance is held out as a bait
to induce unmusical subscribers to face the concert
to the bitter end, and will be tripped to the strains
of original waltzes played and composed by Misses
Fitzstubbs, Blackston, and Summerbell.
The opening scene of “ A Gilded Fool” at the
Lyceum gives an enlightening glimpse of the inner
workings of bachelor apartments run on several
thousand annual “darlars.” Ghauncey Short’s
elegant flat, with its delicate heliotrope silken
hangings and luxurious fixings, is an appropriate
setting for the much-gilt youth in roseate pyjamas.
The hero, by the wav, discards in Sydney the
Japanese dressing-gown in which Melb. criticism
compelled him to emerge from bed ; when he dons
his terra-cotta vest and cast-iron creased clothes,
he is quite a perfect “willieboy.” Also the fur-
niture wreckage looks convincing enough that he
has made a night of it, while the band sympa-
thetically plays the curtain up with “Oh, what a
difference in the morning ! ” Nat Goodwin’s
Ghauncey is, to me, an exquisite and artistic bit
of acting, and there is a familiar air of financial
smash about the whole piece which ought to make
it catch on better than it does.
Maxine Elliott, as Margaret Ruthven, wears
satisfactory but not startling gowns ; she has a
grey poplin with a spider-crawley bodice in black
and white and fur hem-border, hat of pink chip
and black ostrich mounts ; also, a graceful evening
garb of yellow satin with black velvet cloak.
Estelle Mortimer, as Miss Jessica, wears a hand-
some flowered silk and arranges green ribbon
bands round the waist and shoulders in a cunning
way to set off a full figure. Miss Emelie Melville
has hardly physique enough to fill the bill as the
massive Maxine’s mother, and her pretty little
bird-like airs make her look rather like a dilapi-
dated linnet.
The skipper of a certain Govt, steamer believed
m punctuality. Once his wife was going with
him on a coastal excursion ; he told her to be
down on the wharf at 5 p.m. The lady promised
accordingly, and appeared with all her bandboxes
at about two minutes past the time. The steamer
had cast off her lines and was moving away, and
the captain bowed politely from the bridge, and
taking off his cap waved “ goodbye.” Philosophi-
cally packing her Gladstone into the cab again,
the lady went off home, as if she were quite used
to little things like that.

“Guvmentouse has broken out into a rash of
private theatricals, which threatens to become
chronic. The trial gallop was reverently gazed
upon by some hundreds of State school teachers,
hospital nurses and other mortals of no import-
ance, but a second performance on the Monday
was submitted to a crowd of the Elect. Sassiety
has smiled on sadder shows than this. The oft-
told tale of ‘Nan. the Good-for-Nothing,’ gave
H.E.’s histrionics a chance' of emulating the
Lower Orders with considerable attention to
detail. Miss Street, in the name part, was dirty
and untidy enough to suggest the most uncared-
for gel ’ in Christendom. She had a great
show of huge shabby boots and not over-clean
white stockings ; and she ultimately cleaned
up her complexion with the sincerity of a Betsy
Jane scrubbing the front verandah after a
dust-storm. Aide-de-camp Shaftesbury looked
the carpenter to the life. A second piece,
Duchess of Bays water and Co.,’reproduced the
aspiring earl in the form of a duke who acts like a
commercial ‘ drummer ’ of the most pushing type.
“The trail of vice-regal patronage has at last
extended from the public entertainment to the
private party. A Melb. lady prints ‘ln the dis-
tinguished presence of His Excellency the Go-
vernor’ over invitations to her At Home. The
apex of snobbishness, or the very ground-floor of
humility ? Does the hostess want to rub it into
the minds of her guests that her particular little
social show is going to be made a head taller than
any other by the presence of the vice-regals ; or
does she rate herself and her hospitality at so low
a figure that she could not expect sassiety to honor
her unless she took the same precaution as the
chanty entrepreneur and first got his Ex.’s pro-
mise to attend? One of these alternatives must
De selected. You get your card of invitation and
take your choice.

Remedy for Poverty.
Philanthropic men and women are theorising,
and trying to explain and modify the evils of
poverty : and yet, in spite of the almost miracui
ous increase of the “loaves and fishes,” due to
modern invention ; in spite of the advantages ot
education, and advanced civilization; poverty, a»
a pestering disease, as a threatening cloud, holds
fast upon us. Why with all our charity, with ah
our noble impulses, with all our mighty natipnai
progress, why should there be so many helpless-
many even starving ?
The cause of general poverty is not in that there
is a deficiency, but because too large a percentage
of the human race are over-burdened with care .
bound and held down by infirmities and disease,
rendering them unable to hold their own in the
struggle for life and predominance. Thousands ot
poor mortals are suffering to-dav because m
younger days, or in the prime of life, they Avert
handicapped by pain and disease. Little—seem
ingly harmless —symptoms neglected ; occasional
aches and pains, bilious attacks, or disturbance of
the stomach after meals, were allowed to increase
in frequency until they increased by their own
momentum —for habits of disease are creases in
the body often made as thoughtlessly as a fold in a
newspaper, easily smoothed and remedied at first;
neglected, they rapidly develope from ftmctionai
to organic disease, leaving the victim “ a hopeless
invalid.” If your liver (by occasional giddiness,
nausea, periodical headaches, or unnatural feelings
of melancholy) tries plainly to tell you it is over-
burdened, helpless, inactive, and weak, listen .to
its warnings and enliven it to its normal activity
by taking‘Warner’s Safe Cure, which for years
has successfully stood the severest tests by those
who have tried it for torpidity of the liver and the
weaknesses developing therefrom.
Mrs. E. Hallowes, of Bourke, N.S.W., gives he.-
experience with this medicine in these words: _“ 1
have used Warner’s Safe Cure for liver troubles
with good results. On one occassion my liver war.
very bad. I gave Warner’s Safe Cure a trial. It
not only relieved me at once, but ultimately oom
pletely cured me, and I have not had the slightest
bilious attack since.”

The foundation - stone of St. Killian’s
(Bendigo) R.G. Cathedral was laid the other day.
Nearly the whole of the funds for the erection of
the fane are provided by the estate of that acute
business man, Dean Backhaus, whose dexterity in
the acquirement of Bendigo corner lots passed
into a proverb. When the long-haired old Ger-
man died probate was paid on <£loo,ooo, and
under the will this sum was to be placed at
interest and allowed to accumulate for a quarter
of a century, the gross amount to be used for the
erection of the cathedral now begun. Dr. Back-
hans was noted in the Roaring Days for his
remarkable medical and surgical skill, and in the
-old times saved the life of many an injured miner.
Rumored that David Syme is helping the
comps, displaced by the linotype to get out an
evening paper.
The four Aus. provinces with untitled
Premiers —N.S.W,, Vic., S.A., and M.L. —have
each a knighted Speaker.
Judge Hartley Williams, of Victoria, was
53 Oct. 15, Judge Innes, of N.S.W., 62 on Oct. 16,
and Wrixon, M.L.C., of Vic., will be 57 Oct. 18.
Premier Kingston boasts that he works 13
hours a day seven days a week, and never suffers
from overwork. He is an abstainer, non-smoker,
eats little, walks much, and says political life has
never given him a day’s illness. And the greatest
of all these secrets is—eat little.
Judge A’Beckett growled about the
crowded state of Melb. Supreme Court during
the City Bank trials. “It couldn’t be
worse,” he said, “if we gave away free
drinks.” “ Well,” said the genial Purves,
“ we have the bar here.”
The basis of a well-known “Banjo”
ballad appears to be the alleged fact that
when Charlie Kellow, Vic. cyclist, was
mining at Day Dawn, W.A., in ’94, he struck
the township one day when in funds and,
entering the only pub., asked all on the pre-
mises to “join him.” Everybody complied
save a giant Afghan, who was tethering a
gaunt camel to the verandah-post. “ Come
on, Abdullah,” called Kellow, “what are
you going to have?” The pious Mussulman
crossed himself, or did whatever is the Maho-
medan equivalent, and murmured “La Allah
il Allah. Me no drink. But his face
brightened—“ me poo’ camel drink. Mooch
thirsty.” “Right you are,” said Charlie;
“I’ll shout for the camel. Give the camel
a drink too, boss.” The landlord served the
customers, and retired to carry out the latter
order, leading the dromedary to the tank at
the rear of the premises. After about 20
minutes, Kellow wondered what had become
of the man, and, going round, found the
camel like a newly-blown football on legs.
“What’s the bill?” he demanded of the
licensee. “ Ten drinks at a bob ten
shillings,” reckoned that individual, “and 36
pints of water at a bob for the camel—just <£2
6s. ! ”
Writes “ Cobbler” :
The youngest daughter of the (Fergus) Hume family,
Mrs. Willeby Hawthorne, who, you say, is getting noticed
in London as a soprano, is not the only musical member
of the family. Miss Bessie Hume is not only a very
talented instrumentalist, but has a contralto voice which
for volume, culture, expression and range I have never
heard equalled (and I have heard the world’s contraltos).
At one time it was her intention to come out as a public
singer, but through an unfortunate fall on a voyage from
England her spine was injured, and she has been an
invalid ever since. Fergus Hume, it may not be generally
known, is a barrister, but always had a weakness for
scribbling, to which his father strongly objected ; how-
ever, he told Fergus that provided he qualified as a law-
yer he (his father) would not mind his trying literature
afterwards. Fergus duly qualified, but never practised.
Fergus Hume tried hard to get his “ Mystery of a Han-
som Cab ” accepted by a publisher; without success.
Thanks, however, to' fortunate share specs, on advice
received from Miss Cornwall (the woman financier—
Madame Midas of his novel) he was enabled to publish the
work, which, as everyone knows, was so great a (financial)
success and the foundation-stone of his prosperity.
THE USUAL MALE.
SHE: “ Well, why don’t you get a servant? Before I
married you, you were always talking about how
well off you were.”
HE : “ Yes; and I was, too—only I didn’t know it!”
By “ determination ” of the General
Synod (C. of E.) of Australia, after ’97 the Bishop
of Sydney will lie the first C. of E. Archbishop in
Australia, to be followed by others so soon as
new “ecclesiastical provinces ” are created by sub-
division. Archbishop or Archangel, Smith is still
Smith.
Just a month before his death on Oct. 3
Mr. Samuel Drew, a Brokenhillionaire who con-
tinued to keep his money, gave <£2500 to build 12
cottages for the deserving poor of Adelaide. They
are now being built, one stipulation being that no
consideration of creed shall enter into the allot-
ment of the cottages.

Dear Bulletin, —You
say that “The Vagabond”
had seen many lands before
he reached Melbourne. That
may be, but he often claimed
knowledge of that sort which
he did not possess. For in-
stance, in “ The Vagabond
Papers ” we read of how he
once crossed the Atlantic to
London “on a mission not
unconnected with the Ala-
bama claims,” and how the
express stopped at Shrews-
bury, where Lord Beacons-
field was standing on the
platform idolised by the
people of the borough which
had once treated him with
contempt. The “Vag.” mor-
alises and then describes how
“Dizzy” got into the same
compartment and chatted
with him on various topics
during the journey to Lon-
don. Well-informed people
can see the absurdity of this,
but Melbourne swailowed it
whole. Like the servant-
girl who thinks a novel is no
good unless half the chief
characters are in the peer-
age, Southern snobocracy
likes a lord, and “The Vag.”
always had one on tap. But
the absurdity of the yarn is
heightened by the fact that
the express - train had no
business to be near Shrews-
bury at all. To one fa-
miliar with the shires there
are other little discrepancies
in “ The Vag.’s ” experi-
ences of rural England.—A.
Zoologist A. J. Campbell, of Vic. Customs
Department, has almost ready a work on “Nests
and Eggs of Australian Birds.” His collection of
typical eggs now contains those of over 500 dis-
tinct species. He intends after publication of his
book to present his unique collection to the Vic.
Government as trustee for the nation.
Australian send-offs seem to be bad for
the fire-breathing martial hero. Dr. Fiaschi
returns with, mainly, glowing intentions to his
credit, and the historical Soudan chevaliers all got
back with unslaked thirsts. Even Jimmy Tyson,
who received nine days’ wondering adulation,
slumped ignobly, and the dropsical purse he had
offered to Mars was thrown back into his napless
hat.
Speaker Graham Berry’s daughter
is now a most proficient tooth-puller
and shop-teeth-builder, and is likely to
become a most fashionable expert.
There are some queer fish in the
Victorian public service. For instance,
one high-up “ official ”on entering his
subordinates’ room invariably waves
aside with a gesture befitting his im-
portant position an imaginary curtain
in the doorway, and wipes his feet on an
imaginary mat before making known his
kingly will.
Spagnoletti : “A heap of non-
sense is written re Duncan Gillies’ lazi-
ness. Forty years in politics, filled every
Ministerial billet, a keen debater, lengthy
speaker, primed up, informative, why, it
is absurd. He doesn’t go on the sui-
cidal plan of Turner, Seddon, and Bal-
lance. In truth, a man of unremitting
industry, not spread too thick.”
A certain well-advertised Austra-
lian university professor is credited
with having lately remarked in class to
a student who failed to recapitulate cor-
rectly the details of an operation ex-
plained to him: “Oh, go away and
marry a rich wife and have 14 children ;
that’s all you're fit for. I’ll write to
your mother and advise her not to waste
,£2O by trying to make a doctor out of
a like you.”
The late George Verdon, K. C. M. G.,
had a good memory, and not long before
his death paid off an old score. The
friends of a necessitous journalist tried
to organise a testimonial in recognition
of his services to art and literature
and asked Verdon, as a man of kindred
tastes, to help, but he refused to take
any jiart in the matter. The members
of the deputation, all new-chums, were
unaware that some time in the early
Sixties the journalist, then editor of an
allegedly comic paper, published a car-
toon (“ The Wreck of the Royal George”)
descriptive of several lively incidents
that occurred on the banker’s trip to
England. Verdon never forgave the
joke, and bided his time.

Medical Professor Watson, of Adelaide,
who heads the rebellion against the recently-
imported doctors, Leith Napier and Ramsay
Smith, is a son of the late Sydney Grandisoc
Watson, R.N., the big Riverina squatter, “Kins
of the Upper Murray.” The professor was-
originally destined for the pulpit, and, when at
Melb. Scotch College, annexed all the available
Scripture prizes.
