POLITICAL
POINTS
A Maitland (N.S.W.) cabman, the other
day, received an official notice of objection to his
name remaining on the roll, the ground of objec-
tion being that—“you are dead.”
N.S.W. Public Service Board in vain
offered two professional officers, who have decided
to take better-paid posts in other provinces, in-
creases of salary—in one case, of 66§ per cent.; in
the other, of 50 per cent. Why? Either the
Board was “ sweating ’" the indicated officers, or
it was prepared to overpay them to retain them.
Winch ?
While-the deafening racket about the
N.S. W. revolution from indirect to direct taxation
is going on, some sane persons should every now
and then exclaim loudly that N.S.W. indirect
taxes still come to £ 1,500,000 a year, while the
direct ones only reach <£670,000. The revolution
has a long way to go yet to justify the present
volume of noise.
If the changed attitude of Premier Reid
towards N.S.W. Council leads to the dispersion of
the fixed popular idea that the Protectionist party
has hitherto always allied itself with the irrespon-
sible chamber against the legitimate aspirations of
the people, it will be the best thing that could
happen to the Opposition. The moon-orbiting
Premier is now in perigee.
Bananaland squatters are always clamor-
ing for reduction of rent, concessions, &c. Here
is an extract from a pastoralist’s prospectus. The
Fairbairns own Barcaldine, Bimerah, Clare, Peak
Downs, Magenta, and Wolfang, comprising in all
1,468,713 acres (about 932,000 acres leasehold).
Net profits for six years as shown by a careful
audit, -£320,283. received by State, about
£ll,OOO.
In Maoriland, according to a Govt, return,
there are 600 girl-apprentices working without
salary. The apprentice swindle in Maoriland con-
sists in hiring a girl to learn a business with the
understanding that she receives pay after the first
year “if suitable.” No girl ever is suitable, how-
ever, after her term of gratis service expires. This
S f m A/r S x Vil^ le is - very bacl in Australia also, but
the M.L. Govt, is the only one which is trying to
crush it flat.
Newspapers, as
The Bulletin has of-
ten remarked, receive
(that is, they permit'
the sort of correspond-
ence they desire. Just
now, an alleged Wagga
(anonymous) client of
Sydney D.T. is urging
N.S. W. Govt, to fight
(fiscally, as may be
proved logically) the
Vic. Govt, in re the
carriage of daily news-
papers by rail. The
Melbourne dailies are
delivered at Wagga at
5 p.m. ; the Sydney
dailies not till 9.31 p.m.
“Fight for N.5.W.,”
says the alleged Wagga
client ; “fight for the
Sydney papers ! ” And
the D.T. publishes that
letter, without delay.
Curious, too, that the
D.T. declares itself to
be “freetrade,” and
doesn’t welcome Vic-
torian free and open
competition. More cu-
rious that the compe-
tition can’t be free and
open, because Vic.,
which is “ protection-
ist,” charges postage-
fees on the mailing of
newspapers, while N.S.
Wales (which the
“ God-fearing Govern-
ment ” says is “free-
trade ”) “protects ” lo-
cal newspapers by car-
rying them post-free.
Freetrade ,is merely a
free-grab, by everyone
for his own “ free ”
hand. Not, until the
Sydney D.T. does the
naked penance of the
ancient English king,
and is scourged by
Abbot Reid before the
altar of newspaper-
postage, and acknow-
ledges its sacrilegious
grab at the pockets of
the people (according to its own gospel) will it be
believed as a genuine freetrade organ.
The “ Freetrade ” Sydney Evening News,
moralising on Saturday last upon the application
of Freetrade principles to sugar-growing on the
Clarence, knocks the professed policy of that
paper into little bits. Why doesn’t the News
change its hour of publication and come out as
the long-desired Protectionist morning oracle ?
Roll-stuffing and working the
graveyard-vote used to be a fine art in
the bad old whisky days of Maoriland; in
fact, that province is alleged to have held
the record, and things were done there
which would make the hair of even the
Queensland Boodle Ministry stand on
end. In those days the Irish party was
strong and solid, and once when an elec-
tion up Auckland way was trembling in
the balance the party got all the bullocks
of two Irish farmers put on the roll, like-
wise two heifers, a Maori idol, and a
horse. They all voted to the satisfac-
tion of a very-drunk returning-officer,
and the Irish candidate got in by a
majority so small that it made his suppor-
ters shiver when they thought of it after-
wards. Even now there are very old
horses in some northern districts whose
votes helped materially to turn the scale
when their party was in a tight place, and
a chaff-cutter, which is said to have
once been on the roll, is still in existence.
The first article of Sydney Tele-
graph’s promised series on the evils of
Protection in Victoria contains just one
argument : that in ’93 N.S. Wales pro-
duced wealth of all kinds (pastoral, agri-
cultural, mining, manufacturing, and
other sorts) to the amount of a little over
=£35,000,000, while Victoria produced
wealth to the value of barely .£26,000,000.
Nothing is said about the fact that N.S.
Wales is nearly four times the size of Vic-
toria, or about the other fact that N.S. W.
was a protected province in ’93. In pro-
portion to area, Victoria produces 2g
times as much wealth as N.S.W. ; then
come protected Maoriland and Tasmania,
and the cornstalk straggles in fourth. In
proportion to population the protected
Queenslander produces the most wealth;
then come Maoriland, Westralia, and
N.S.W. again wanders in fourth. The
prduction argument doesn’t seem to pan
out so well as it might do.
A freed slave blessing his quondam flogger;
a pagan calling upon the idol he has burnt to
ashes; a Wesleyan kissing the Pope’s toe —these
and many other images crowd the fancy when
reading of the French Republic waving flags,
firing guns, pouring out money, getting drunk,
and metaphorically crawling on its belly, in order
to hug to its anti-monarchical bosom the Czar of
Russia—the apex of the cone of this earth’s
despotic monarchy.

Sydney Telegraph :
Other things equal, there can hardly be any question
about the preferableness of penny postage to "twopenny
postage. . . . But letter-carrying costs money, and the
obligation devolves upon the Postal Department of
honestly paying its way like every other business concern.
. . . Mr. Cook stated last night that he was an advocate
of penny postage only on the condition that the country
could afford it. This is the correct view.
Also, newspaper-carrying costs money, and the
obligation which “ devolves upon the Postal De-
partment of honestly paying its way ” is not met
because of the loss incurred in subsidising the
press by free postage, and if the country can’t
afford to carry a letter to Bourke or Walgett or
Broken Hill for Id., it can’t afford to carry a
newspaper, which is far larger and heavier, there
for nothing. And that also is the “ correct view.”
Perry, M.P., Opposition whip, carried the
adjournment of N.S.W. Assembly in the small
hours of a recent sitting. Isn’t this a new prece-
dent ? The Premier and co. were mostly absent
“ refreshing,” and the incident is illustrative of
how nowanights legislation is left to take charge
of itself.
IN THE DAYS WHEN THE WORLD IS STIFF.

In all comparisons between the popula-
tions, the industries, the agricultural develop-
ments, &c., of Victoria and N.S.W., it should be
remembered that N.S.W. has more than 3J times
—something between 3 7-15ths and 3 8-15ths—of
the area of Victoria. Yet the N.S.W. Freetrader,
fond of denouncing the protectionism of Victoria,
is equally fond of insisting that “everything”
comes out of “land and labor.” The tonunyrot
that can be written after gazing at any large ques-
tion through one solitary slot of an otherwise
light-excluding chamber !
A wail goes up from Vic. Opposi-
tion that Turner’s One-Adult-One-Vote
measure is “ inopportune. ” The MTntyre
crowd isn’t absolutely against it; all it
complains of is that the present isn’t the
right time to deal with the subject, and
what will be the right time it doesn’t
profess to know. Anyhow, the right
time has never come yet, and no one
sees it coming, and the MTntyre lot
doesn’t even give a description of it so
that it can be recognised when it arrives.
Good old Inopportune; what a lot of
service it does in the sacred cause of
stagnation !
The Maoriland Tory party’s case
against the measure making all interest
over 8 per cent, illegal and irrecoverable,
practically reads as follows: (1) It will
prevent borrowers getting money at all, as
lenders won’t do business at 8 per cent.
(2) It is unnecessary and ridiculous, as
interestrates are steadily going down and
promise soon to reach 2 per cent. (3) It
is a brutal infringement of the liberty of
the subject. And how, with money at
2 per cent., business will be stopped by
a legal 8 per cent, minimum, and how

Victoria has an admirable Health
Act for the suppression of dirty dairies,
tainted milk, diseased cows, and the like,
but the weak spot in it is that the ad-
ministration of the law is entrusted to
the municipal authorities. Apparently
municipal authorities are filthy by in-
stinct, for the Act is practically a dead
letter. The leprous heifer and the tuber-
culosed oow, and the dairy which is Bin. deep
in filth, and the milk which is ripened in an
old boot-trunk under the bed are, in most
municipalities, practically free from interference.
The general impression now is that the central
Government will have to personally tackle the en-
forcement of the Act. Local government has been
a success in almost every other respect, but the
dirtiness of the municipal fathers seems incurable.
Tas. Premier Braddon to introduce a
Referendum Bill, whereby any measure twice re-
jected by the Council may be submitted for the
yea or nay of the people. But as the landlords
don t want to abdicate, Braddon’s Bill will not be
paid on presentation. Knowing this, boodleism
in the Assembly will vociferously support the
Referendum proposals.

Q. ex-Governor Norman declares that
“ Australia is hardly alive to the dangers of hos-
tility from Asia.” Perhaps it is too busy begin-
ning to think about the dangers of hostility from
Hingland.
One absolutely irrefutable assertion of
Lyne, leader of N.S.W. Opposition, has to do
with Sydney morning press. “The morning
papers often contain articles misconstruing unre-
ported points in the speeches of the Opposition.”
This is wholly true. A sentenoe is cut out of the
reporter’s notes and given to a leader-writer;
afterwards the article goes in, but the excerpt
does not go back to its place in the report. This
is not on all occasions proof of malevolent design,
but it is sufficiently offensive to justify the speech-
maker’s indignation.
SEDDON: AN ESTIMATE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not his, perhaps, the noble aims
Marred when hard fate let Ballance die.
Nor love the great Pro-Consul claims
For altruistic chivalry.
Not Stout’s or Russell’s wasted chance,
Grasping at power beyond their reach ;
M‘Kenzie’s dourness, Ward’s finance,
Nor Reeves’s clear, incisive speech.
But with an iron will to meet
And thwart each Tory turn and twist,
Each envy-bred attack to greet
With ready tongue and heavy fist;
Strong in the people’s confidence,
Tactful o’er friend and foe to rule,
Combining with his honest sense
The “ noble firmness of the mule.”
Tyrant, they say, in use of power ;
But this, at least, deny who can :
Fate never pointed to the hour
But Premier Dick has found the man.
Gisborne, M.L.

The N.S.W. Art Society’s Show.
There is a current story that N.S.W. Art Society
rejected one “ picture ” sent in for the 17th annual
exhibition, just to show its unswerving deter-
mination 'to uphold artistic ideals, at whatever
cost to the catalogue. The writer merely guaran-
tees that most of the exhibited canvases are not
worth hanging, though some of their perpetrators
come near deserving it. Really, in what purports
to be an exhibition of Art, the committee might
be a little less catholic. Perhaps their difficulty
is that of the good patriot who was convinced that
half the world wanted guillotining, and refrained
from pressing his idea merely because he knew he
badly wanted guillotining himself.
The artistic pretensions of the passing show
rest mainly on the efforts of two men, Coutts and
Piguenit, and though both show remarkably clever
and faithful work, neither attempts to infuse into
it the highest of artistic qualities—the quality of
imagination. They invent nothing, paint objec-
tively, paint outsides merely. It has been said of
Henry Lawson that he describes characteristics,
does not create characters. Coutts and Piguenit
work from a similar standpoint : they paint artis-
tic pictures without creating Art. Their hands
are admirably dexterous, but their minds are
almost quiescent. Of the two, judged by the pic-
tures shown, Piguenit gets the most artistic re-
sults. It is difficult to conceive that his beautiful
land-and-water-scapes could be better painted.
He chooses dawn or dusk, when the tints are
clear and tender; he composes his picture
with great skill, taking the best bits from
every skyline; and he reproduces laboriously,
with a neatness of drawing and a translucent per-
fection of coloring that rival in effect the charm of
Nature herself. But his method is still in essence
photographic: he has added nothing of his own :
he has merely caught a beautiful light, a beautiful
shadow of rock on water, a beautiful effect of
trees against gleaming sky, and fixed it imperish-
ably. This is good to do, but it is not the highest
Art. A really great picture is unique : it is im-
pregnated with the artist’s own personality : no
other individual in the world, it seems to one, has
had precisely the same idea, or has portrayed it in
precisely the same way. But Nature repeats
Piguenit’s pictures year after year for whomsoever
will come with no very remarkable eye to gaze
upon them. In essence, therefore, Piguenit is not
an Artist, but a Naturalist.
* * • *
This is partly why his pictures gain little from
their size but price. His “First Gleam,” at 15gs.,
is really more attractive than his “ Valley of the
Upper Murray,” at 150 gs. It is the same with
Coutts. “Waiting,” at <£2s, is quite as good an
example of his art as “At Twilight’s Hour,”
which is priced at £lBO. Coutts’s power lies most
in his fine coloring and his finer conscientiousness.
Both Coutts and Piguenit seem always trying to
do their best. (Lister seems occasionally careless
whether he does his worst.) But Coutts photo-
graphs figures just as Piguenit photographs land-
scapes. “Waiting” is all but Art: it merely
wanted a little imagination, a little more charac-
ter put into the face and eyes, and it would have
been a really fine painting. But it seems as
if the artist had been afraid to let himself go—
as if he had been afraid to drop the actual model
which' he knew he could handle, lest his
shadowy ideal should desert him also and leave
him resultless. Now, Nature never makes any
model, who is exactly, to the last jot and tittle,
what a, model might be. To express any over-
mastering emotion an artist must supplement her—
perfect her. Coutts does not seem able to do this
yet. When he can, if he can, he will be all
Artist. And why shouldn’t he ?
' * * * *
Apart from Coutts and Piguenit there is at the
Art Society’s show little to be glad and’a good
deal to be sorry about. There are some admirable
examples of technique in drawing, and one or two
in colouring. Minns has some purpler aboriginals,
and Marion Drewe’s New England aboriginal,
though less artistically done, is better done, be-
cause done less untruly. Percy Spence shines in
black and white : his more ambitious cattle-paint-
ing displays some energy and a good deal of skill,
but he has not yet excelled his “Plough-
man” picture in the N.S.W. Gallery. There
is comedy in his fowl-picture, which is very
neat in its way. H. W. Macready’s “Work” is
good and vital: a promising thing. Aline Cusack
and Win. Birkenhead work in the way of Piguenit,
and catch a good deal of his result. But, as a rule,
the artists represented are contented to creep
feebly along a beaten track : they take no initia-
tive, display no strength of conception. The
Bulletin quite recognises that they must creep
before they gang, but some of them have been
creeping for years now, and gang no forrarder.
Whence these tears in the voice of their critic.
Yic. Attorney-General Isaacs intends in-
troducing a Bill to deal with the rates of interest
chargeable, by money-lenders. Another attack
upon Capital ! Soon a man will not be able to
get a living in Victoria except by working for it.
Sydney Truth deplores that several Euro-
pean Powers “ are awaiting a chance to dismember
our Empire.” Yet Truth calls itself a republican
organ ! “ Our Empire ! ” A phrase worthy of
Sydney D.T., which boasts of the British navy as
“ our navy,” and of even Indian troops as “ our
army.” Australia is an adolescent republic, just
beginning to feel for the first hairs of its
moustache.
The Curse of Dean still works. Exactly
one year, to a day, from the date of Crick’s arrest,
his dear friend, John Norton, was committed for
alleged sedition. Also, the Curse seems to have
fallen on the convicted murderers Dederer : Arch-
deacon Piddington’s recent terrible error of judg-
ment probably arose from a misconception by him
of the trend of the discussion re the historical
action of Salomons, Q.C., in disclosing “in the
interests of justice ” information professionally
gained.
Dr. Fiaschi, of Sydney, who volunteered
for Abyssinian war service, and has just returned
to Sydney, per American mail, has in the British
Medical Journal of Aug. 29 a peculiarly-inter-
esting paper entitled “ A Report on the Mutil-
ated and Evirated of the Battle of Adowa,” from
which it appears that the Abyssinians, when they
took black soldiers prisoners, cut off the right
hand and left foot to prevent them fighting again ;
but, when they caught Italians, they invariably
emasculated them to' prevent the continuance of
the race of the accursed white fighters as far as
possible. Also, though the arm and leg amputa-
tions were made simply by sweeping a sword or
dagger through the joint, without any attempt at
forming any sort of surgical flaps, the recoveries
were almost uniformly good, thus showing what
splendid constitutions the African blacks have
while still in the Dark Continent. Curiously
enough, American records show that the slaves
there rapidly deteriorated when they took to
white man’'! inod, *nd were carried off in thou-
sands by phthisis

Albert Trott and Jack Harry” (writes
“ Monico” to The Bulletin from London) “ left England
per Oruba Sept. 4, both looking well, and the veteran
especially much younger. They are returning next
season to qualify for Middlesex. A.E.’s bowling record is
better than his batting, showing 2074 balls for 1171 runs
and 43 wickets, an average of 27'23, and placing him 37th
amongst English bowlers.
“ Yorkshire are the cricket champions for ’96,
having lost but three matches. Surrey, the premiers for
the past three years, are but fourth on the list, having
been defeated seven times owing chiefly to what is now
widely termed the ‘ Surrey rot.’ When Abel, Hayward,
and Richardson fail, Surrey fails. The ‘ tykes ’ are the
best all-round county and have fairly earned their honors.
Lancashire is second, thanks to M'Laren, and Middlesex
a poor third.
“F. (ancy) S. (tuff) Jackson heads the batting
of the champions and comes eighth in the all-England
averages with 40.83. It is. related of this very self-appre-
ciative cricketer that, having made a fairly large score in
a college match, he replied to the congratulations of an
admirer : ‘ Ya-as, I’m glad I made a scoah ; it may help
fathahin his politics.’ ‘Father’was afterwards Chief-
Secretary for Ireland, so perhaps it did.
“ The Australians, who conclude their English
tour this week with a defeat and a probable victory, are
admitted to have been less susceptible to the Great National
, Thirst than any of their predecessors. On the night pre-
ceding the final test every man was in bed at 10.30. Of
course there were sufficient lapses to secure the team
from the sneer of ‘ cast-iron cherubs,’ but they were of a
temporary nature. The thanks of Australia are due to
the one player who ‘ stood out,’ and sacrificed himself on
the ‘ toasting ’ rack to shield his comrades.
“Grace in ‘undress’ usually wears a black
loosely-fitting beaufort, pillar-y shaped trousers of the
hue of the St. Kilda Red Bluff, and a square-crowned
brown bowler. He thus has a striking resemblance to the
Goulburn Valley farmer for whom so many kind friends
wait at Spencer-street to show the city sights and a pecu-
liar match-box.
“The death of Mrs. Jack Barrett was a great
blow to her husband, the doctor-cricketer. The body has
been embalmed, and will be taken to Australia for inter-
ment.”
* * * *
Grace’s captainship in the fatal test-match is
something for inexperienced Australian cricketers to
remember. Grace changed the bowling when one Aus.
wicket was down for 0; took off Richardson, who had
bowled Iredale a maiden, and put on Peel with Hearne.
Then seven for 14 ! Great Scott! Why didn’t the Aus-
tralians send in their wives ?
* * * *
The thoroughly professional spirit in which
W. G. Grace plays cricket was never better shown than in
his creating the precedent that when an injured batsman
has another man to run for him, that other must wear
pads—as many pads as the disabled one. He should have
been thorough while at it, and insisted on the substitute
wearing not lighter boots, or lighter pants, or less hat or
moustache. The boss amateur was always the boss
pointer of English cricket.
* * * *
Wicket-keeper Jarvis (S.A.) will be 36 to-
morrow, Sunday, Oct. 18. Jarvis, who did not succeed
with his Adelaide sports-depot business, has gone back
to his trade as blacksmith. Darling, bv the way, has
now a monopoly of the Adelaide cricket anil football trade.
* * * *
Adelaide cricket-matches are again to be played
this year on the two halves of Adelaide Oval. For years
cricket experts have ascribed the dearth of good fieldmen
and bowlers in the S.A. teams to this fact. No bowler
can possibly develope when batsmen can score four runs
for a hit which on larger grounds would be fielded. The
small playing-space is killing S.A. junior bowlers and
spoiling the batsmen, but the agitation for a change is
steadily ignored.
* * * •
M‘Kibbin was admitted in England to be boss
break bowler ; in fact, his big break lost almost more
wickets than it gained on sticky pitches ; since it would
bowl the batsman and miss the sticks.
* * * *
Iredale was one of the studies of the tour.
During the excitement of the second test-match he put a
wet towel round his head to deafen the crowd’s din. One
day he failed like a dead man, the next he pasted All-
England. In the outfield he took 35 consecutive catches,
and was ranked the first outfield in the world.
* * * *
Mold, the English fast bowler, knocked a bail
63yds. 6in. from the sticks in Surrey v. Lancashire.
Previous best was 62yds. by Rotheram (Eng.). Mold’s
ball is the sort that hitting a batsman is likelv to knock
his pluck clean out of the ground; or, as in “the case of
George Giffen and others, bowls them before they come in.

One reason for the former preponderance of
football over cricket interest lay in the fact that cricket-
matclies may take days to play, whilst a game of football
is decided in a couple of hours. With one day’s play not
one of Saturday’s Melb. cricket-matches is advanced be-
yond the first innings.
* * * *
• Manager Musgrove has all the best of the argu-
ment with the meddlesome Australian Cricket Council.
He points out that the trip will only delay the team’s
arrival in Australia by two weeks in the early part of the
season ; that the team took upon itself all financial re-
sponsibility of the tour, and therefore deserves all the
success it can command ; that the team has worthily up-
held the fame of Australian cricket; that it should not
be called upon to break faith with M.L., especially as no
previous team has been peremptorily ordered back by the
Council. The Council’s case is first to call the team “ a
money-grubbing, vagabondising combination” ; secondly,
to assert that the Council, by arranging the “ test match
in Sydney, gave the team £llOO, which is bosh, since the
cricketers, not the Council, commanded the public, and
the public, not the Council, paid the £1100; thirdly, that
the Council lent the team £3O0 —which was promptly re-
funded, perhaps with interest or perhaps without.
* * » *
Harry Musgrove’s idea of taking a team of 20
Australian baseball players to the United States next
Spring is a perfectly lovely notion ; why no one ever
thought of it before is a mystery. Australians don’t play
baseball, but they are hard-up enough and clever enough
with hand, foot and eye to learn baseball or any other
game which will pay dividends. The Great Republic will
be a huge hunting-ground for the Australian representa-
tive baseballer; success in the game means more cash
than the Australian cricketer can catch in England. Base-
ball —by all means let us play baseball. It was a foolish
thing for that Yankee baseball team to come here ; but to
take an Australian team to the States is a perfect inspira-
tion.
* * * *
The Centennial Football Club, winners of the
premiership of the N.S.W. Northern District, began the
season under the most inauspicious circumstances. It
was heavily in debt and had only six players remaining,
four of whom, however, were interprovincial men. Wal.
Oobb, the popular full-back and captain of the team,
stuck to his uphill task with unusual spirit and determin-
ation. This club has a singular record. Of the first 73
matches which it played as juniors it won 72. Their
first year as seniors saw them easily premier. On the
death of Cobb’s brother, who, it will be remembered,
expired in the football field, the club became hopelessly
demoralised. This year, however, it has pulled itself
together and re-asserted its supremacy.
* * * •
Copy of the conclusion to a letter of application
for a billet (as accountant), recently received by a town
firm : —“ I was in ’s employ for some years as clerk,
and then left to go as bookkeeper on a cattle-station. I
left there through getting hurt while out mustering.
Seeing your firm are enthusiasts in sport, I am a little in
that line myself, as I was in the club that won the junior
premiership last year, and have since played senior; and
if you have any Brisbane footballers in your establishment,
you will find I am ‘ not so stinking,’ as the saying is.
I am also a fair cricketer, as the score-hooks of the
Cricket Club can testify, and it was in acting as see. and
treas. of the above club that I picked up my knowledge
of bookkeeping. I am offered another job here, but, if
you answer quickly, I would kindly oblige, as I would like
to play with your boys.”
» * * *
The Vic. Football Ass. strictly forbids profes-
sionalism in its ranks; yet (writes “ The Jay ” to The
Bulletin) it is alleged to have been declared on behalf of
nine leading players in a certain very big match that they
would play “ stiff” in that momentous contest unless they
received more than 20s. each —(it appeared two leading
players were to receive more than the others). The com-
mittee were told of the players' decision, and were given
till the evening before the match to increase the rate all-
round. Outcome : the committee decided to increase the
amount to £2 each.
* * * *
The Melb. police showed keen foresight on the
Saturday evening of Collingwood’s football premiership
victory, by sending Black Maria to that ambrosial suburb.
Had “ South” been successful, they would probably have
been favored with the presence of the legal conveyance.
As it happened, a hearse seemed more suited to their
needs.
* * * *
If the mishap to Fort had been as slight as was
at first supposed it would possibly have given the Caul-
field Cup to the son of Gibraltar. The average Australian
horse needs an interruption in his training to prevent him
from coming to the post “ overdone.” Hence the resusci-
tated Miraculum has friends for Melb. Cup.
* * * *
Jim Gough is among the many who reckon that
Newhaven will win the double —Derby and Cup. All the
same, the chestnut colt isn’t a more likely Cup-winner on
paper than Loyalty was in Tarcoola’s year. Loyalty fol-
lowed up his early three-year old performances in the
Spring Stakes and Craven Plate at Randwick (Newhaven
lately scored in the same two races) by winning the Melb.
Stakes (1J mile) brilliantly. And then he couldn’t stay
the Cup distance, with 7st. 31b. in the saddle. And
against Newhaven’s chance it must be noted that the
“hot pot” is a Newminster, whose offspring are rather
notorious for being non-stayers in their young days, and
long-distance goers when they get seasoned. Vengeance
and Idolator were cases in point.
¥ * * *
The best Adelaide judges seem to be more fond
of Broken Hill (despite his dicky leg) for Melbourne Cup
than of Thunder Queen for the Caulfield. But the mare
ought to finish near the winner next Saturday.
♦ * * *
First-day results of the V.A.T.C. meeting
afforded three pieces of valuable information re the
coming Cups. In the first place, Hopscotch stood out
from a crowd of seeming commoners as a very probable
winner of the lj-mile race. Hopscotch stays well enough,
it may be, until he gets tackled by an equally fast horse,
such as the Hova and Wallace of last year. Nothing
troubled the big chestnut on Saturday, and his strong,
masterful finish in the Toorak Handicap suggests that he
ought to go very close for the Caulfield Cup if Hon. Bill
Long, in his doubtful, wisdom, doesn’t reserve his cham-
pion for the longer journey at Flemington. At time of
writing, the stable intentions are misty.
The run-away victory of Hopscotch in Caulfield
Stakes is said to have been less remarkable, under the
circumstances, than it appeared at the moment. They
took the precaution to run him in light plates, conse-
quently he galloped easily, whilst the barefooted toilers
in his rear failed to get a good hold of the greasy ground.
This was the excuse offered by several losing jockeys
after the race, and it seems reasonable.
Wallace’s decent performance in the Stakes
gave the public some little encouragement to hope that
the hero of last season will re-assert himself. The writer
fears that Wallace has “ gone off,” like Carnage, his half-
brother. He has thickened ;he ran more lazily than ever
on Saturday, and his white hind-leg threatens to be dotty
when the ground hardens. Nevertheless, Wallace, who
seems to have done plenty of good work, came at the
finish of the Stakes with a few imposing strides, and next
to Hopscotch he was the least distressed animal in the
crowd.
The downing of Newhaven in the Guineas may,
or may not, have been a real defeat. Anyway, it proved
that W. T. Jones’s “pot” couldn’t do as he liked with
them at the end of a fast-run mile. Newhaven was not
“ making hacks ” of Coil and Cydnus as they came
together down the straight, and granting that The
Officer’s run took him unawares there still remains the
doubt as to whether he could have stalled off the challenge
of Cook’s horse even if jockey Holmes had blown a
whistle to notify the favorite that he was rushing up.
One thing is certain. The faces of Newhaven’s friends
after the race were painfully sad, and they didn’t feel
cocksure of upsetting the Guineas “ form” at Flemington.
Evidently The Officer, a strapping son of Robinson
Crusoe, and reputed to be the apple of Sam Cook’s other
eye, is a good three-year-old, with a brilliant turn of
speed.
As regards Caulfield Cup chances, last week The
Bulletin named The Parisienne, Bloodshot, Marusa, and
Duhallowin preference to Hopscotch, Merry Boy, Thunder
Queen, Ayrshire, and W. R. Wilson’s chosen, which latter
will be Resolute, all going well with the youngster mean-
while. Of the nine candidates aforesaid the writer has
lost faith in Bloodshot, Duhallow, and Ayrshire, for one
reason or other. The bad-tempered Maorilander seems
not to be ripe for a severe tussle. Duhallow ran roguishly
the other day and may be getting stale, whilst Ayrshire is
not fancied (by those who should know) as much as his
stable-companion Marusa. Also, it may be said of Du-
hallow that he has no visible pull over Excel, Canoona, or
one or two other second-rate horses that are favorably
handicapped. Many people swear by Excel.
Tempering its previous opinion with last Satur-
day’s experience, The Bulletin now takes Parisienne,
Resolute, Hopscotch, and Thunder Queen —four horses
to fill the first three places. Three of them will
certainly run, bar accidents. If this season’s three-
year-olds are a pretty good lot (and they look
it), the older horses, being a rather poor lot, with
the solitary exception of Hopscotch, can’t be ex-
pected to run away from the young ’uns in accordance
with Caulfield Cup traditions. Thunder Queen has
“ form” to recommend her, Le Yar is a stylish colt, and
Resolute, handicapped at the weight which Waterfall won
with last year, is worth backing before any other three-
year-old in the race. Hopscotch and Parisienne are
here reckoned the best chances among the older
animals. The big horse may not start, and
should he go for all he is worth even then the doubt as
to his stamina will, to the writer’s way of thinking, make
him a less tempting investment at the price than The
Parisienne. She is very well just now, and Holmes is al-
most certain to have the mount. The race that has twice
gone to Paris may this year be captured by The Parisienne,
and perhaps Resolute and Hopscotch may see the lady
home. All the same, there is no special reason for sug-
gesting that this tip will turn out better than many
former tips for the same race. As a rule The Bulletin
can’t guess what is going to win Caulfield Cup, so in the
possible event of Parisienne proving the exception this
newspaper will not feel puffed up.
Marusa, Erelnvon, and Bloodshot have been
scratched.
All things considered, the greatest surprise of
last Saturday’s sport at Caulfield was Galway’s starting
price. Galway had not been seen on the course since he
won a sensational steeplechase after falling at one of the
half-way jumps. The gelding then showed such speed
that he was surely worth backing in moderate hurdle-
race company —nevertheless, the Ballarat gelding went
out at 10 to 1. Nothing seemed to have a chance with
the good thing, save Wirrawa, who suddenly dropped out
of it three furlongs from home. Young Wirrawa ought
to win a hurdle-race shortly.
* * • *
Mr. W. A. Long left Melbourne at 1 o’clock on
Saturday, which must have galled him at least a little,
considering his horse. Hopscotch, was destined to win the
double at Caulfield in the afternoon. It may be safely
surmised, however, that the impassive Mr. Long had backed
the gelding for sufficient to pay the ex’s of his trip ere
leaving Melbourne.
* * * *
Another Carbine, Metford, won the Debutant
Stakes at Caulfield. Each winner that turns up makes it
more evident the horse should never have been allowed to
cross the pond.
* * * *
N.S.W., with a numerically weak contingent,
did exceedingly well on the opening day of the Southern
carnival, winning the three principal races and running
third in two of them. Le Var, Reka and Lieutenant are
three more or less show-cards yet to be played.
* * * *
Considering there was no rival meeting, Sydney
Hospital sports proved but a dead-and-alive success. Only
5000 of the Sydney crowd thought it worth while to see
considerable athletic talent perform under a hot sun, in
the presence of vice-royalty, for a blue ribbon and the
good cause.
The Maypole Dance, being performed by chil-
dren, fell flat as a sermon. Sydney’s crowd is blas6 ; the
dancing of young children bores it to death. If the Hos-
pital sports management would supply a high-kicker on
a raised platform, while Bilmartin, dressed in tights, went
round and sold button-holes and cadged silver, the result
would be an eye-opener.
The cycling part of the bill brought out two fine
rides by M’Coombe and Kerr. M'Coombe, off 20yds., did
the half-mile in lmin. 2sec.; Kerr, off ser., in lmin. 2 4-ssec.
G. Mead ham, with lmin. 3 2-ssec., previously held the half-
mile record on Sydney Agri. Ground. Kerr’s brother was
timekeeper for Kerr. Goodson, the Rockhamptonite, did
not compete ; he has gone home.
The programme dragged heavily; quarter-hour
waits completely ruined the crowd’s temper. Some of
the trying intervals were made more miserable by the
spectacle of Y.M.O.A. young men springing and vaulting
and climbing about parallel - bars and things. The
Y.M.O.A. young man should never be allowed to do any-
thing in public, until the public has left. Because the
said young man never seems to do anything which is
worth doing.
While the Hospital nurses were selling sweets
and roses at 50 times their value the Ikey Mo tough im-
proved the shining hour by howling the odds and making
occasional hauls. No official suppressed Ikey ; the charity
spirit, which is the church spirit, which is the bazaar
spirit, which is the gambling spirit, tolerated him at his
loudest.
Davison tried to ride a race with a finish against
M‘Coombe in the mile scratch race, but when it came to
the final spurt Davison was thrown yards behind by
M'Ooombe’s first effort.
(“ Sporting Notions ” continued on page 20.)

This company is being formed the purpose of acquiring and further developing the leases known as
Clough’s, consisting of about fifty (50) acres, situated at Walhalla. ,
There is a tunnel driven into the hill about 200 ft., giving about 2ooft. of backs to work upon.
The stone varies in size from Ift. lOin. to 2ft., arid going strong underfoot; a winze is now being
sunk, frofn which excellent prospects have been obtained. The crushings from a poor battery yielded
550 z. of gold from 16 tons of stone. ' . , . . ... (
A battery will be purchased as soon as the company is formed, and as there is a large quantity ot
stone in sight, crushing will be started as soon as the battery is erected.
The company will be considered floated when 10,000 shares have been sold, m which case £lOOO
will be put to the credit of the company, and the proceeds of 6000 more shares when sold will also be
placed to credit of the company ; proceeds of 4000 remaining shares go to the vendors.
The following is the report of Mr. F. Tupper, who has had a large experience of mining in this
district . Walhalla, 15th August, 1896.
I beg to report having visited Clough’s Tunnel, now called the Resolute, on Saturday last, and was greatly taken
with the show. The claim is situated about a mile to the east of the Long Tunnel mine. ~
The reef is 2ft. wide and showing beautiful gold. A winze is being sunk, which shows the reef is making uider as
it goes down ;it has a beautiful footwall and hanging wall. If it continues the way it is at present, it will be another
There is every convenience for a battery, plenty of water, a creek flowing all the year round traverses the pro-
perty, and there is timber in abundance.
(Signed) Frederick Tupper.

menced practice as a patents agent in Sydney.

ment to bring this colony within the pro visions of
the International Patents Convention, so as to
secure to a local patentee protection in all the
countries within the convention.*

Mahlstick: 1 ‘Outbrief candle! DuMaurier.
An artist with about seven puppets, and a dog.
His women, to my idea, are more conventional
and much less pleasant than those of Leech.
Trench critics dub Keene the overlooked genius
of Punch. Beside the infinitely superior social
drawings of Partridge, of late, I have been unable
to tolerate Du Maurier. He is no loss to the
London Charivari. An artist, nowadays, must
work in the spirit of Mulready, who, as a veteran,
drew with the lads in the Life School.”
The failure of the jury to agree in re the
criminal charge of libel preferred by the N.S.W.
authorities against John Norton, editor of Sydney
Truth, for libelling Queen Victoria, is a severe
knock-m-the-eye for the Crown, but cannot be
marvelled at considering the circumstances sur-
rounding the initiation of certain more or less
recent criminal prosecutions. Norton defended
himself most adroitly, and throughout held the
advantage which an able amateur lawyer always
holds before a jury over his professional anta-
gonist.
Lord Rosebery’s resignation of British
Liberal leadership, so far as he is personally con-
cerned, merely removes the fifth wheel from the
party coach. His rule was never very popular or
very successful, and he was only foisted on the
party by an intrigue which wounded Harcourt
and enraged Labouchere. But Rosebery the
name-and-influence counts for a great deal more
than Rosebery the man. His retirement means
difficulty in managing that large section of the
Liberal-party which likes to crawl pleasantly after
a peer.
The Vic. Royal Commission on legal pro-
cedure will bring some spicy evidence to light.
Tor instance: A farmer near the Yan Yean,
whose wife had been burnt by a fire lit by Board
of Works employes, claimed <£lso damages. But I
the White Knight got his back up at the enormity j
of allowing ratepayers to be robbed thus —he I
resisted the claim, and the farmer went to law. j
Result, verdict for plaintiff. The Board appealed.
At the second trial the jury disagreed. Trial
No. 3, verdict for plaintiff with costs, and this
time the judges had not face enough to order a
fourth. The cost to the country is something like

Mr. Arthur Griffith wishes to notify intend-
ing patentees that he has commenced practice as a
Patents and Trade Marks Agent at 39 Castlereagh-
street, Sydney. He has entered into arrange-
ments with leading agents in Europe, America,
Australasia, and South Africa, so that inventors
may rely upon having their interests protected.
Mr. Griffith’s charges will, in all cases, be so
moderate as to place a patent within the reach of
the poorest inventor. For £2O, exclusive of the
cost of plans, Mr. Griffith will obtain Provisional
Protection for any invention in the seven Austra-
lasian colonies and Great Britain.*
While Capt. Slocum, of the yacht Spray,
was in Newcastle, the mission steamer, John
Williams, called for bunker coal. The sky-pilots
paid him a visit and openly expressed their
astonishment at the hardihood of a man who
could trust himself lone handed in mid-ocean in
such a craft. Their astonishment became still
greater when they saw his navigating instruments,
which consisted of a rusty sextant, an old-
fashioned chronometer and a battered telescope.
“You must indeed trust a great deal in the
Almighty,” said one of the reverend gentlemen.
“Well, yes, sometimes I do,” said Slocum. On
leaving the Spray they invited him to pay them a
return visit. The following day Slocum boarded
the John Williams and was shown all-round. The
chart-room especially interested him. It was fur-
nished with a couple of Sir Win. Thompson’s
spirit compasses, three of the latest type of
chronometers, and with all the most recent forms
of navigating instruments. Slocum examined
them with great attention, and then gravely said :
“ I guess, gentlemen, you don’t trust much to the
Almighty in this hooker.”

Death.
GRIFFITHS.—On September 27, at her resid-
ence, Sentinel Road, Ponsonby, Emma, the
beloved wife of Albert Griffiths, and dear mother
of Charles Frederick Griffiths, Werter Albert
Griffiths, and Harold Egbert Griffiths, all of
Auckland, aged 66. A patient sufferer for years.
Rest at last.

Compare : Martin has won in his 11 months
63 firsts, 10 seconds, 8 thirds—value, £977 ; with retainers
and bonuses added—say £l4OO. Martin, examined the
other day by a prominent Sydney doctor, was pronounced
to be an abnormal man, who would eventually collapse
through heart disease.
* * * •
Martin is looking for long distance matches just
now; wants to race J. Carpenter, of Vic., 200 miles for
£2OO. But it is quite unlikely that any Australian will
take Martin on for a long-distance scratch race.
• * * *
Last Monday week it was a sight for the “ gods ”
to seg-Bilmartin coming out of N.S.W. Savings Bank care-
fully, examining his bank-book (he having just added a
hundred or so to his little lot). Other athletes, cyclists,
and especially some of our successful jockeys please note
and be present to see Bilmartin coming out again on the
Monday after the carnival meeting.
* * * *
The Goold Bicycle Co. writes us that Red Bird
Martin does not receive from them a weekly salary of £B.
* * * *
It is the policy of the Australasian and the
Leader to disagree in everything. They even disagree, in
their illustrations of the Sydney to Melb. cycle relay ride,
as to the manner in which Gov. Brassey was dressed when
he received the despatch at the termination of that grease-
ful personal advt., one paper representing him as wearing
a “hard-hitter ” hat and a sac-coat, and the other a “ bell-
topper ” and a frock-coat. As a matter of fact he wore a
feather behind his ear, and a blanket.
* * * *
George Burston, of Melb., probably the most-
travelled cyclist in the world to-day, has just returned to
Vic. from a European jaunt of 5000 miles odd, accom-
plished without a fall or mishap. Burston it was who
rode from Calcutta to Smyrna half-dozen years ago, and
since that date he has been consistently exploring foreign
lands per cycle at the rate of 10,000 miles a year.
♦ ♦ *
“ Jimmy ” Carpenter, winner of the Warrnam-
bool-to-Melb. Road Race, cut down his last year’s time by
2h. 12min., doing the 165 miles in Bh. 40mim,and winning
the race from scratch and 26 competitors. At Geelong,
40 miles from home, Carpenter was “ all out,” and, though
20min. behind G. Broadbent, managed to effect the really
splendid performance of catching the latter at Footscray,
three miles from the winning-post, and pedalling in smin.
ahead. During the progress of the race, Carpenter re-
duced the Australian 50 mile record by lOmin., and the
100 mile by 40min., though out of the latter he wasted a
quarter of an hour repairing a puncture. Middleton, the
plucky wobbler who carried off the 25 and 50 mile road
championships, broke his chain three miles after starting.
The wind was favorable, but the road none too good.
Con. Dwyer, the hero of the ordinary machines
before the advent of the Busst brothers, started from
limit in the road-race above-mentioned, and finished in the
train. How are the mighty fallen ?
* * * *
What has become of M'Donell, lately vanquished
by Martin? Very probably the so-called “Road King”
has had enough of Sydney. The N.S.W. Wheelmen’s
League gave him a two-hours’ gruelling for alleged queer
riding in his match with Martin. The League accepted
M'Donell's Yankeeish explanation : “ I wobbled across the
track so as to give Martin all the disadvantage of the
wind ; and I hit her up sudden and stopped her with a
jerk, because by that means I would tire Martin and his
big gear out.” Martin, now in Melb., tried to get a road-
match on with M'Donell, but when Martin’s man called
at M'Donell’s hotel, the Road King had left.
* * * *
A Melb. bicycle agency was burned to the
ground last week, and 150 machines destroyed. This
scorching must be put down.
* * * *
A royal row brews between Melb. Bicycle Club
and the League of Wheelmen, which practically means
the backing-down or extinction of one. The trouble
originated over a very simple matter, the color system.
The lacing men complained that the silk jacket enforced
by M. B.C. rules was uncomfortable and expensive, and, as
a result, the League decided upon action. The M.B.C.
then suggested to lease its system to the League, and, on
the latter's refusing, offered to hand over the system for a
sh'lhng a year. Chagrined at the prior demand of the
club, the hot-headed crowd of the League council would
have none of it, and carried its own svstem —necessarily
founded on that of the M.B.C. The latter now announces
that its color regulations will be enforced strictly and in
their entirety at the coming Austral meeting, and
threatens the League with law for infringing copyright
The M.B.C. is entitled to the credit of being the first to
stop the confusing of red, scarlet, magenta, claret, crim-
son, &c., which so often appear on the racing-card as differ-
ent Colors. Under its system there is red and only red, blue
and only blue, and so on, the manner in which the colors
paj be combined being also defined, so that mistake is
impossible.
* * * *
At Essex (Eng.) last month, a ped. beat P. W.
Brown, the mile champion cyclist, in a 100yds. sprint.
Time, 10 4-sths sec. Same meeting, in a mile handicap
between a cyclist, a runner and a walker, the latter, who
had 1150yds., won. Cyclist, scratch, was second : the
runner, who received 735yds. start, did not complete the
distance.
* * * *
Parsons English and American experiences
seem confined to the facts that he got “home” two
months late for business and that he could not get the
pacing necessary to do himself justice in his few races
He has four triplets coming out from France, and when
they arrive he will go for Bilmartin’s scalp. He gets a
consideration for using the Simpson chain. He talks
about, challenging Michael, the Welsh wonder, in the
future. Michael has a sextette, four quads, and four trip-
lets to pace him in a hard race.
* * * *
Parsons says that his English and American
trips have put him in the way of averaging £lO per week
above the screw he formerly received in Australia. He
also says that while in Australia, between Oct, ’95 and
April, ’96, he banked more money than Martin did. ’ His
American records—half-mile in 51 1-5, and mile in 1 51 2-5
—have brought him good retainers from the trade. ’
Parsons has grown a little ; is much harder and
sturdier than on his departure from Australia • he now
weighs 10st. 71b. stripped : he used to be nearly a stone
less. He has kept himself in buckle on the way out and
is confident of reaching A 1 form at the N.S.W. Wheel-
men’s approaching carnival.
Re Martin, Parsons says : “ Bill is the best all-
round cyclist in the world ; < all-round ’ means good for
a paced or unpaced race, for a lap-dash or 1000 miles.
Also, he is perhaps the most knowing rider the globe
over ; I certainly have met no man who gave me more
tips than did Martin.
“ For combining sprint with staying powers the
Frenchmen, as a class, head the list. This is all the more
remarkable because they don’t train with the laborious
conscientiousness so popular with the English and Ameri-
cans. The American racer won’t smoke ; and he would
shun wine like leprosy; buthe sucks up iced water just
as an Australian drinks tea.
Ihe American trainers are great believers in
the kneading* process; and the trainer’s word is law.
Many a top American rider obeys his trainer in the model
way in which a child is supposed to obey his father.
It is quite true that American cracks get over
$6,100 a season for riding for a manufacturer They cul-
tivate all sprinting in the States ; lap dashes, half-mile,
mde and two mile races ; they are wonderful sprinters,
the hist half-dozen of them. John S. Johnson comes to
Australia for certain, so far as I know now j if he strikes
form, he will show us what finishing means. Michael,
the Welsh wonder, promised me he too would come.”
* * * *
Much disgust is prevalent in Melb. at the return
of Parsons without competing in some first-class races at
( line or Murka. Considering that he was given a com-
-1 nient.ii'y meeting, a national subscription, &e., it is
thought 1 hat the least he could do would have been to try
and beat some of the English cracks.
* * * *
Parsons’ matches with Jay Eaton ended in
hollow victories for the swift and cunning American. The
first match (best two heats out of three), one mile, w r as
won straight out by Eaton in 2.14 3-sths. and 2.16 4-sths.
The second match, five miles, similar conditions, was won
by Eaton easily in 11.55 and 12.00. The American papers
excuse Parsons, on the ground that he is not Al. But
will Parsons ever be Al on the racing track? Up to now
he has given at least some indications of being a racer
who cannot race.
* * * *
Lewis’ recent letter from England to the Sydney
press about Megson, Payne and himself is a very weak-
backed story. “At Wood Green I ran second in my heat
in the half-mile handicap off 25yds. ; beaten by a foot; it
was hard luck. In the final of the five miles, I was fifth ;
a tablecloth would have covered us.” Now listen to this :
“ We would like to stay here or go to America till next
season, but seeing Bill Martin scooping the pools so easily
we are anxious to have another race with him.” Fancy
the dauntless three returning 12,000 miles, although they
are happy where they are, simply because thev are
anxious to race Martin. Before they left, Martin offered
to race all three over 25 miles, a fresh man at every
8)4 miles, for any sum they liked to put up. He also
offered to race any one of them any distance.
* * * *
Unlucky in politics, ex-Premier Rosebery strikes
the best of luck in racing. He has won two Derbys, and
now possesses the two best 2yr-olds in England. Last
week he secured the Imperial Produce Stakes of £3OOO
with Chelandry (half-sister to Derby winner Ladas), who
a short time previous won Nat. Breeders’ Stakes of £4357.
Velasquez (half-brother to Derby winner Sir Visto) won
Prince of Wales’ Stakes at Goodwood worth £2400, and is
now first favorite for next year’s English Derby.
* * * *
Kerr, if his business will allow it, should accept
the Johannesburg challenge, and also go to England.
Kerr’s ride last Saturday was a fine performance for any
cyclist, and supports Parsons’ assertion that Kerr would
do well against the English amateurs.
* * * *
The Fitzsimmons-Corbett preliminaries have
reached the stage of another “agreement.” The men
met, drank wine, shook hands, “ agreed ” to box for the
largest purse yet offered and a side stake of $lO,OOO.
“ Although a very small table separated the pugilists, no
blows were struck, and nothing more than an occasional
hard look was passed.” The battle of the century is sup-
posed to take place not sooner than Jan., ’97, and not
later than March.
* * * *
A Vic. country newspaper which received the
agency-telegram, “Baron V. Mueller dead,” gave the fol-
lowing ingenious interpretation to its startled readers the
following day
“FATAL PRIZE FIGHT.
“ MUELLER KILLED.
“ A prize-fight took place in Melb. last night between
the well-known boxers Baron and Mueller. Boch men
were heavily hacked hv their respective supporters, and
the fight was of a most desperate character. In the last
round Baron got a heavy uppercut on Mueller’s jaw, and
the latter fell unconscious. After lingering some hours,
the unfortunate young fellow died without recovering his
senses.”
The rival rag in the next township would have
waxed extremely sarcastic only for the fact that it itself
ascribed another meaning to the word “ dead,” and said
that the cycling-match between Baron and Mueller had
proved to he a fake, and the League was taking action to
disqualify both competitors.


A PRISONER’S ESCAPE.
Between the months of May and August last
year a party of convicts were hard at work against
the side of Table Mountain, Cape Colony. The
overseer, who with keen eye was watching the
operations, showed signs of fatigue consequent on
his exertions during so many months of toil in all
sorts of weather. For many a month his wife had,
with an anxious heart, watched her husband’s
health, which she knew was declining. His work,
from six in the morning to three in the afternoon,
began to tell on his constitution in a more marked
degree from day to day. At last he began to spit
blood. The distressing anxiety of his poor wife
now became most painful to observe. What she
had long ago dreaded now seemed certain, and
with intense sorrow she had now to yield to the
awful expectation that her husband’s ailments
would culminate in death ! The doctor had treated
him for a long time, and had showed him
every attention and kindness, but in spite
of all attention to instructions given Mr. Ru-
dolph’s health continued to decline. At last
an event took place which sealed his fate
in the opinion of his friends. He was out attend-
ing to his work one day, when he burst a blood-
vessel and brought up an enormous quantity of
blood. He was placed in a cab and sent home,
where his wife received him in.a terrible condition.
He was bleeding, pale, and shattered ! For days
his life was despaired of, and he was often uncon-
scious. After lingering in (his critical o ruiition
for a long time, during win h he had inllammation
of the lungs and was utv! i medical treatment, he
was ordered to go into the country, which he did.
He spent months in the country. His medical
adviser did all in his power to tinrg back strength
to his patient, but no skill or sing seemed to
make much difference. Mr. Carries John Ru-
dolph (for that is the name of the patient in
question) was called on at his residence, No. 11
Drury-street, by a newspaper representative, to
whom he gave the above information and pro-
ceeded with his story thus:—“I returned from
the country in an enervated state, having given
up all hope of ever being able to do my work again.
The futiire was dark. My poor wife’s anxiety was
telling on her. Misery reigned in our home. At
last she heard of the wonderful cures effected by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. She
had tried so many medicines that she at first
hardly expected even this remedy to have any
beneficial effect. But she determined to try. She
bought a box of the Pills, and I used them. I felt
my appetite slowly returning. I used a second
box, which increased my strength wonderfully. I
have now used the fourth box. My legs are
strong; as you see for yourself, I can go about and
do work. My appetite is good. That awful feel-
ing of enervation and weariness has left me, and
the dreadful and trying anxiety of my poor wife
has made room for hope and happiness. I have
persevered in the use of Doctor Williams’ Pink
Pills, and can now declare to the effectual cure
wrought by them, having changed me from an in-
valid to the man you see now—able to do his work
and enjoying health. I have spent a lot of money
in medicines and medical attendance, not caring
what I spent so long as I might reasonably expect
improvement in my miserable condition, and here
I have used only four boxes of the Pills and they
have done what nothing else’ seemed able to do.
Taught by personal experience, I now recommend
this wonderful medicine to all sufferers from weak-
ness, poverty of blood, and all the attendant ail-
ments. ” The reporter then withdrew, after thank-
ing Mr. and Mrs. Rudolph for the information
gi ven.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are a scientific prepara-
tion discovered by an eminent physician, after
years of careful study and experiment, and used by
him a number of years in both hospital and private
practice. They are a specific for such diseases as
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, and a genuine
Bloodbuilder and Nerve Tonic. Used after In-
fluenza, they immediately build up the system.
Females' troubles of all descriptions are positively
cured by them in a very short time, and pale and
sallow .complexions soon attain a glow of health
by their use.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are obtainable from all

North Sydney, June 25, 1896.
Messrs. Fisher and Co.
Gentlemen, —I cannot speak too highly of your excellent
preparation of KOLA NUT. As a tonic, I found it far
superior to anything of the kind I ever used, and have
strongly recommended it to several friends, all of whom
(after taking it) are enthusiastic in their praise of it. Kola
Nut Tonic has a most beneficial effect after an attack of
INFLUENZA ; on some of my acquaintances it has pro-
duced wonderful effects, particularly in strengthening the
muscles of chest and limbs. Many of my cycling friends
swear hv it. I notice its use has been adopted by the
Alpine Club as a STIMULANT and NUTRIENT in moun-
tain-climbing expeditions, &e., as it generally increases
the power of the LUNGS and keeps off muscular fatigue,
and will prove invaluable to athletes. I feel confident
Kola Nut Tonic will have a large consumption, for those
who once try it will always keep it in store.—Yours faith-
fully,
CHARLES WISEHEART,
Willoughby.
NO CYCLIST SHOULD BE WITHOUT THIS REMARK-
ABLE INVIGORATOR, ESPECIALLY LADIES.
EVERY SINGER, AUCTIONEER, and PUBLIC
SPEAKER should take KOLA NUT TONIC.
A recent issue of the Planters’ Monthly contains a
description of this remarkable nut and its uses :
“ Kola has the property of staying hunger, whilst sup-
porting the struggle for existence.
“ If a supply of nuts had been included in Mr. Stanley’s
outfit his men would not have suffered so terribly for want
of animal food.
“ As might be expected, the value of Kola increases as
it makes its way into the interior of Africa, and some of
the tribes farthest removed from the sea pay for the dry
powder-an equal weight of gold-dust.
“In a despatch from Mr. George Alexander Stevens,
Her Majesty’s Consul at Bahai, he calls attention to the
great powers of endurance in lifting heavy loads and
transporting them to long distances in the tropical
climate, attributed to the free use of Kola Bean. An old
African, after biting a piece of the magic Bean, lifted 179
English pounds weight on to his head and carried the
same for a distance of four miles.
“Still another merit is named, however, which will
interest the temperance party. The negros who chew
Kola Nut have no desire to drink rum, and hence save
money and retain their strength.”
A woman —whose name under the circumstances cannot
be given—came into our shop the other day, with tears of
gratitude in her eyes, and said, “ My husband used to
come home on Saturday's DRUNK, but he has been taking
Kola Nut Tonic for four weeks, and now comes home
quite SOBER and places the whole of his wages in my
hands. God bless you ! ”
UNEQUALLED.

‘ ‘ Ghostly comforter ! ” By the way, a com-
forter is something to be put round the neck.
Norman Malcolm, sec. Westralian Agri-
cultural Dept., who seems to be the leading spirit
in the good work of prosecuting sellers of tuber-
culous and other diseased cattle, was until recently
agricultural editor of S.A. Advertiser.
In part excuse for Chief Justice Madden’s
record of 18 judgments upset on appeal it is only
fair to say that Victoria’s dandy “Chief’’gives
many straightforward decisions in knotty cases
which his less exalted colleagues would probably
refer to the Full Court. Judge Williams, for
instance, is very apt to avoid the risk of an upset
by shunting his responsibility upon the F.C.
Madden sits in the place where one is expected to
be almost infallible, and the excessive number of
his failures is the penalty of the greatness that J. B.
Patterson appointed him to. In a sense this is
hard upon him, for nobody ever thought of the
garrulous Irish gentleman as a real boss Judge.
He was practically created permanent Acting-
Governor on account of his gaudy “frill” and
waxed moustache. The only legitimate way of
working him into a cocked hat was to give him the
Chief Justice’s wig to play with, hence the melan-
choly humor of the legal situation in Victoria as it
affects litigants.

Is. bottles. Made in London. *
Dear Bulletin, —Among the hooraying
“ Christian Endeavorers ’’ who garlanded a loco-
motive and came to spread self-righteousness in
Sydney, was Rev. Silas Mead, a good old sort of
devil-dodger from Adelaide (S.A.), “who wouldn’t
hurt a fly ’’ (as Brunton Stephens says, for “ why
should he hurt a fly?”). I never see that man’s
name but I feel that he owes me something for
personal damages— twice. First time : He butted
at me in a newspaper controversy upon a quasi-
religious question, and, after I had replied, he re-
fused to hold further argument with an opponent
who could act in the dastardly way that I had
done, &c., &c. Editor Finlayson, to my sorrow,
backed him up (i.e., to me, verbally). What had I
done ? I had used up a whole battery of metaphor
about Silas Mead being not only a wet blanket but
a hip-bath, a plunge and a douche on the very
desirable object under discussion. “ Well ? ” says
I, still puzzled. “ Well!” says Finlayson, “he’s
a Baptist clergyman ! ” Great Scott! How was I
to have known that ? Case No. 2: In another
quasi-religious article, in which I had occasion to
mention Mr. Mead’s name (and I had become
cautious about treating this 'particular Baptist
with a special respect, almost amounting to rever-
ence), an evil-minded compo. introduced an utterly
gratuitous “e ” into it, and the Adelaide world
read, above my pen-name, “the Sileas Mead ” !
and pronounced the “i ” short ! No use my try-
ing to make anyone, then, believe that I hadn’t
fathered it! So I didn’t try, but bore the blame and
my burden of sorrow in secret. There was a Sunday-
school teacher of Mr. Mead’s church—also a press-
man, really /—who was to give some Bible-class, or
church-meeting, or something-or-other of the small
organisms that such institutions sp>awn, a lecture
on the poetess Eliza Cook. That Sunday-school
pressman asked another (not me) if he’d draw out,
or suggest, a good, short, attractive advt. about
the lecture. How the other pressman managed it
I don’t know, but I’m told that the advt. actually
went in the very proper Register, in the space
allotted to religious lectures, under the big-typed
heading —“ A Night with Eliza Cook.” When we
remember these things, do we think that we can
ever give up the drink ? —Yours, in retrospection,
The Dipsomaniac.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

A Western Yic. butter-factory paid by test
results. A supplier skimmed his milk for a week,
and received a percentage of nearly 5; previous
test, 4'9. No wonder everyone isn’t satisfied with
testing-machines. “Solemn fact,” says a Bulle-
tin correrpondent.
The Yic. Agricultural Department recom-
mended farmers and graziers to sow a new fodder-
grass called lathyrus sylvestris. Baron Von Mueller
now assures all whom it may concern that said
grass is poisonous and injurious to cattle not
reared on it.
New business combination has been intro-
duced by a N.Q. publican, who hires a Javanese
tailor at a few shillings a week —soliciting orders
over the bar and making a good thing out of it.
But how long before the tailor owns the pub. and
runs a white snip ?

S : “I have seen it mentioned in The
Bulletin that the hardened smoker who loses his
eyesight presently ceases to find pleasure in his
pipe, and in due course abandons his favorite vice.
The supposition is that the real pleasure of smok-
ing is in seeing the smoke. But why are smokers
so particular about brands ? A few days back I
made the acquaintance of a blind man (blinded
years ago in a Ballarat mine). He is a persistent
smoker, and always blows the smoke upon his
hand—practically feels himself smoking. Asked
the reason of this, he declared that he found no
satisfaction in his pipe if he simply blew the
smoke into the air. ‘ Might as well suck at a cold
clay,’ he said.”

A favourite food of the N.Q. blacks is the
little green ant. This insect builds its nest of
green leaves in the fork or branch of some tree
and lives mostly on fruit and honey. The red
meat ant is also regarded as a prime morsel by the
average aboriginal, who will knock the top off an
ant’s nest and then “ scoff ” a fistful of ants, dirt,
and ant-eggs with the greatest gusto.
Twenty-three years ago Ceylon exported
just 231 b. of tea, and now the export has reached
102 million lb. in the year. As tea has increased
coffee has decreased. Wherefore other countries,
Queensland among the number, are entering the
arena, of coffee competition— with what success
remains to be proven.
Dear Bulletin, — Re Barcoo rot being
caused by dust from trees. This disease extends
all over Western Leperland, where you can travel
for days and never see a tree, barring an odd
coolabah in the water-courses. Barcoo rot only
shows on arms or hands exposed to the weather.
The slightest scratch will turn into a large festering
sore, taking weeks to heal. Sometimes blood-
blisters appear on the backs of the hands, break,
and turn into large 'sores. The disease never
appears on the face, or other parts of the body,
and if the sores on the hands or arms are kept
tightly covered with oiled rag they soon disappear.
Some think the want of vegetables the cause ; but
people with plenty of vegetables get it just the
same. Also, red-headed folk suffer most. I think
the cause is poison in the air—from the swarms of
flies, perhaps.—G.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
Dear Bulletin, — I have to apologise to
“ V.” if I said that no whisky is made from malt.
I meant that no whisky worth mentioning is made
from it. I know little about small distilleries and
probably I am hopelessly ignorant. So thought
Mr. Gladstone of Huxley in the swine contro-
versy. But Huxley out-savvied the G.O.M. and
conducted his argument like a gentleman, which
William Ewart did not. Again, is rye an “inferior
material ” ? What is Bourbon whisky ? Inferior ?
Ask America. Whatever the Albury puffer may
say, in the most paying distillery in Scotland the
white spirit is now called foreshot, or was a few
years ago. Allow me to compare two authorities
m the same page (adjacent columns) of your issue
of sth inst. British Liquor Commission—“ The
fusel oil is not an impurity—and gives the charac-
teristic flavor, and if entirely removed the result
would be silent spirit. Whisky does not lose its
fusel oil in ageing.” “V.” (probably of Corowa
or Albury)—“Fusel oil is deleterious, but with
age decomposes and adds flavor and bouquet to
the whisky.” If, moreover, I stated (I keep no
copies) in my letter that grain whisky is sent to
France to be converted into brandy, bet your last
dime on me. Of course I acknowledge that since
whisky has become the rage there is an out-
let for the patent-still-alcohol, call it what you
please. My authority is the man I called my
governor. He told me it was so, and, therefore, I
plunge on one distillery, and that same whisky-
mill has draff (do you know what that is, “V. ”?)
run from John o’ Groats to the Solway in its own
railway-trucks, with the legend (say) Scotty’s Dis-
tillery printed on the sides. And does “ V.” also
know that there is not enough real brandy made
in France to fill one-tenth part of one exporter’s
bottles; and that we, i.e., the above Scotties of
the whisky-mills, supplied him with all our forty-
rod for years ? But, then, like Huxley, we don’t
abuse our temporary Gladstones, leaving that
D. V. to d d“Y. ” of Corowa or Albury. Lastly,
if Scotty, qui ego sum, says that good brandy is
produced near Narandera, just field again and bar
Scotty. Ido not think it is out of place to state
that the gentleman who allowed me to taste his
home-made brandy is Mr. Jenkins, of Hucking-
bang, who, unfortunately, cannot either sell or
give it away, it being only permitted as a fortifier
for his wine.— Scotty the Whinklek.
