“The Vagabond.”
The mystery in
which the career
of “ The Vaga-
bond, ” previous to
his oometic ap-
pearance in the
Australian sky, is
enveloped will
' probably never
now be solved. It
was his own choice
to keep his past
veiled in clouds.
Judging by the
notices published
since his decease,
he had not in his
latter years departed from the secretiveness which
was so marked during the period immediately suc-
ceeding his first coruscations. Many people can
remember the sensation his writings caused, the
intense curiosity as to the unknown author, and
the active attempts to discover his antecedents,
when at length the veil of anonymity was dropped
and Mr. “Julian Thomas” stood revealed as the
author of “The Vagabond Papers.” That dis-
closure was a great disappointment. From an in-
termittent sequence of grandiose suggestions and
tantalising hints in the famous “Papers,” the
public had formed rather magnificent theories as
to the importance and social rank of the Great
Unknown.
* * * *
Just previous to the appearance in the Argus of
“The Vagabond Papers,” the London Daily
News or Daily Telegraph had been publishing
a series of papers, signed “ Azimut Batuk,”
written somewhat in the same vein, and it was
understood that “ Azimut Batuk ” was the nom de
guerre of some very important personage indeed.
At one stage of “ The Vagabond ” series it was
bruited about that “ The Vagabond ” was no other
than “ Azimut Batuk ” under a fresh mask. Some
passages in “ A Month in Pentridge ” shook that
belief. It was agreed that Azimut was far too
great a person ever to have acquired those qualifi-
cations of dispensing chemist and tooth-drawer,
which enabled* “ The Vagabond ” to work up some
of his most telling episodes in his Pentridge
sketch. One of these was his account of how he
relieved the hangman of a huge aching molar.
But the touch-and-go hints and glimpses of alleged
past events, scattered through the Papers, un-
questionably tickled the public expectation. “ The
Vagabond ” was, by fitful flashes, shown by him-
self as an individual who had been on chatting
terms with Gladstone and other men of European
magnitude, and behind the scenes when events of
European importance occupied the stage. There
was a touch here displaying the diplomat, and
there another, the man of action. Without much
circumlocution he gave narratives from personal
knowledge of events previously unknown, relative
to the Fenian movement at the trial of Head-
Centre Stephens, also touching the Agricultural
Laborers’ awakening which brought Joseph Arch
to the front and seriously alarmed the British
Home Office and the Govt, generally. He had
been mixed up in some rather important but occult
way with the uprising or suppression—which of
the two was not made at all clear—of the Com-
mune in Paris, and had been arrested and locked
up in the Mazas Prison in that connection.
* * * •
Then again the Western Hemisphere had wit-
nessed his activity. He had housed and nursed
Stanley in his chambers in New York, when that
eminent explorer, in the first flush of his fame,
returned thither in broken health, after his suc-
cessful expedition in search of Dr. Livingstone.
Anyhow, when Mr. Julian Thomas stood dis-
closed as the writer of the really brilliant sketches
in Melb. Argus, his personality didn’t seem to fit
in with the conceptions built up from the demi-
semi-revelations scattered through the articles.
The writing conveyed impressions of an indivi-
duality much greater than the writer cared to
communicate. The intimations of importance
had the appearance of sheer unavoidable leakages.
They seemed as though they were due to momen-
tary relaxation of a deliberate effort to conceal a
real importance. If readers gathered that “ The
Vagabond” moved in lofty company and dis-
charged weighty functions, that was due merely
to slips of the pen and expressions fraught with
more impressiveness than had been designed. The
public had fancied that, when “The Vagabond ”
should deign to disclose himself, it would be a
step antecedent to his immediate absorption into
the innermost circles of Government House and
Ministerial closets. The real outcome of the dis-
closure of the personality of Mr. Julian Thomas
never got nearer those ideals than a public banquet
by “ our leading citizens.” Instead of the majestic
being, the living monument of self-repressed power
and almost unconscious magnitude, the real man
proved to be of florid style, rather apt to magnify
than to depreciate his claims to homage and
admiration. It became apparent that instead
of being in a state of semi-exclusion, and of having
stooped to gratify Australia with his observations,
Mr. Julian Thomas felt himself to be at the
zenith of his fortunes, and was almost peremp-
torily greedy of the admiration which his recent
performances entitled him to. It was perfectly
clear that this was not the sort of man who would
reserve any circumstance which might contribute
to surround him with recognitions and deferences.
He was not.the type of individual into whose ear
a Gladstone would pour confidences. Whatever
his distinctions, whatever his deeds of mark,
whatever his abilities, no one could fail to see that
he “ had them all on.” His bearing was not the
bearing of a Ben Franklin, fraught with vast
influence and wide knowledge under an exterior
of modest plainness. His manners were not the
manners of a Talleyrand emerged from an incog-
nito. It was rather the effusive swagger of Red-
mond Barry, insistant on exacting recognition of
the utmost of his claims to distraction. Thus, he
had not long dropped an incognito for a pseu-
donym before he tacked D.C.L. to his name,
alleging a learned degree conferred by some ob-
scure collegiate institution somewhere in the
Southern States of the Gre*#t Republic. As to
other antecedents, he continued vague and ora-
cular.
* * • *
“ The Vagabond’s ” peculiar conversation at this
period was interlarded with references to where
he had been—which covered the whole extent of
the Bohemia of his times. But what he had been
here and there, or when he had figured in this or that
region, were points touched with dexterous inde-
finiteness. It is not certain whether it was from
his conversation or something published by him
that it was gathered that among his adventures
had been participation in the expedition of
Walker, the. filibuster and sometime Presi-
dent of Nicaragua. It was probably in con-
versation. He claimed to have made ac-
quaintance in that association with the two
Lysters William and Frederick who also,
according to that account, were among Walker’s
recruits from San Francisco. This was not ex-
actly a piece of self-exaltation. Walker’s despera-
does were not at all ashamed of the sort of fame
which they believed due to themselves. It was a
common boast among them that “ San Francisco
is the sink of the Pacific coast, and we are the
dregs of San Francisco.” The Lysters, for a long
series of years connected with opera in Australia —
William as entrepreneur and Fred, as score-
adapter and basso—did come from San Francisco,
and had on the Pacific Coast experienced many
strange vicissitudes. It was there, probably, that
they had for comrade that magnificent specimen
of manly beauty, one of the Haigs of Bemerside, a
wandering scion of an antique race of Scotch lairds
who occasionally joined their company in Aus-
tralia, and under the stage-name of De Haga
added to the attractions of the operas his noble
presence and tremendous voice. All three, Haig
and the Lysters, were grand figures of men : quite
6ft., and built in proportion. The scraggy fili-
buster, Walker, with his intuitive knowledge of
men and his heart of steel, would have chosen
them from amidst thousands. And Julian Thomas,
although not of the same stature, was a well-knit,
solid man, brimming over with talent, and not one
whom Walker would fail to welcome.
* * . *
What “The Vagabond’s” actual name was re-
mains par of the mystery of his past. He arrived in
Sydney from ’Frisco not long before his first article
in the Melbourne Argus commenced his celebrity,
gyffiSSl'terft £2
Circular Quay. There he was known as “ Sydney
James,” a name which he had gone under
years before in another part of the world. His
first article, or at any rate the first one which
struck the public attention, was a sketch of “A
Model Lodging House”: apparently-it might
almost be said obviously—an account of personal
experiences in the semi-charitable public estab-
lishment so called, in Melbourne, where men
found shelter and subsistance when at the end of
their resources. The thing was written on the
lines and in the spirit of Greenwood’s “Amateur
Casual” article, which made such a sensation in
London and set a fashion for journalistic work of
that character. The subject and the signature
were unquestionably the outcomes of the writer’s
circumstances. The article was very clever, and,
being offered to the Argus, its value and merit
were recognised by the late Hugh George, at that
time practically an absolute ruler over the paper.
Its instantaneous success, on publication, led to a
demand for others in the same vein, ‘ and the
quality rather rising than otherwise, a long series
found place in the Argus and Australasian,
achieving perhaps the most emphatic and best-
merited success ever attained in Australian jour-
nalism. In fact, the “ Vagabond ” Papers con-
stitute what in athletics is termed “ a record,”
and the man who can surpass it has not yet
appeared
11 '* * * *
The moment of the “Vagabond’s” abandon-
ment of his incognito was that of the climax of his
literary career. While the authorship of his
writings remained a matter of speculation, their
intrinsic merits were reinforced by the interest of
the mystery surrounding the authorship. The
peculiar suggestiveness already dwelt upon, of
passing hints, had teased and excited the curiosity
of the public almost to a point of frenzy,
Readers were seized with a fever of con-
jecture. Thus in the articles they found not
merely clever writing and interesting disclosures
but all the provocations of a hidden identity.
When this curiosity was satisfied—or disappointed
—by the disclosure of the author, and his writings
were consequently deprived of that intrinsic charm
their popularity rapidly abated. “ The Vaga-
bond ” had fairly exhausted the resources of local
interest. The manager of the Argus decided to
utilise the popular writer’s talents in a fresh direc-
tion. Mr. Thomas was despatched to Cooktown
on a special mission to investigate on the spot the
problem, just then very much in evidence, of the
best course to be adopted with respect to the pro-
digious influx of Chinese to Northern Queensland,
attracted by the Palmer gold discoveries. Mr.
Thomas accordingly proceeded to Queensland,
and in Brisbane broke fresh ground by appearing
as a lecturer. The effort was not happy. His
manner did not please, and his method 01 pro-
nouncing with a self-sufficient complacency, on
points of political difficulty which the Parliamen-
tary leaders of the province had confessed pre-
sented to them almost insoluble complications, was
received with a mixture of derision and resent-
ment. Arrived at Cooktown, a single week
sufficed Mr. Thomas for the announcement by
telegram to the Argus, republished by Queens-
land papers, to the effect, in very concise terms,
that he had arrived at a solution of the whole
difficulty. This proclamation was too much for
the public risibility. Respect for the ability of
the writer began to sink in amusement at the self-
sufficiency of the man. Mr. Julian Thomas’ star
was visibly on the wane.
* * * *
It was not long after this that the connection
between “ The Vagabond ” and the Melbourne
Argus came to an end. But, prior to that part-
ing, he did some very good work as special cor-
respondent at New Caledonia for the paper, at the
time of the aboriginal insurrection. Later, his
pen was transferred to the service of the Age. He
obtained a post as secretary to a Government
board, which for some years exempted him from
the strain of an unattached contributor to the
press. But his later years were occupied in that
vocation.
* * * *
On the subject of his parentage and birthplace,
Mr. Thomas seems never to have opened himself.
Some of his writings hint at a Virginian origin,
others indicate Wales as his natal land. Indica-
tions bore witness to a far-back infusion of Afri-
can blood. As for the early part of his career, an
authority beyond question competent, the late
Mr. William O’Carroll, whilom editor of the
Courier, himself proud to tell that in Ireland he
had been a member of the Fenian Brotherhood,
although in Queensland he claimed that the
Queen had no more loyal subject, pronounced with
deliberation that “The Vagabond” disclosed in
certain of his “ Papers” too intimate acquaintance
with the minor workings of the Fenian body to
have been casually obtained, yet not sufficient to
have been procured by actual affiliation. Again,
a settler in the Caboolture district, who had been
intimately associated with Joseph Arch in the
formation of the English Agricultural Laborers’
League, professed to recognise in “ The Vaga-
bond,” who had written for the Argus a very inte-
restingaccountof the agricultural question, a person
who, bearing the name of Sydney James, had myste-
riously made his appearance at the headquarters of
the agitation, endeavored to ingratiate himself
with the organisers, as a sympathiser, but who, in
default of definite knowledge of what his precise
business on the scene might be, was repulsed with
suspicion. These observations, taken in connec-
tion with the indefinite sort of occasion for Mr.
Thomas’s presence in Paris, as narrated by him-
self during the Commune, and his determined
reticence regarding his antecedent occupations, left
in many minds in Brisbane and elsewhere an im-
pression which has never been effaced. “ The
Vagabond ” was “ good company ” in a Bohemian
way, and a most entertaining raconteur, although
constantly the hero of his own capital stories.
His age, at the time of his decease, must have
been nearly 60 years. But nothing precise can be
stated in the absence of such particulars as are
commonly known of even less gifted and far less
notable men.
Sydney Daily Telegraph the other dayan-
nounoed a sale of the late Henry Parkes : curios,
one of them being “ two coins joined together
bearing date of 270 B.o.” Such a coin should
fetch a lot of money. Very few ancient monarchs
knew enough to date their coins in advance like
that.
The Yackandandah (Vic.) medical case,
Coane v. Mueller, which would have revealed the
possibilities of rural medical cut and thrust, and
have shown the occasional hilariousness of medical
journalism, has been very wisely settled out of
court, both sides withdrawing imputations and
considering each other gentlemen.
Careful trials at Wagga experimental-farm
show that the best results are got by hand-sowing
wheat grains separately Sin. apart in parallel rows
6in. apart. Director Yalder has, for the last
three years, thus got from 17 to 36 bushels to the
acre, which is far above the record of much better
land in the neighborhood. This method only uses
up 41b. seed to the acre. On some of the French
Government farms wheat machine-sown to the
same scale averages 60 bushels per acre. Moral
plain.
The continuous series of diving fatalities
at Thursday Island appears to be non-preventable
by precautions or regulations. Nearly all the
accidents are caused by carelessness or imperfect
appliances, and occur to men diving on their own
account without proper assistance, and not to men
working for a white or other employer. To end
them you would have to employ an inspector on
every individual ricketty craft that every am-
bitious Jap or Manilla-man is able to hire for a
month to risk his life from.

stantinople.”— Brassey. Not if she can’t help it.
Mr. John Reay Watson, of Gympie, Q.,
will shortly publish, in London, a story which,
if the tale contributed by Mr. Watson to last
week’s Bulletin is a fair criterion, will be very
successful indeed.
A well-known Australian artist writing
from London: —
I believe this time of the year is termed the “ silly
season,” but it is in no way different from any other time
so far as I can see. What Ido notice is that the most
silly-looking people you see about always seem to have
plenty of money.
A Bulletin man writes from London :
Per last mail I forwarded you a Daily Mail containing
a sketch by Souter, taken from The Bulletin and intro-
duced as bearing on the question of “ Husbands on holi-
days—with wives or without T ’ A letter I wrote to the
Evening News aforesaid drawing attention to their mis-
appropriation of Bulletin matter was not acknowledged,
and I am afraid it is hopeless to expect to cure the Harms-
worths of plagiarism. They have always done it and
have made it pay handsomely. The Answers Publica-
tions, Ltd., according to the balance-sheet of July last,
netted £53,000 for the 12 months (by using free copy
other papers had paid for). A div. of 224 per cent, was
paid and £BOOO placed to reserve. (Brain’s will never be
wealthy; it’s the exploiter of brains who takes the swag.)
Most of the London papers have a standing grievance
against the Harmsworths, who regularly “ lift ” special
articles, also “ exclusive information,” and insert same in
their dailies, thus filling those dailies on consistent and
economical lines.—
He who prigs and makes it pay
Will ne’er stop prigging night or day.
There is great activity in the literary and journalistic
worlds here—new' monthlies, weeklies, dailies are being
published, or in preparation, and the stock of short-story
makers is rapidly increasing and producing literature of a
shallow fireworks type. Publishers say Australian tales
are a drug in the market, but T. Fisher Unwin thinks
otherwise. The “ bike ” craze has undoubtedly harmed
the book-trade, but the wheel is even now' on the wane
ow'ing to the number of ghastly accidents, and the develop-
ment of anaemia amongst immoderate girl riders (vide
British Medical).

The Bank I Knew.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There were three of them in the little up-country
bank. The manager was troubled with a perpe-
tual thirst, and didn’t care how he spent his
money or how things went in the office. The
teller (or accountant, as he liked to be called) was
a young married man with tastes matching £lOOO
per annum, instead of his poor little £l5O. The
junior was likewise a youngster who found that
his £BO a year hardly paid for his board and
collars. It certainly could not be stretched into
providing billiards and nap.—let alone touring
with football and cricketing teams.
The mischief all started through the manager.
He would look in on his journey from one pub. to
another and say to the teller “ Give us a couple of
notes, Bogson, and I’ll give you a cheque when I
come in.” Perhaps he would not come in again,
and the amount would be held over as “notes”
until next day, when very likely a similar per-
formance would take place. This would go on
until, when the manager did give a cheque, his
credit was not big enough to meet it. Then the
junior would inform him that his account would
be overdrawn if the cheque was paid, and : “ Oh,
tell Bogson to hold it over to the end of the
month! ” At the end of the month the cheque
would very likely swamp his salary, and so the
game would start again.
The teller also found it convenient to have a
small floating loan from the bank in this way, and
the junior was not above borrowing a fiver from
the teller, though he knew quite well where it
came from.
Of course these small loans did not appear in
the weekly overdraft-list. The amounts were too
small to bother headquarters with ; and, besides,
the money was just lying idle in the till. So the
cash-book was generally doctored to the extent of
£5O or £6o—a sum which the boss could at any
time get from Murphy, the publican; and, as
there was only one train at 6 p.m. every day,
there was not much chance of being caught nap-
ping if things were managed properly.
At the yearly inspection the old fossilised in-
spector certainly made a “point” at Murphy’s
cheque for £6O, held in the cash, but was appeased
on being informed that Murphy was a valued
client of the bank who often required change after
hours. Murphy, on his part, got his money back
the day the inspector left. He asked no questions,
as the boss was too good a customer. Besides, he
had been told it was to square a temporary over-
draft not authorised by headquarters. So things
went smoothly on—the floating loan gradually in-
creasing in size.
One day, however, a mining co. in which the
manager was deeply interested had to be wound
up, and he was told he was liable for a guarantee
of £5OO entered into to oblige a friend in another
town.
“Oh, Bogson ! ” he said, “I wish you would
remit this sum through the Disunited ” (the oppo-
sition pawn-shop). “ I’ll be getting the amount
up from Bungtown shortly; my brother there
owes me nearly £1000.”
Bogson knew this was a lie, but he sent the
money as directed. He was pretty bard-pushed
himself just then, and the manager <u6uld not say
anything if he also borrowed a hundred or so. The
junior was not supposed to know anything about
the matter, but he soon found it out and came in
for a share himself, as he had lost heavily at the
last races.
Bogson knew very well if an inspector should
come along there would be a short shrift for both
himself and the manager. The manager trusted
to Providence to right things, and drank more
whisky. The junior reckoned that, if there was
any mess, he would be out of it, as he was not
supposed to know anything about the cash.
Bogson was not altogether a fool, and tried hard
to find some means of covering the deficiency. At
last he hit upon a plan. There was a customer of
the bank called Mucker, a bachelor who lived by
himself on a little farmland who, to all outward
appearances, was as poor as—you are. This
Mucker had two fixed deposits with the bank—
£looo each —the receipts for which deposits he
was in the habit of endorsing and leaving with the
bank as “ bills for collection,” so that he should
not lose a day or two’s interest, as he would do if
he were not present on the day they fell due. In
this manner the receipts were renewed without
requiring his presence.
Next time these receipts were renewed, instead
of putting them to “ bills for collection,” Bogson
marked them off as paid in the register, stating in
the margin that “ money was required to purchase
land.” As the bank often let good customers
break their fixed-deposits before they became due
for a special purpose, this passed without note in
the next inspection. Of course, Bogson knew that
when the year came round again he would have to
pay Mucker’s interest, and also make out new
receipts. But a year is a long time :no one knows
what may happen in a year.
In this case the devil favored his own. Before
the year was up Mucker died suddenly. He left
no will, and his estate was wound up by the
Government official. The usual notice came to the
bank, asking if John Mucker, deceased, had any
moneys there. The reply was made that Mr.
Mucker had a couple of hundred at his credit on
open account. Nothing was said about the fixed
deposits, the boss remarking : “If they find any
memo, among the old fellow’s effects about those
receipts, we can simply say it was a mistake on
our part and hand over the money. If they don’t
find any, we will just stick to it.”
Nothing more was heard about the matter, and
old Mucker’s money liquidated the floating loans
and paid a handsome dividend. The moral is
unfortunately absent, for no retribution followed.
The manager died a few years ago after a bad turn
of d.t. The teller is now an inspector himself.
The junior is an inspector’s accountant. That’s
me.
WEKA.
At Calga station, Coonabarabran, N.S.W.,
4366 hogget ewes shore for 21 bales, weighing over
21 tons—a fraction short of 111 b. per sheep.
One of the pioneer prospectors of Kiandra
goldfield, John Hart, aged 81 years, was found
dead in the bush near his hut the other day,
having succumbed to exhaustion. The old man,
who in the early days of the ‘ ‘ Snowy ” was said
to be worth £lO,OOO, was at his death partially
dependent on his neighbors for food.
The Sydney Telegraph man who writes
the papers on “ The Disastrous Effects of Pro-
tection ” has done his second whoop. His
strong point this time was that Vic. and N.S.W.
factory statistics couldn’t be compared because—
The practice adopted by the Victorian Statist is to ex-
clude from his returns all establishments employing less
than four Europeans, or less than two Chinese, except
where steam or other mechanical power is used. (See
Parliamentary return, October 1, 1896.) The Statistical
Bureau of New South Wales, however, excludes all estab-
lishments employing less than six hands, regardless
whether they are Chinese or Europeans, and also with the
exception of those using steam or mechanical power. (See
“ Wealth and Progress,” 1894, p. 92.) It is admitted that
the Victorian establishments thus excluded over and above
those included in the New South Wales returns, i.e., all
those employing four and five Europeans, and from two
to five Chinese, employ more than 1000 operatives, and
this number, therefore, ought to be added to that shown
as employed in New South Wales factories.
This looks explicit enough, but on page 92 of
“ Wealth and Progress ” for 1894 there is no such
statement as that quoted ; in fact there is nothing
about factories at all, page 92 being a dissertation
on British shipping !
Dr. Knaggs’ paper to the Aus. Medical
Association on the causes of human fallibility
was highly interesting, and brought together
some curious illustrations of the way in which a
man’s life is taken out of his control by sub-con-
sciousness, or by the action of nerve centres in-
dependent of volition ; but the case made out for
continuous expert examination of public officials
was not strong enough to definitely silence ob-
jections. Tests and examinations for the
simpler abnormalities of the senses are easy
to devise, and are, to some extent, already in
operation, but the “automatic action” and the
“ imperative idea ” which bulk so largely as causes
of human error cannot in the nature of things be
measured except with the assistance of the subject
—and doubtfully then—and in the nature of things
the candidate for a public office is not going to
tell of such defects—if he is aware of them. What
form of expert examination could have hindered
the striking accident to which Dr. Knaggs refers
when a station-master who had rightly said “ Line
clear ” for many months said it wrongly and auto-
matically on one fatal day and wrecked two
trains ?
A Good Boy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.
“ ‘ Having heard the evidence before you, have
you anything to say in your defence ? You may
enter the witness-box and be sworn ; but if you do,
whatever you say will be taken down in writing,
and may be used against you, and if you make a
false statement you will be liable to be prosecuted
for wilful and corrupt perjury.’”
The prisoner glances at the card whereon are
printed the words that the court-orderly has just
recited, and, with a smile of scorn, he remarks
sotto voce that he “ain’t tykin’ any,” and waves
his hand contemptuously.
“Your worchips—won’t yer let me —can’t I
speak a word or two ? ”
It is a short, stout, motherly-looking old woman
in a faded black bonnet and with her face stained
with the tears welling so recently from her red-
rimmed eyes.
Her identity is not hard to guess as she steps
forward to the side of the attorneys’ table and
sobs the broken sentence towards the bench.
“ Who is this woman? ” demands the chairman.
“ I’m ’is mother,” she volunteers, nodding her
head towards the young man whom the last five
witnesses had been abusing with such vigor, “ an’
if your worchips would be so kind, I’d like to say
a word or two.”
“Do you wish to give evidence on the prisoner’s
behalf ? ”
“Yes, your worchip.”
“ Then get into the box and be sworn.”
With trembling limbs and with fingers ner-
vously clutching the rail of the stair, the little old
woman obeys.
“Take the Bible in your right hand,” orders
the C.P.S. ; “ your right hand—not your left.
The evidence you shall give in this case —don’t
kiss it now —shall be the truth, the whole truth,
and nothing but the truth, so help you God. Kiss
the book.”
“Now, what do you wish to say?” asks the
presiding P.M.
“ He’s a good boy, your worchip, an’ I’d ask yer
to treat him lenient. I never had a day’s trouble
with him in me life, an’ he’s looked after me and
the childer, and worked for us ever since me
’usban’ died, this twelve year. He’s a real good
boy, your worchip. P’r’aps a little wild when he
has a drink or two, but I never had the least cause
of complaining about him. He’s a hard-working
young feller, as any of the neighbors’ll tell yer,
an’ if all mothers had boys like ’im they’d be
better off. Let ’im off this time, your worchip,
woncher ? ’E’s a good boy.”
The poor old woman’s appeal is not thrown
away. The reporters pause in their exchange of
anecdotes to listen, and even the solicitors,
grudging every minute that keeps them from the
case they are waiting for, manifest an interest in
the plaint. The prisoner has lost his air of
bravado, and his lip trembles as he looks at his
poor old mother in the box. Possibly he’s not
such a bad sort of fellow, after all. Those five
witnesses were certainly dead against him, but
all of them were policemen and it was one of
themselves that he was fighting when they came
up. They stick to one another like glue, these
constables, and possibly the one he hit thoroughly
deserved his thrashing. Some of these officious
police would try the temper of any high-spirited
young fellow to its bursting point, and this one,
being a powerfully-built man, was a trifle too good
for the representative of the law. The sympathy
of the spectators is as manifestly with the accused
now as it was with the police at the beginning, and
theßenchseemsdistinctly favorable. The only man
in court whose position is difficult to define is the
orderly. He fingers a huge sheet of paper the
whole time, and never shifts his eye from the chair-
man on the bench. He moves a pace or two in
front of his desk and then drops a pace or two
back, and, as he smacks his lips, he is reminiscent
of nothing so much as a hungry caged tiger lick-
ing its chops in anticipation of the fine fat dogs it
sees outside.
Suddenly the chairman speaks: “Anything
known of him ? ”
“ He’s a good boy, your worchip,” remarks the
mother by way of a reminder.
Then the orderly lets out: “Nineteen times
drunk, seven times obscene language, once insult-
ing behavior, once armed with an offensive weapon,
five times assault, seven times assaulting the police,
twice discharged for assault and robbery, l and 12
months on 30th July last for housebreaking and
stealing. He’s just discharged from gaol after
doing that sentence, your worship. What d’ye
say to that, Gilligan ?” ,
The prisoner did not speak.
The bench did.
It said “Six months.”
St. Kilda.
MONTAGUE GROVER.
The Hard’s Confession.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ They learn in sorrow what they teach in song.”—
Shelley.
‘My Love is void of Vanity ” —a sonnet
Endowed my charmer with a dainty bonnet.
“My Heart is Full ” —a noble piece of verse
Paid Snip and Snob when empty was my purse.
Those vigorous verses, “What Care I for Gold ? ”
Brought guineas twain that backed “ The Baron
Bold.”
My noble ode on “ Water pure and pale ”
Supplied me several days with amber ale.
That doleful dirge, “ How soon we pass away ’
In vinous joys turned one dull night to day.
“ Engulf me, Grave —I fear not Death or Pain,
The doctor paid who made me well again.
We bards have each a noble-soaring soul,
Our bulging craniums butt against the Pole.
We’re cradled into Poetry by thirst,
We gain by warbling what we lose in Burst.
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.


HOW TWO BEAUTIFUL CHILDREN
Ware Rescued from Disease, and Restored to Health
and Happiness by Ayer’s Sarsaparilla.

Throughout the world Ayer’s Sarsaparilla is noted as a blood purifier
and a tonic and invigorate... Testimonials to its worth are to be numbered
by the thousands, and come from all the four quarters of the globe. One
of the latest is from Miss Mary Maguire, a well-known Philadelphia
business woman, who has two nephews, both, aged five years, Elliott
Maguire and Walter Kester. Out of gratitude for the cure of their
diseases by Ayer’s Sarsaparilla she sends us their portraits, which we
reproduce above, and writes :
“I can safely say that Ayer’s Sarsaparilla has saved those children much agony and
pei liaps theii lives. Since they were born, not one dollar in doctor’s fees has been spent upon
them, and the only medicine they have taken has been the Sarsaparilla. They first began on
it two years and a half ago. They hadn’t been strong, but were never exactly sick till then.
It was early in the spring and both children, who are cousins of almost the same age, fell ill
at the same time. Elliott’s trouble was chiefly in his stomach at first. He had violent indi-
gestion, and at such an early age that was something alarming. Then eczema made its
appearance. These unhealthy conditions were largely hereditary. The little boy’s skin
began to blotch up and eruptions made their appearance. With Walter it was different. His
arms and the upper part of his legs were badly swollen and the veins seemed to stand out like
pm pie cords. He also had stomach trouble, which was inherited, and it is pretty hard to get
medicine to cure inherited diseases. However, I had seen Ayer’s Sarsaparilla advertised to
cure such cases and I got a bottle of it and gave it to both hoys. The first bottle produced
a 1 e tillemarkable change. It was wonderful. Then I began a regular Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
ie.i.tment. I gave the medicine to them three times a day. The blood troubles disappeared
ami their skin cleared up splendidly. Then their stomachs got into condition again. Since
that time I have given it to them regularly every spring. They have got so used totaking it in
that season that when I miss a dose they say: ‘ Auntie, yon forgot our Ayer’s.’ They have
never had any other medicine whatever, and that alone has cured them and made them well,
strong and hardy. I consider the Sarsaparilla a wonderful medicine.”

“ My Demi Mb. BONNINGTON.
